Of Marcus Clarke (and Some
Others).
HE was nice. It is not a flattering term in
masculine association, but it fitted him like a
glove. And his niceness was as a velvet
cushion whereon to display the few facets—not
one of them is a perfect jewel—of his genius.
Nice as his surroundings he seemed when I met him,
in the year 1876. It was late January, and fiercely hot,
but within the National Gallery there was coolness and
rest, and in the room with the pleasant outlook off the
library just such surroundings as a literary man might
desire.
I had a bundle of manuscript, and no other intro-
duction whatever. Nevertheless the “lips of laughter"
moved pleasantly and the “eyes of light” shone in a
little while, and I got a glimpse of the most charming
personality that has so far graced the literary world of
Australia. He had only the old familiar counsel as to the
literary life. “If you want to prove that life is not
worth living, persist.”
It was discordant with himself as with his sur-
roundings. And when after a while the door was
suddenly opened, and with a lot of frou-frou and flutter,
and “you’re a very pretty fellow, are n’t you,” a tall and
stately lady faced him, I was not a bit more convinced.
(That lady, I learned later, was not remotely connected
with a novel in which a Dr. de la Morte figured largely.)
“ Come up this evening,” he said, “ and we will dine
together at the club.”
j*
The club was the Birdcage, in all its glory—
Butters’ birdcage, just opposite the Argus office. Don’t
look for it now. It seemed a place of glory from
the threshold inward that night. Butters was then
in his prime, fresh from his Premiership in Fiji, a Rob
Roy in modern costume—the Butters costume, which
has never changed. And round about, in the vestibule,
a score of men, who talked of big affairs in city life. The
waiters, who moved with noiseless grace, were full-
blooded Fijians, and the whole interior was a glory of
palms and ferns.
Clarke was very quiet, but persistent. He got exactly
what he wanted and without any fuss. When we had
found our seats in the dining hall it seemed that, without
any preconcerted arrangement, Dr. P. Moloney and W.
Sauriu Lyster were right opposite, and Fitzgibbon, the
Fitz. of Tom Carrington’s cartoons, was but half-a-dozen
chairs away, and two or three other men, who had read
all the magazines and remembered strangely well, near
about. v .
The doctor talked most, I think, and the subject was
the book and the lady I have mentioned. Clarke listened
persistently. He was making up. his mind. Later
on, as if to change the subject, he said, “ I have been
looking up some nice novels and verses, Fanny and Mor-
timer Collins.” Later still, I knew to what end he had
looked them up.
That dinner beneath the palms and the ferns, with the
gorgeous service on the table, seemed a triumph. And
indeed it was no mean feast, for Butters knew the art of
dining fairly well. There was turtle, venison, and
every sort of Australian game. There were' mangoes
nursed all the way from the tree to the table, there was
pineapple marvellously served in great silver bowls,
there were figs which, like the cherries of Arnold’s
“Sick King in Bokhara,” had been steeped in drifts
of snow. There were ice cones along the 5 table
mantled in flowers. There was delicious coolness and
fragrance in the air, with well ordered luxury and
beauty all around. And he took it all as a natural part
of life, as one of the proprietors, so to speak. I had been
“ a-droving ” from the Warrego right down to Gippsland
and was in Melbourne for a week or two. And—“ if you
want to prove that life is not worth living, persist.”
Lyster cut the dinner short. “I’ve got the Governor
to-night,” said he : “ must go.”
Clarke said we would go too. And so we turned
the Town Hall corner and into Bourke-street and halted
for a moment with the crowd. Lyster was ahead. We saw
him -marshalling a half-dozen flunkeys with a great-
crimson carpet. As the outsiders clattered up they
rolled it to the kerb. Down then came Lyster to the
carriage door, .handing out my lady, and greeting
old Sir George Bowen be-ribboned and starred. (No-
body has done that thing quite in the same style
since Lyster’s day.) We followed them up the carpet
as of right, and my companion’s name was on many
lips in the mob below. The manager’s box was at our
disposal, and when the show began (it was only panto-
mime and not very fine), there was evidently a very
fascinating entente cordiale between that box and the
stage, all the approaches coming from thither. He
responded indifferently and cut suddenly across all my
unprofitable nmsings with “ Don’t you think Lyster a
singularly handsome man?”
I had 'not thought seriously about it. “He seems to
me the exact image of an old Assyrian king." I realised
it instantly then and have never forgotten it. You may
see the head of W. S. Lyster on many of the rock fragments
recovered from Babylon and Nineveh, and the artist
who would have painted Belshazzar in his pride might
have taken him tor a model.
“ I have to put in a little time at my office,” said
he about nine, “ will you wait here or come into the
vestibule?” The lonely box menaced irksomeness or
worse. We went out and encountered a lame man with
a rubicund visage and a wicked eye, whom Clarke called
Thersites, and othergnen “ Jos,” and that lame man said
many things pleasant to hear and very fit for vivd voce
publication. But he became cross when a tall and thin
old man climbed the stair and fastened on to us garrulous.
“ Who is it?”
“ Old Dick Birnie, essayist of The Australasian."
Great God, I had thought of a Browning’s bishop, a
Blougram, sitting in that chair. He was the first dis-
cordant or convincing note. “If you want to prove that
life is not worth living, persist.” That essayist had
always been associated in my mind with epicurean sur-
roundings, Erasmus-like erudition, and all the splendour
of unclouded intellect. I will not seek to pourtray him ;
he has gone to his rest long since.
He smote one flash out of “ Jos,” who tried from the
outset to talk down his tedious pedantic mumbling—
“Jos! Jos! You have caecethes loquendi.” “I have not!
I’ve got fever and measles and cancer, and leprosy, and
paralysis ; and other things if you like. But I have n’t got
that.”
“ There is time for another look in at the club,” said
Clarke, returning. And then to the palms and the ferns
and the splendour we returned, the lame man accom-
panying. But the way this time was upstai-rs to a room
tolerably bare save.of what seemed most desirable human
furnishings. The smoke was heavy, the liquor earnest,
the beards wagged and the broad, smooth faces shone.
Suddenly up rose Butters in the midst of all and shouted
“Ronald Macdonald.” Why did not all that broadcloth
and fine linen fall away from him, and kilts, tartan,
sporran, and blackcock’s feathers materialise in their
stead? Never was there such a Rob Roy Macgregor.
To the call we formed in a ring and “ Hey for
Ronald Macdonald ! ” was sung with much zest and glory.
That was an acknowledged good night at that club.
There were others later ; but we went quietly down to the
old Port Philip Club, and after ten minutes such talk aa
was there in Spooner's time, he took the last train to
Brighton. I returned to town repeating once again, “ If
you want to prove that life is not worth living, persist 1"
What?
Later in the week Clarke said, “ Would you like a day
or two at Lyster’s place in the bush ? ” Thus came it
that we arrived at that pleasant home Lyster had
made for himself a couple of miles east of Fern Tree
OulW- There in occasional Inurs, when our host had told
his yarn of life, that is very fit to put into a book ; and
when the Sweet Singer had gone down to the mulberries,
or to look for imaginary trout in the creek, Clark®
would talk at large and lament, querulous and unreason-
able as it seemed, disclosing another sort of being not
much to be envied. We were right on the edge of the
Big Bush. A half-hour’s walk would take one into the
true Amygdalina forest, one of the wonders of the
world. I do not think Clarke on any occasion took that
walk willingly. I know he had not the faintest per-
ception of the character, the sentiment, the glory of
the forest. I often contrast him in memory with
John Ford Patterson, artist, who also lived a good deal
about the Big Bush, and who used to say with bated
breath almost : “ It is well for ye if ye can catch a
little bit of the idea of the like of that."
Clarke said, “ If you get right on top of the range, and
it is a beastly walk, you will see the Yarra Plats on the
other side. Castella’s place is just out of the view. I
must introduce you to Bleasdale. He will take you out to
Castella’s. They make rare wine and give you a jolly
good dinner as well.” Similarly when talk turned to
outside country (surely he who wrote the introduction to
Gordon’s poems must understand outside) the sense of
draw ing a blank was painful. He hated the Bush and all
its discomforts too thoroughly to understand a bit of it.
The Wimmera was “ nice country enough, Mount Zero;
and the rest! ” But what of that rampart-like front of
the Grampians, with its wing of low hills skirting Aus-
tralia Felix and reaching right round to the coast of
Mount Aurepiles, lone and strange out there on the
misty plain ? Of Wimmera and Yarrambiak, meandering
away inland, repeating, in miniature, all the mysterious
inland river system of the Continent? Of the glassy
sheets of water, and the innumerable wild fowl rising
with thunderous beat of wings in the night hours ? Of
the strange, mysterious swamps, reed covered, bittera-
and curlew-haunted, about the mountain’s feet? Of
the gigantic oaks and gums on the sand ridges,
finest in all Australia, I think, in those days?
Of the webs of their foliage against the
evening and the morning sky ? He had never seen them.
He did not know them. He had not got beyond “ Mount
Zero and the rest; very nice; yes.” He would rather
have one smell of the fleshpots than the heritage of deso-
lation whole and undivided.
J*
I write this without any intent of depreciation
and in full knowledge of what certain very distinguished
people have said on the other side. “ He acquired know-
ledge,” say Messrs. Sutherland and Turner, in that fool
book of theirs, The Development of Australian Litera-
ture, “ that impressed the seal of truthfulness on his
description of the Australian bush to an extent unequalled
by any other author who has essayed the theme.” And
this, in 1892, when some others who did know had
essayed.
Clarke would not talk of his own work. I do not think
he had pleasure in writing one single line unless that line
was a lash and the object of his aversion well within reach.
He did a good deal of that sort of thing, and out of a
whole heart in his later days. Of books he talked
curiously. The larger lights of the world, even as the
wider horizon of Australia, seemed in some mysterious
way beyond his ken. He would talk Theodore Hook,
Leigh Hunt, Praed, Clough, Pater, Hamerton; but
Browning was “ a weariness to body and soul,” and, if one
spoke of Spenser as a gold mine, he retorted, “Dig him,
then, if you will; I myself prefer the minted coin.’
A very little intercourse served to clinch the conviction
that he held everything save the joy of life in utter
contempt. If a true pathos seized and compelled him at
times as in “ Holiday Peak ” and “ Pretty Dick,” he held
it as a hateful obsession. Of his great book he said, “ It
is a chamber of real horrors indifferently assorted, and
confound it! it does n’t draw.”
I was equally delighted and disillusioned on that trip.
Never was there a merrier, kinder, less exigent com-
panion. Take him as such, as those who knew and loved
him best did, and all was very well. But regard him
as the bulk whence samples had been given to the
world in all his fragmentary work, and the disappoint-
ment was keen. He regarded, or affected to regard,
those fragments as shed scales of a foul disease. As
is very well known, he took this view seriously in his
writings. Whether there was any hidden regard for
them I had no opportunity of discovering. It seems im-
possible that such fairy children as “ Holiday Peak ” and
“Pretty Dick” should not have a permanent abiding-
place in some close-walled chamber of their creator’s
mind.
Getting back to town unless at the close of a roystering
day is usually a serious affair, and I have always tried to
shunt the memories of that morning drive. When Lyster
spoke of London and the prospects of migrating thither,
Clarke answered, “ Ask the Lord and his prophets ! ”
When the Sweet Singer trilled a bit in response to a
magpie who sang divinely on a dead bough by the Big
Hill, he said, “That bird is typical of everything Aus-
tralian. There is something in him, but it can’t get out.”
je
From the outermost Mallee country of Victoria
and out in wider lands between Lachlan and Darling
rivers, I waited for Marcus Clarke through five years.
Not with much hope of any great things. He would
never write a novel: that was abundantly plain. So far as
Australia was concerned, he had no power of inter-
pretation in him. But he had the key of fairy
land, and also that ' knack of Saturday Review
reviewing which shakes up city peoples. But little
came. One letter I remember. It told the only bit of
wholesome truth at the time when “all that
is respectable” in Melbourne was raging furiously about
“ Black Wednesday,” so called. It occurred to Clarke to
ask the ragers why they had not protested on the occa-
sion of other wholesale retrenchments ? If the sufferings
of the “ curled darlings,” who should have substantial
savings, and assuredly had many friends, were so poig-
nantly felt, “ what of the fettlers, railway porters, junior
clerks or labourers sacked by the hundred often enough?
And often enough, too, with the store account closed,
the bailiff shadowing the landlord, half-a-dozen children
on the floor, and a wife most unsesthetically near her con-
finement V”
His knife was into the old Conservative party through
all his later days. They were of no use to him and had
not much use for him, when troubles gathered, as they
did. Even the old Yorick Club in those days took
Judaism to its inner chambers and affected respectability,
and the “Cave of Adullam" was, after all, not even the
refuge Clarke would have chosen. Even the Maison
Doree was not always open, and the effects of frequent
chills became cumulative bitterness. But he never
graded down, never surrendered to dram or drug
or the sordid carelessness which has clothed the de-
clining years of so many of • our best and brightest
like flthy rags. Fresh, well-groomed, even debonair
to the "world to the last. A sad end it was to
a mad life prematurely closed. But not so
bad as to deserve the Sutherland-Turner epitaph;
“ Had Marcus Clarke possessed in a moderate degree
a sense of-the importance of prudential considera-
tions, his life would have been a happier one for himself
and for those he left.” Oh, commonest of commonplace!
and all in ignorance of the plain and glaring truth that
“a man can’t be two sorts of man.” What comes of the
attempt is very well instanced when a Henry Gyles Turner
essays to deal with a Marcus Clarke, or an Alexander
Sutherland with Henry Kendall.
Z.M.
THE RED PAGE.
The picture gives you Olaf Gulbransson s idea
of Eleonora Duse in the part of Hedda Gabler, in Ibsen s
play. Gulbransson’s work appears often in Simplxcxssv-
mut, from which this drawing is taken. Note the famous
“ beautiful hands ” of the actress, and their significance
of gesture.
The best humour comes unconsciously. It was
not “ Steele Rudd," but the prize essayist of the Austral
Association, Toowoomba (Q.) —writing on ‘ The Re-
sources of the Darling Downs ” arid commended for
“ literary merit"—who penned this sentence recently :
Ostrich Farming.— Give practical lessons to our
farmers. They are easily reared and managed ; a Sft.
fence will keep them securely. Their feed — lucerne,
corn, melons—all grow on the farm. The feather
return from a single bird is an eye-openar !
And it was not “Hop,” but Julian Ashton, who drew
the funniest illustrations one has seen for a long time.
Thev come In a booklet about Free Trade and Tariff
Reform, printed in England and published in Sydney ;
and they come in colours. You see'Edmund Barton
wooing, betrothing, ami betraying Australia in a way in
which no Barton was ever drawn before. Then Australia
dismisses him—and nobody interested in local art should
miss “ The Dismissal.” The original should be hung in
the “ Australian Court” of N.S.W. Art Gallery. There
is nothing more marvellous even there.
“ Mariquita ” and “ P.L.” answer that “ Audie-
mur’s” quotation is from Goethe—
Was du ererbt von deinem Vdtem hast
Erwirb es—vm es urirklich zu besitzen.
The text of both correspondents has been collated for
the foregoing: neither can supply a textual reference.
The origin accounts for the simplicity of the
idea—Teutonic simplicity is often simpletonish. The
thin partition between genius and madness is familiar:
that between wisdom and folly is as thin. Say that
Goethe was a thor who flung thunderbolts.

Wore a Torture
BELT.
SUFFERED TERRIBLE AGONY.
From wearing an Electric Belt
charged by the use of
SULPHURIC Tie in.
Mr. W. Folley, Alfred Woollen Mills,
Williamstown, Vic., writing to the Dr.
McLaughlin Electric Belt Co., tells how he
was scarred and burned by the use of a
Free Belt, which he had erroneously
been led to believe was as good as theirs.
He paid £4 for the Free Belt because
he had been convinced that by paying
this cash price he was obtaining a similar
appliance to Dr. McLaughlin’s Famous
rejuvenator which has gained World
Wide fame as a Curative Agent. It goes
to show how misleading are advertise-
ments which seem to offer something for
nothing.
Gentlemen—ln answer to your kind in-
quiries after my health, I beg to state that
I have been completely cured of the Scia-
tica that has troubled me for years. I
cannot thank you enough for your gentle-
manly treatment and fair dealing with your
patients, as I know from others that their
treatment has been the same as mine.
I may just as well let you know my ex-
perience in seeking a cure for my trouble
before I purchased one of your High Grade
Belts. I had been under doctors and re-
ceived scant relief; and also tried various
quacks, but it was all the same. I then
placed myself in the hands of a Specialist
(in Melbourne) guaranteed to cure Rheu-
matism and Sciatica, but received very
small benefit from the treatment. I then
had my attention drawn to Electricity.
Well, I thought, “ Never venture, never
win,” so I PROCURED first of all a Belt
for £1 and was very much disappointed
because I was informed that it was neces-
sary to take a long and exceedingly ex-
pensive course of drug treatment. I would
not do this and then I wrote for, and ob-
tained a so-called Free Belt, with a
most “ Wonderful Pedigree,” at a cost of
£4. I wore the Belt according to the in-
structions given faithfully for a period of
five weeks, and by that time I was Burnt
and Blistered so badly that I could not
wear it longer, and still no relief was
gained, although the Belt was guaranteed
to Cure Anything in Sciatica in One
Month. You bet I was very much dis-
couraged in regard to Electric Belts.
However, I was persuaded to come and
just see you, gentlemen. I then found
that I had been wearing a Torture Belt
and very Wrongly Applied, and quite
enough to give and patient a Horror of
so-called Electric Belts. After the con-
sultation I decided to place myself in your
hands. I knew my case was very diffi-
cult, as I was compelled to follow my em-
ployment, and standing at the vice was
irksome, and the complaint was a vei y
stubborn one. However, gentlemen, your
Belt, coupled with your advice, has made
me a grateful patient. I thank you kindly
for supplying me with a remedy which has
Completely Cured Me, and strongly recom-
mend it to all and sundry that I find are
suffering from the distressing malady-
called “ Sciatica.”
I have not been wearing the Belt for the
past fortnight, but have passed it over to
my wife, who has received great benefit
for nervous troubles.
I remain, your grateful patient,
W.FOLLEY
iENUINE BELTS give GENUINE RELIEF.
Send to THE DR. McLAUGHUN CO.,

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Discordancy.
There on the blackened, wet rocks it lies,
The tall cliffs beetling above it bold ;
Too tall the cliffs, too vast the sea
For a poor little body so worn and cold !
O, the heart full of pride she had
To choose the sea for her place of rest!
The sea that mirrors the stars of God
And hides the moon in its mighty breast.
Knew she no pool in a quiet nook
Where she might enter and cease to be ?
When a wayside pond would have served her end
What did she want with God’s great sea?
The pond would have rippled about her head
And locked her close as a lover locks
His best beloved; but see how the sea
Has cast her there on the wet, black rocks!
O, the pathos of little lives
That dream and dream and awake at last
To find how petty—how poor they are;
The world about them how cold and vast!
O, the folly of all their deeds,
Their nights of darkness and noons of grace,
The dreams they dream, and the deaths they die—
So strange, unlovely, and out of place !
N.s.w,
RODERIO QUINN.
Another Spring Song.
Spring shakes her curls,
Her eyes laugh glee,’*
Peeping through glades
At you and me.
Spring loves a Lover—
That was you.
Last Spring—she saw us.
Guess she knew.
Spring winks one eye.
Does she remember
That swift, sweet mo;.th
Of last September ?
Ah, Lady Spring,
Who looks askance,
And views grave brows
With troubled glance !
Gold-crowned and sweet
With dainty ear
Bent low, where streams
Run glad and clear ;
Where birds sing loud,
From bursting throat.
Sprtng cannot bear
To lose one note.
And so she weeps
And frets—at you.
The change she notes
From rose to rue.
She does not know,
Sweet Spring, blue-eyed ;
That we were wed
The month she died.
VIC.
HELEN JEROME.
Eve.
Eve of my heart, what pages
Of Love you wrote for me
In the old Eden ages
By the Forbidden Tree !
The snow-white blooms were o’er us,
Life’s river laved our feet,
And through the calms a chorus
Of birds rose, angel-sweet.
O’er wide fields, golden-crowned, there
The low, soft winds did run ;
The grapes of God were drowned theie
In wine-vats of the sun.
There, where the sunlight glistened
Upon your tawny hair,
Who cared what serpent listened
In his unhallowed lair?—
When regal hours, attended
With all untold delights,
Passed in procession splendid
Of Everlasting lights.
Till one wild angel found us,
With a red, angry sword,
And called the Furies round us—
The vampires of the Lord.
And now the light that swims on
The river foams in blood,
And all the sunset’s crimson
Broods sullen o’er the flood.
Without the walls the thunder
Of jealous gods that grieve
Taught us ; and yet what wonder
They mourn thy wisdom, Eve?
M.L.
D. M. R.
Dreams.
You are dreaming the dream of your life to-night,
With a glad content in each happy sigh,
A vision of love in a future bright,
Of unborn days, uncoined delight,
And I am dreaming of days gone by—
Of another time and another place—
A place where the world looked bright and new,
Of a girl I can see in your own sweet face,
Though her eyes were brown and yours are blue.
Ah, sad is the bud that outlives its fellow—
A blighted flower on a blighted tree—
Love comes not again in the autumn mellow,
When the leaf is faded, and sere, and yellow;
Your kisses can wake no warmth in me,
For we love but once in this world of ours—
One pure, true love, and ne er again,
And long ago. in the golden hours,
I loved my love, and loved in vain.
Vic.
J. McCLELLAND.

General Debilitation.
A CASE IN PYRMONT.
A Permanent Recuperation.
The Case of Mr. W. H. ATKINS.
(BT OUR REPORTER.)
What an easy matter it is for a benevo-
lent being to diffuse pleasure and gladness
around him. In order to do so it is only
necessary to consider what means are pos-
sessed to lighten the burdens of our fellow-
men, and to help in whatever direction the
conscience may dictate. It is evident that
Mr. William Henry Atkins, of No. 211
Crown-road, Pyrmont, shares in this
opinion, for he said in reply to a question
put by our reporter :
"Of course, I will give you any informa-
tion you require, as the knowledge I gained
may be turned to good account by some
poor sufferer in need of good advice. It
has always been my motto to help those in
distress, and I think I can do so by letting
humanity know about the remarkable ex-
perience I had. I think that worry was at
the back of all my troubles, for after an in-
cident whicfckgrieved me greatly, I fell into
bad health J did not care about anything
to eat, simply because I had no appetite.
The most tasty morsels imaginable failed to
tempt me as a rule, although I had to force
myself to take some nourishment when I
fe.t, that I could not hold out any longer.
Not only was my appetite bad, but I ab-
stained from eating because of the pains
which afterwards appeared. My stomach
always got full of wind, which shifted about
from side to side, and left cramping pains
in all directions, and heavy pains affected
my chest. Sometimes I felt as if the
breath was being crushed out of me, the
oppr. ssion was so great, and I naturally felt
deeply concerned about it, as chest com-
plaints are so serious in their consequences.
As my food was not doing me any good I
lost strength rapidly, and soon I dropped
into that languid state of indifference to
everything which is so disastrous to every
business man’s career. I felt as if I did
not care how things went, and the morbid-
ness of my spirits quite precluded me from
thinking about anything pleasant. Haras-
sed as I was by depressing headaches, and
with my eyes dim and feeling as if they
were swollen, no wonder that I lost all in-
terest in life, for the average man does not
wish to ljtye unless he is contented and at
ease.”
"But your lot was not so hard to bear
when night came, for then, I suppose, you
obtained relief in sleep ? ” Remarked Mr.
Atkins’ interrogator.
"Don’t you believe it; if anything, my
nights were more intolerable than the days,
as I could not succeed in getting anything
like a night’s rest. Certainly, I used to
doze off from time to time, but the sleep
was not refreshing, because of the intrusion
of repulsive dreams, and when I got up I
felt as weary as if I had been awake all
night. I could hardly stand the bitter taste
which was in my mouth every morning, and
the unhealthy condition of my stomach
could be detected by the fur on my tongue;
but what I regretted more than all else was
the quick and painful throbbing of my heart.
When I laid down I felt that organ shaking
inside in a way that caused me much alarm,
so that, with every particle of strength
being gradually eaten away by my com-
plaints, there was nothing in my condition
to assist me in entertaining hopes of a re-
covery. The least exertion brought on a
feeling of faintness, and when I stood up I
used to get quite giddy, as if everything
was on the move. All this I put down to
my extreme weakness, which was also re-
sponsible for the annoying aches in my legs
and loins From some cause or other my
arms were also intensely painful, and if ever
a nervo«v system was wrecked it was mine.
On the least provocation I became excited
and irritable, while the trembling of my
nerves served to increase the general misery
of my existence. My body was greatly de-
nuded of flesh, and I can tell you in all
seriousness that my life was a burden which
I would Willingly have cast off at any
moment.”
“I believe you suffered in this way
several years,” interjected the scribe.
“ During the period of your illness did you
ever enlist the services of a medical man ? ”
“ Considering that I was lingering, wait-
ing, and hoping for years, I should rat her
think I did,” replied Mr. Atkins. “ Why,
I consulted dozens of doctors, yet my suffer-
ings grew worse, and I may also state that
I have taken scores of medicines which were
procured at stores and chemists’ shops. It
would have been far better if I had kept
the money in my pocket, for all but one of the
medicines were of no service to me. The
only remedy which suited my complaints
was Clements Tonic, and this was recom-
mended to me by a man living in Harris-
street, who had used it, and knew what it
could do. After taking two bottles of
Clements Tonic I felt much better, and was
able to get about with much more freedom.
I went to a chemist to get some more, but
he put me off by saying he could give me
something better, and pointed out the
difference in the size of the bottles, so,
foolishly enough, I bought his medicine in-
stead of Clements Tonic, and in a few days
I was as bad as ever. Then I insisted on
having Clements Tonic, and again I im-
proved under its influence, so that there was
no doubt about its being the best medicine
in the market. I would rather have one
dose of Clements Tonic than a barrelful of
any other medicine, for it proved its supe-
riority in every possible way. The first
thing it did was to make me eat and sleep,
and I was glad of the respite from my
afflictions which I had when resting. Be-
fore long a feeling of animation spread right
through my system, and I then shook off
the yoke of depression which had clouded
my past few years, as I felt assured that
Clements Tonic would pull me through. In
course of time the pains arising from indi-
gestion were quelled; the nauseous taste
left my mouth, and my head was free from
aches. Some of my doctors said my liver
was diseased, while others said my stomach
was extremely weak; but if they had seen
me after a few weeks’ use of Clements
Tonic they would not have known me. My
stomach could then digest and retain any
kind of food, and my liver symptoms were
non-existent. The shaking of my nerves
was promptly checked by Clements Tonic,
and the regulating and strengthening effects
of that medicine on my system generally
were really extraordinary. Every ache and
pain yielded to the pacifying influences of
Clements Tonic, and I am heartily thank-
ful to say that by its exclusive use my
health was re-established. I have lived in
this house for the past 17 years, and all the
neighbours know that Clements Tonic cured
me; but for the benefit of those who are
unaware of these facts you may publish
them in any form you like,”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, William Henry Atkins, of 211 Crown-road,
Pyrmont, in the State of New South Wales, Com-
monwealth of Austra.ia, do solemnly and
sincerely declare that I have carefully read
the annexed document, consisting of four
folios and consecutively numbered from one
to four, and that it contains and is a true and
faithful account of my illness and cure by Clements
Tonic, and also contains my full permission to-publish
in any way my statements—which I give volun-
tarily, without receiving any payment; and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the
same to be true, and by virtus of the provisions of
the Oaths Act, 1900.
Subscribed and deolared at Sydney, this
twentieth day of February, one thousand nine hun-
dred and two, before me,
JNO. a HALLAM, J.P.

THURSDAY, NOVEMBER 26, 1903.
Wanted-Just One Freetrader Who
Believes in His Own Belief.
That Federal Freetrade candidate, or leader, or
party, that is in favor of real Freetrade hasn’t yet
transpired, and any hope that he or it will
transpire may now, apparently, be abandoned
Seven days have elapsed since The Bulletin
last inquired for the Freetrader who believes
in his own belief, and during these seven
days many strange crimes and poems and
deadbeats have happened, but the Freetrade
candidate who is prepared to give Australia Free-
trade cometh not. In his complete absence Free-
trade arguments don’t require refutation, and the
importers’ lies should be treated with the cold
half-brick of contempt. When Bruce Smith
finds his faith too dirty for use, no one
need grieve much about all the bogus statis-
tics at the back of Bruce Smith’s creed.
There is no practical interest attached to
George Reid’s eloquent lectures on swimming if
‘George Reid sinks every time ; and there is no
joy in hearing Joe Cook hold forth brilliantly
about horsemanship when Joe Cook always walks.
No rational individual wants to hear Pump-
kin Smith and Edward Braddon preach fer-
vently about teetotalism if Smith has a bottle
sticking out of his pocket and Braddon has a
whole brewery. Not one of these men practised
Freetrade when he was in office, and not one of
them is prepared to undertake that he will prac-
tise it if he gets into office again—in fact, they all
promise that they won’t. When the alleged
devotee won’t even be found in the same paddock
with his own creed, what is there to be gained by
his holding forth in a paddock ten miles away
about the beauties and advantages of that creed ?
* • * *
The Bulletin is prepared to make the best
offer in its power to that real Freetrader, if he
will only transpire. First let the Freetrade
party, if there is one, find a leader whose word
can be relied upon— George Reid is barred, but
M ‘Millan or Bruce Smith or Edward Braddon
will be accepted despite the unspeakably bad
record of unmanly apostacy held by all three.
Then let that leader promise on his honor that if
he gets into power he will stand or fall by pure
Freetrade as it was until quite recently prac-
tised in England, the country which all these
public frauds and their press barrackers hold up
as the example to be followed-*-and The Bulletin
will support him cordially. It will subscribe
to his party’s funds. It will bring to
his aid, and to the aid of every reliable
Freetrade candidate who will swear the
same oath as he does, all the votes it can
command It will speak kindly and respectfully
of him as a sort of reformed criminal, and it will
be his friend—the only one he will have in the
Australian press. For the Freetrade S.M. Herald
and the Telegraph and the Argus and the
Courier will reach for the scalp of the Freetrader
who proposes real Freetrade ; and the Launceston
Examiner and the Sydney Mail will pursue him
with fury and an axe; and the Town and
Country Journal will sit like a vampire on the
head of his bed and gibber, and the Even Noose
will crawl up behind him like a Thug in the dark.
The Bulletin will support that species of Free-
trade leader for two reasons. First, it is practi-
cally certain that it will cost nothing, for the
votes The Bulletin brings to him will be the only
votes lie will get. Secondly, if, by any in-
credible chance, that real Freetrade that all
the alleged Freetraders say they want, but
don’t want, should arrive, though it will bring
great disaster on Australia, yet the disaster
will be short-lived and the ultimate results will be
most beneficial. After that short-lived experi-
ment there will be no more Freetrade papers or
parties or leaders in Australia for a generation.
George Reid and Edward Pulsford, even if
the mob "hasn't hanged them on a lamp-post, will
have as much chance of getting a seat in the
Commonwealth Parliament as the late lamented
Judas Iscariot; and Pumpkin Smith will have
fled from the country in disguise; and Joe Cook
will have escaped into the interior with two
vegetable baskets, and will seek to evade his
constituents by professing to be a Chinaman. It
only requires a year of genuine Freetrade, in all
its naked callousness and inhumanity, to end
the fiscal controversy in Australia till every-
body now alive is dead. And it is because every
Freetrader—every Cook and Smith and Reid—
knows this, that there isn’t one among the crowd
who ever attempted real Freetrade when he was
in power, or who will promise to try it if he gets
into power again.
* * * *
As to why Freetrade politicians advocate a
policy which, as their actions show clearly, they
aren’t brutal enough to put into effect, there are
many reasons. Most of the prominent Freetraders
are dull men, with a monkeyish capacity for imi-
tation, and a brown and sodden habit of mind.
They were filled witli Freetrade literature in their
youth, and are too much the creatures of routine
to get away from their book-lore and the things
they learned by rote. The humane instinct which
prevents them being so brutal as their theories
is something apart from the book-drivel they
picked up in childhood. They are in exactly the
same position as the Calvinist, who thinks he
believes in the damnation of infants and yet is the
kindest of men in practice. They are on the same
mental level as the devout missionary who is sure
it is infinitely better to be in Heaven, and who
fights 40 cannibals single-handed with an axe, and
kills 39 of them, to avoid getting there. They
are humorless men who can’t see what a shrieking
joke it is for a M'Millan to howl for Freetrade
and protect nearly every industry in N.S.W. ; or
for George Reid to state so conclusively that
Freetrade makes the people “jolly and fat,”
and then sneak in Protection for the sugar
industry because “ there was flesh and blood
behind it,” and lie didn’t want to reduce that
flesh and blood to the condition of a skeleton.
And only a gang that is utterly devoid of a sense
of the ridiculous could solemnly advance, as a
reason why it should be returned on the Freetrade
ticket, that it isn’t going to seriously injure or
reverse the nefarious and utterly accursed Protec-
tionist tariff—that tariff which makes its comical
little pot boil so furiously whenever it hears of the
thing,
* • • •
As The Bulletin has already shown, there is
nothing to prevent Reid and Pulsford and the
Smiths and Cook and Symon and the rest giving
Australia Freetrade, if they get into power,
save their consciences. Australia only raises at
present, in all forms of taxation (Federal, State,
and local) £3 16s. 6d. per inhabitant; Britain, in
national and local taxation, raises £4 7s. 6d. The
average income of the Britisher ranges, according
to the best statistical information, from £2O a
year per inhabitant in Ireland to £45 a year per
inhabitant in England ; in the Commonwealth
the average is £46 a year per inhabitant. There-
fore, unless it is held that Australia’s income will
disappear as soon as Freetrade happens along,
the Commonwealth is better fitted to try genuine
Freetrade than England is; it has less taxes to pay
and more income to pay with. That is saying
nothing of the fact that, according to Freetrade
authorities, genuine Freetrade would increase the
people’s incomes. Britain’s policy is to raise
four-fifths of its tax revenue by direct
taxation. The rest, until its recent slight
departure from Cobdenism, was raised by a very
few import duties which, when they applied to
things that could be locally produced, were
counter-balanced by equal excise duties so as to
put the local manufacturer absolutely on a level
with the foreigner. Britain doesn’t pamper
its farmers by specially low railway rates, or
coddle its Freetrade papers with free carriage. If
under Freetrade the incomes of the Australian
people would increase, as George Reid says they
would ; or keep fully up to the present level, as
Bruce Smith says they would ; or wouldn’t slump
horribly, as Edward Pulsford swears they
wouldn’t —then Australia is better fitted to try the
Freetrade experiment than Britain is. It could
abolish all customs and excise duties, except those
on spirits, and still levy no more direct taxation
per inhabitant than Britain does. The only reason
why there isn’t a real Freetrader in Australian
politics is that every one of them knows in his
inmost heart that, under the system he thinks he
wants, but knows he doesn’t want, the slump
would be something beyond words. So he crawls
out under cover of the complicated lie, that he
should be returned on the Freetrade ticket; that
anybody who doesn’t advocate Freetrade in
Australia is a fool; and that Freetrade is im-
possible in Australia because this country requires
revenue —just like every other country.
• • * *
All through Australia, whenever and wherever
a Freetrade candidate gets up to quote the fiscal
example of England, he should be asked the
uncompromising question: “If you get into
Parliament, will you vote for following the fiscal
example of England in its entirety?” If he
won’t, then the audience should leave and let the
subject drop. There is nothing more to be said.
No honest Freetrader has any use for such a candi-
date, and no Protectionist has any use for a Free-
trade candidate in any case. If he is prepared to
follow the noble British example then it is best to
find out that lie knows what that example is.
Britain has close on 400 different kinds of direct
taxation. The Englishman can hardly breathe
without being previously stamped, and the col-
lector batters at the door even of the humblest
three-roomed humpy with half-a-dozen different
taxpapers in his hand. Britain got rid of a mass of
import duties which troubled no one save the im-
porter, and got instead a huge, endless, inquisitorial
system of petty direct imposts-—a system which
puts its inquiring eye at the keyhole of every
citizen, and looks in at every citizen’s window,
and sniffs down every citizen’s chimney. The
Australian hasn’t the patience that the English-
man has to endure this sort of thing. Still, it is
almost a pity that the method shouldn’t be tried
for, say, half a year. The spectacle of George
Reid flying on two short legs for a foreign land,
with his warm admirers in pursuit, would be a
thing of some interest.
The Campaign for Protection in
Britain.
As the tariff agitation progresses in Britain it
resolves itself more and more into a Chamberla i
Balfour campaign for Protection, and more an
more the preferential-trade or Imperial side o
the question is pushed to the rear. I hot is t i
development which The Bulletin forecasted in
its first article on the Chamberlain programme
a use of “ the colonies as a pretext, by the help
of which John Bull ccyild pretend _ to conceal
from himself the fact that he had been worship-
ping false gods of fiscalism until he got a little
used to the idea of Protection, and then a plea,
straight-out, for a fiscal policy founded on sense
instead of superstition. The suggestion that
Britain needed to abandon that Freetrade which
was just as much a part of the traditional national
character as the morning cold bath and the Bible;
the confession that the Chosen People of these
modern times had been actually wrong in a
matter over which they had in the past assumed
such offensive airs of superiority, was an awkward
one to make, and had to be masked somehow at
first. The plea that Britain needed Protection,
not for its own sake but for the sake of its poor
little “colonies,’’ which would perish if Britain
didn’t come to its own aid with a tariff to protect
ts own interests, was an artful, if not exactly a
logical one. It took off the edge of the blow to
British vanity. It gave John Bull a dignified
means of retreat from a bad position. He could
get to shelter, not because he had to run away to
save himself, but because he had to bolt to save
his children. Now, however, that the pre-
text has served its purpose, it is being abandoned.
Sensibly and properly too. The “help the
colonies” call was very well for a “surprise
cry, but it would not stand examination. “The
colonies” already had Protective tariffs of their
own and wished for nothing better than to be
allowed to continue and improve them as they
wished. The benefits to come to “ the colonies ”
from inter-imperial tariff arrangements were not
such as could be indicated with any certainty, or
as could he negotiated for on any basis but that of
a preliminary British Protective tariff. That,
indeed, was the marrow of the matter. Preferential
trade with “the colonies” depended on a revo-
lutionary change in the British fiscal system;
necessarily that change was the issue, the sole
issue, and an issue of such vast intrinsic importance
to the British people that it must be considered on
its own merits. If Freetrade is good for Britain
it would be an utter absurdity for that coun-
try to embrace Protection for the sake of “the
colonies.” If Freetrade is bad, then the first work
is to sweep it away and then consider, as a
separate and distinct issue, how far Preferential-
ism can be of mutual advantage to Britain and the
rest of the empire. Chamberlain got the
Britisher over the gasping shock of the suggestion
that Freetrade was not part of the special Divine
Revelation to the modern Chosen People with his
“colonies” pretext very cleverly. Now his issue
is Protection versus Freetrade, and from all the
■signs he is going to win, within a very short time,
with Protection.
* * •
The cause ot Protection is helped enormously
by Chamberlain’s personal influence. As a
prophet he has the valuable characteristics to
commend him to a Chosen People that he is
intensely positive and practically predatory. He
cries out “Woe” convincingly; he points to
a Promised Land with an air of profound con-
viction ; and if anyone accuses him of being
an impractical visionary (which is the common
accusation against innovators)he can point proudly
to the golden provinces he stole in S’Africa as
proof of his practical British nature. But the
Protection cause suffers much, and will continue
to suffer much, from being championed by the
Government responsible for the War Office inepti-
tude and the new Education Act, which has
aroused the Nonconformists in Britain to resolute
hostility against the Balfour Ministry. Many
electors will dourly refuse to even examine into
the fiscal question because of their anger against
a Government which taxes them to support
schools controlled by a church which is infected
with “damnable episcopacy and Arminian-
ism,” or, worse still, by “the Scarlet
Woman.” Still, withal, the progress of the
Chamberlain campaign is astounding in its
success. Protection is everywhere acclaimed
with enthusiasm, and, significant sign, those
papers which depend most on the popular penny
or half-penny, are becoming converts to the cause.
When the Protectionist programme was first pro-
mulgated it had the support of the London Times,
the chief of the Conservative papers, and among
the new London half-penny papers it had a cham-
pion in the Daily Express. The Daily Mail,
Harmsworth’s organ, with its circulation coming
just to the million point, was at first against Pro-
tection. Now it finds that it cannot keep its cir-
culation and its Freetrade policy, and it was con-
verted by the Glasgow speech of Mr. Chamber-
lain. And, very significantly, the Daily Mail
suggests to Mr. Chamberlain that he should
drop altogether the Preference part of his
proposals and go for straight-out Protection.
The suggestion is probably an inspired one that
came from Chamberlain himself. It, at any rate,
stresses the present trend of his policy, which is
to attack the Cobdenites directly and ignore the
“ colonies ” subterfuge. The Newcastle Leader,
the most important English paper north of Bir-
mingham, lias also turned round, and now
supports Protection. There are hosts of minor
conversions. Evidently the public mind in
Britain was ripe for a change, and had uncon-
sciously come to a recognition of the hopelessness
of the Freetrade position before Chamberlain
spoke a word on the subject, but no one had the
courage to utter blasphemy against Cobden until
“Joe” arrived. Now, in spite of the handicap
of the Government’s misdeeds, he may win at the
coming general elections ; will certainly win at
the next,
* * * *
Further illustration of the new trend of Cham
berlain’s policy—to drop the preference notion,
and to go straight for Protection —is the fact that
he has abandoned an important part of his
Glasgow speech. At Glasgow on October 7 he
said :
We can say to our great colonies: “ We understand
your views and conditions. We do not think ourselves
superior to you ; we have taken the trouble to learn your
objections, to appreciate and sympathise with your
policy ; we know you are right in saying you will not
always be content to be what the Americans call a one-
horse country with a single industry, and no diversity of
employment. We understand, we can see, that you are
right not to neglect what Providence has given you in the
shape of mineral or other resources. We understand and
we appreciate the wisdom of your statesmen when they
say that they will not allow their country to be solely
dependent upon foreign supplies for the necessaries of
their life. We understand all that, and there-
fore we will not propose to you anything that is
unreasonable or contrary to this policy, which we
know is deep in your hearts. But we will say to you—
after all there are many things which you do not now
make, many things for which we have a great capacity of
production ; leave them to us, as you have left them
hitherto. Don’t increase your tariff walls against us.
Pull them down where they are unnecessary for the
success of this policy to which you are committed.
Do that because we are kinsmen, because it is good for
the Empire as a whole, and because we have taken the
first step, and have set you the example. We offer you
a preference. We rely on your patriotism, your affection,
that we shall not be the losers thereby'.”
Mr. Chamberlain does not propose now to say
“to our great colonies,” mild as is the sug-
gestion therein contained, that the colonies should
to some extent limit their aspirations after new
industries by regard for Britain s export trade. He
now abandons it, recognising that it is not likely
to meet with support abroad, and that it is, in
the present stage of negotiations, visionary and
impracticable. All things, in short, point to the
conclusion that Chamberlain is preparing to
throw overboard all the preference part of his
programme, and confine his proposals to the
simple issue of Protection against Freetrade.
And, now that “the colonies” pretext has served
its purpose, and Britain can discuss the fiscal
issue without going into hysterics at the mention
of Protection, that is the straightforward and
sensible course.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Importer Borrows the Deity.
The N.S.W. “ Protestant Defence ” jim-jam is
hard at work selecting candidates to stand for the
Australian Parliament in the name of William
of Orange, a defunct person of the Boer descrip-
tion who never got within many thousand miles
of Australia. And the fact that all its candidates
are violent Freetraders presents to the country
the weird spectacle of God’s name being taken
in vain in the interests of sixpenny worth of calico,
produced, probably, by an Asiatic heathen. The
importer is a person whose interest it is to
destroy Australian industries (run almost wholly
by Protestants) so as to make more room for im-
ports ; to deprive the mostly-Protestant Aus-
tralian workman of his work, his wages, and his
means of supporting his wife and family, so as to
make room for imports from Catholic France and
Austria and South America; and from Greats
Britain and the United States, which are about as
Roman Catholic as Australia ; and from Germany,
which is twice as Roman Catholic as Australia.
Apart from the league’s frantic desire to let the
goods of foreign Catholics in free so as to destroy
the factories where the Protestant Australian work-
man gets his living, the Yellow Pup movement
exists to help the silly, vulgarly-commonplace
Wilks into Parliament under false pretences, and
to float other nonentities into Australian politics
on the strength of a lie, and to supply Dill
Macky and others with a hoodlum species of
notoriety. The Roman Catholics are a diminish-
ing force in Australia, and form a much smaller
percentage of the population than they did 30
years ago. The alleged R.C. revival is, in the
light of figures, a lie. There is no pretence that
Wilks and his clan, if they get into Parliament,
will either kill off, or convert, or expel any
Catholics. Theydon’tpromiseto shut Catholicsout
of Parliament, or out of the Defence Force, or the
Public Service, or to confiscate their property, or
to do anything tangible. It isn’t shown that
Protestantism is a whit more advanced through
Wilks and his kind having been three
years in Parliament already; in fact, it
isn’t on record that Wilks has cursed the Pope
once in the House. Apparently nobody will
gain any advantage by the election of Wilks on
the Pup ticket, except Wilks, who borrows the
name of religion to help him into a position
which has £4OO a year attached to it; and the
importers, who take the loan of the Deity to help
them to crush Australian industry. At present,
the Commonwealth imports nearly <£15,000,000
worth of goods annually from Roman Catholic
countries or from countries which are much more
Roman Catholic than Australia is. Its purchases
from Britain have long been declining while those
from strongly R.C. countries have been increasing;
and the Protestant importers of Australia invoke
the names of the Lord and Kruger’s countryman,
Dutch William, to bring about a reduction of
import duties so that we may buy more from R.C
countries and leave our own Protestant artisan
out in the cold. Truly the name of Pro
testantism covers many faked invoices, and helps
many awful Wilks people into billets where they
can loaf on the community, and keeps many
clerical hoodlums in a good state of advertisement.
And when an aged and respectable Protestant
applies for his old age pension, most of the Tory
importers, who run the Protestant ticket so
furiously in the interests of the goods they buy
from the French and Belgian Catholic, would
refuse it if they could, and would much rather put
that aged and respectable Protestant in gaol for
vagrancy. And, as for giving the Australian
Protestant a job at making iron-work when a
Belgian R.C. will do it 2d. cheaper, they would
sooner see the blessed bones of Kruger’s relative,
Dutch William, thrown into the Yarra.

The National Black-and-Tan Australian
Party.
A curious animal, which is branded “The
National Liberal Union of Queensland,” has
begun to wander over the face of that afflicted
State. It is spreading itself across large areas of
the advertising space of the newspapers and on
numerous hoardings. Candidates for the Com-
monwealth Parliament are restricted by law as
to their expenditure ; and apparently this curious
animal is, in the first place, helping certain candi
dates to dodge and defeat that law. The animal,
that is to say, spends a great many pounds in ad vo
eating certain alleged principles; then the can-
didates, with the restriction upon them, spend
the few pounds which the law allows in
merely pointing out that the alleged principles
of the “National Liberal” animal are theirs.
Still, they might almost be forgiven this trans-
parent dodge to defeat the law of the land if their
alleged principles did not so shockingly travesty
the name which the beast has taken. The first
and, as it declares, the principal plank in its plat-
form is “The Promotion of a Sound Patriotic
Sentiment ”; and that seems good enough “ This
means,” it goes on to explain, “the cultivation of
that deep feeling of national pride and patriotism
which is the marked characteristic of Great Britain
and Ireland ; of the Germans, and of other nations
of Europe. It is our desire to cultivate among
young Australians a love for the land of their
birth” —and that is good, too. But then, haying
pointed out how fine a thing it is for Australians
to be patriotic, and to take a national pride in
their country, this curious “National Liberal”bird
demands that Australians shall open their doors
wide to “desirable” immigration. It isnotgame to
say straight out that it believes black and yellow
labor to be desirable; but, as it declares itself
dead against the political party which has opposed
black and colored labor, the omission doesn't
matter‘much. This highly patriotic “national
Liberal” reptile represents the black-and-tan
labor party without having the pluck to say so.


Of course the “Six Hatters” incident is dragged
in ; and its reference to the matter shows what a
contemptible fraud this bogus “ National Liberal ”
Union is. For it declares that it is in favor of
“ the immigration of desirable people of British
and other European races ”; yet because half-a-
dozen people were kept waiting tor a few hours,
through no fault whatever of tne Government, in
order to ascertain whether they were desirable or
not, and were admitted as soon as it was shown
that they were desirable, this bogus “National
Liberal” creature is amazed, and horrified, and
disgusted, and demands that the law should
be got rid of straight away. It doesn’t say how
it proposes to find out whether immigrants are
“ desirable ”or not; and the only presumption is
that it doesn’t propose to find out at all, but is in
favor of letting ’em all come. And it can’t be
too clearly stated that the country which is
prepared to let ’em all come isn’t the country
which is going to get the desirable immigrant;
and it can’t be too clearly or too often stated
either, that there is not one word in the present
law which blocks the desirable immigrant. On the
contrary, since it takes some trouble to block the
undesirable immigrant, it is all in favor of the
desirable immigrant, for no immigrant of the
latter class wants to come to a dirty, leprous
country. That is the law, then, which this
“National Liberal” party is so desperately
opposed to; and the opposition shows what sort
of a party it is, and how much its patriotism and
the patriotism of its candidates amounts to.
*
N.S.W. Labor Party Goes Back on the
Referendum.
Some things in the way of disgrace and backslid-
ing from democratic ideals, which the degenerate
N.S.W. Labor party had not previously accom-
plished, were attended to in full during the recent
debate on the Referendum Bill in the Cornstalk
Bear-garden. The Bill wasn't much at the
beginning, but it was on the motion of a Labor
member, and with the support of the Labor party,
that it was made a great deal less. The proposal
to take a popular vote re the reduction of the House
of Riot represented the melancholy acknow-
ledgment of Premier See that he and Carruthers
couldn’t hang up their alleged cherished principle
much longer, and must really pretend to do some-
thing. No two men ever struggled harder
to avoid getting what they said they wanted
than See and Carruthers, but a time had
come when the thing they said they wanted
became so loud and insistent that it could hardly
be dodged. But the Referendum, as originally
proposed, was a fake and a farce, and it was
made a worse fake and a viler farce. It pur-
ported at first to let the people say, by a
popular vote, whether they wanted the nose-
pulling branch of the Legislature kept at its
present absurdly swollen figure of 125 mem-
bers, or whether they wanted it reduced to 100 or
90 or 80 ; but there was no provision for allowing
the people to say whether they wanted further
reduction in the form of abolition of the Upper
House, or whether they wanted the Assembly
reduced below 80 members. As regards See and
Carruthers there was nothing remarkable about
this arrangement; nobody suspects them of any
liberal or progressive ideas. But the Referendum
is one of the biggest gods in the Labor
party’s pantheon. That body’s political creed
is that the voice of the people, expressed
with the most absolute freedom, should prevail.
The idea of stifling the voice of the people is
supposed to be specially abhorrent to the Labor
party. Therefore, it is an amazing thing that a
Labor member should have arisen to propose that
the Referendum should be made still more in-
efficient than previously proposed, by providing
that the people should not be allowed to vote for
any reduction of the Assembly to less than 90
members. At this point the disgraceful See
Government voted enthusiastically for the mutila-
tion of its own Bill; the Carruthers party mostly
contrived to be absent; and the Labor party came
out openly as the anti-Referendum party, and the
party which wanted to stifle the popular voice so
far as it dared. No matter what arguments may be
adduced against the reduction of the Assembly,
the fact remains that the people have a right to
decide, and that whoever interferes with the abso-
lute freedom of their decision drags the democratic
principle of the Referendum in the dirt, and is a
traitor to the most sacred of democratic principles.
As matters stand, the person who wants to vote
for the simplification of the cumbrous legislative
machine by the abolition of the Upper House
won’t be able to speak; and the person who wants
the Assembly membership reduced to 80 or 70 or
60 will be stifled, and the Referendum will be a
mere mockery. So far as this Referendum
goes these people are disfranchised, and the
Labor party has helped materially to disfran-
chise them—for no visible reason save that, if
the people weren’t thus deprived of their rights,
a few Labor politicians might lose their seats
amid the general reduction. The Victorian House
of 48 Rich Landlords wouldn’t have done any-
thing much worse. It would have refused any
Referendum; the N.S.W. Labor party grants a
Referendum with the proviso that the people
mustn’t vote for anything that the N.S.W.
Labor party greatly objects _ to. And the
Victorian House of Landlords, in another sense,
wouldn’t have done anything so bad as the
N.S.W. Labor party has done. It never
professed to have any regard for the self-
governing rights of the people, so it doesn’t
drag its own god in the gutter. Amid all
these remarkable doings it is hardly worth
mentioning that the Bill doesn’t provide that the
reduction voted by the people will be carried into
effect, and that the Assembly has most carefully
reserved to itself the right to take no notice at all
of the popular vote if the vote is displeasing or
inconvenient to the Assembly. Only one member
(and he was not a follower of See nor a Labor
member) proposed that the House should bind
itself to accept the people’s decision —even the
small amount of decision that the people are
allowed to give. He was a' very lonelv member,
was Oppositionist Garland, and he was ruled out
of order.
Suggested Exclusion of One Red-Hatter.
Discussion in the Reid organs of the exclusion
of the Six Hatters—who were not excluded—
reminds The Bulletin that one red-hatter, Car-
dinal Moran, is due in the Commonwealth on Dec.
10th, and that it would be of very great advantage
if be could be excluded—until at least the Aus-
tralian elections are over. The present Aliens’
Immigration Restriction Act is a law with a long
arm, and that arm should be stretched out to
meet the case. The position is this : The cause
of calico and cheap labor—of cheat and Chow,
so to speak—has, as a last scoundrelly refuge,
taken to the dirty ditch of sectarianism, and
from that position wages the battle of foreign-
trade with the stinkpots of religious animosity.
So far its ‘ ‘ religious ” tactics have been met with
a dignified silence on the part of the attacked
church. Rev. Dill-Macky, and Bill Wilks, and
George Reid, and Bill Kelly, and Sid Law
have raged more or less furiously, and Orange
demonstrations have screeched throughout the
land with a brass band fore and a yellow
quarantine flag aft, and the Yellow Pap has
barked for the Dry Dog with all his might,
without eliciting a single squeal from
the Green Pig. That animal, in spite
of some frantic suggestions from its press
organ, has steadily refused to demonstrate, or
to yell, or to set a brass band going, or to wave
green flags against yellow ones. This common-
sensible attitude on its part has left to the Orange
fanatics all the odium of the sectarian strife.
Their frantic cries have doubtless attracted to
them some few rabbit-brained people, but they
have repelled many thousands of citizens who,
whilst they are Protestants, perhaps extreme
Protestants, have been disgusted with such folly
and wickedness. But if Cardinal Mokan arrives
before the general elections he is likely to change
all that. The Cardinal is a violently combustible
person who has developed his enthusiasm at the
expense of his sense. He is liable at any time to
get up on a platform in a red robe, and commit an
indiscretion to match its violent hue. The odds
are even, or perhaps a trifle “on,” that he will
get excited at Bill Wilks and start a Roman
Catholic Defence Association, or say something
generally insulting about Protestants, and thus
make the sordid row, which is now mostly confined
to the disreputable and silly sections of the
community, far more general and consequently
far more disastrous in its effects. In the best
interests of Australia, then, Premier Deakin
should take steps to have Cardinal Mokan
stopped at Fremantle and put to the test of
an examination in the Basque language, or asked
to prove that he is not a contract laborer coming
out here to take a steady job from Archbishop
Kelly, or accused of being an Asiatic and
undesirable person. This might be done with as
much innocence of the Cardinal’s real identity as
Mr. Deakin chose to assume, and after Dec. 16th
the Cardinal might be allowed to land with ample
apologies for delay. This course would be
unusual, and probably lacks Australian pre-
cedent, but its public utility would justify its
unconventional character.
Braddon’s Doormat, regarded as a
Brother.
Old John Murray, one of the Queensland
National Ass candidates for the Senate, was
recently drivelling at great length, in Brisbane
Centennial Hall, about the iniquity of keeping
out niggers and the indubitable “ rights ” of every
British subject, when he was asked suddenly and
violently if every British subject had a right to a
vote. Old John nearly died on the spot, but,
when he recovered from his partial insensibility,
he explained. “ Why,” he cried, wildly, “seven-
eighths of them’s Indians ! ” Which exactly sums
up the case. The political rights of a British
subject, in a large, free, theoretical sense, are
these four:
(1) Local self-government.
(2) An equal share with every other British subject in
the control and government of the empire.
(3) No taxation without representation.
(4) A right to travel in and reside in any part of the
empire on the same terms as any other British subject.
These four propositions about sum up the case in
a strictly political sense. But to grant rights Nos.
1,2, and 3 would be bad for the pomp, dignity,
and supremacy of Britain, so as regards all the
great nigger community which forms something
like nine-tenths of the empire, Britain kicks those
three political rights over the wall, and knocks
those three great, precious, noble principles into
the cocked hat of derision. And not one loyal
Australian groveller of the John Murray and
Bruce Smith and Edward Braddon and G.
Reid stamp has a word to say against this outrage
—or rather those three outrages—on our beloved
fellow-subject. But the fourth right—that of
free locomotion through the empire and free
residence in all parts of the empire—doesn’t
affect Britain. No great hordes of niggers
want to go to England. The climate is too cold ;
the place is too crowded ; and the distance is too
great. The assertion of the sacred rights of the
British nigger and the British Chinaman in
respect of No. 4 would only bring ruin on Aus-
tralia and a few other places, and the Britisher, in
his brute selfishness, doesn’t care about them in
the least. So we find the British Government,
which has trodden remorselessly on every one of
the nigger’s political rights the assertion of which
could possibly damage Britain, almost weeping
with grief when it is proposed, at this end of the
world, to interfere with his one alleged political
right which could be asserted without injury to
Britain. Britain has decided, without turning
a hair, that the nigger can’t have the British
subject’s rights of local government, of Par-
liamentary representation, of having a share
in the government of the empire of which he forms
nearly 90 per cent. But Britain, to make up for
the other injuries, is extra anxious about the
nigger’s political claim which would only damage
“the colonies,” and as a consequence Australia
can only exclude the black and yellow myriads by
stealth with an Education Test. Britain has un-
blushingly stolen all control of their own country
from 300; 000,000 people of India, and yet is
shocked at our wickedness in not letting them
overrun a country which is not theirs. It has
declared them inferior beings, utterly unfit to
enjoy any of the political privileges of English-
men, and is broken up when we also regard them
as inferior. And the Australian Tory professes to
be broken up also. Even a Tory like Bbaddon,
who spent some 30 years in India, politically
kicking the nigger, showing him that he was an
inferior being, demonstrating to him that he was
unfit to enjoy any of the political privileges of a
British subject, and generally wiping his boots on
him in British interests, is outraged in his finer
feelings because we won’t treat the same nigger
as an equal and acknowledge his “rights” as a
British subject to pervade the Empire as he
darned well pleases. The idea that Australia
doesn’t want Braddon’s human door-mat as a
brother is quite beyond Beaddon’s comprehen-
sion. And Australia suffers from a dreadful lot
of Bbaddons.

To the People of Tasmania.
The Tasmanian Upper House, which has all this
session been throwing the Government measures
down the stairs, got its second wind the other day,
and rejected Premier Peopsting’s Civil Service
Reform Bill by 11 votes to 3. Then it threw out
the Occupiers Tax Bill by 11 votes to 2, because the
measure exempted the occupiers of houses worth
less than 6s. a week rental; and it rejected the
Land Tax Bill because of the tax on absentees.
In American phrase Premier Peopsting is “up
against it.” His financial and reform programme
has been thrown out bodily by the overbearing
House of Landlords. The Landlords say flatly
that they care nothing for the overwhelming
majority that returned Peopsting, for the
Assembly, for the people, for the popular
mandate. No Australian Premier in the
last 25 years has been so completely “up
against it ” as Peopsting is. He must either be
the most complete failure of the last quarter-
century, or he must fight the Upper House tooth
and nail. He must take off his gloves and prepare
to break things. Nothing short of the abolition
of the Council, or such complete reform of that
body as will make it a mere duplicate of the
Assembly, will meet the case, and that can
only be done by intimating that the Assembly
will vote no money till the Council consents
to its own abolition, or by some equally
radical proceeding. If the Assembly refuses
to stand by Peopsting in making a square,
manly fight with the bigoted old incapables,
then he should appeal to the country; if
the country refuses to stand by him then he can
resign and retire with honor. There is no other
honorable way out. Meanwhile every Tasmanian
should vote for only such Federal candidates as
are in favor of a Federal graduated land-tax and
Federal absentee-tax. The heavy ancients of the
Tasmanian Upper House have not yet realised
that there is a power above them to be reckoned
with, and every live Tasmanian should do his
share to bring the fact home to them. “ State
Rights ” in Tasmania mean the right of the people
to have everything they desire rejected by a crowd
of ignorant old landlords; Federal Rights mean
the transfer of power to a Parliament both Houses
of which are amenable to the people. The fewer
State Rights Tasmania has the more rights it will
have.
»
Victorian Laborites and the Elections.
A pbetty good Democrat writes from Melbourne:
The Labor party urges women to vote its ticket
because it “ has always advocated strongly and worked
for the extension of the franchise to women.” This is
quite true. Yet Vida Goldstein, the Victorian lady
candidate for the Senate, is not on the Victorian Labor
ticket, though she is a pronounced radical with socialistic
ideas. Now, the movement for women’s emancipation
and the Labor cause have sprung from the same seed in
the same soil. The one is the complement of the other;
and it is the natural consequence of extending the vote
to women that, if they can get elected, they should be
allowed to sit in Parliament. But the Labor party has, I
think most unwisely, ignored Miss Goldstein’s candida-
ture. The party is throwing away a magnificent chance
of gathering together almost the whole of the women’s
vote under the Labor banner. This refusal to seize a
golden opportunity is one of the biggest blunders that
can be committed. No arhount of good intentions can
make up for it. Certainly Miss Goldstein did not
submit to the Labor plebiscite for Senate candi-
dates ; but should that really make any very
serious difference ? Not only in this particular instance
of Miss Goldstein is the Political Labor Council running
the risk of over-reaching itself, but there is also the case
of the breach with Hume Cook, the retiring member for
Bourke. Hume Cook has been branded as a heretic by
the Labor Council, and the result has been the alienation
of a large number of Labor voters, who are going to stick
to Cook and see Hannah, who has the official imprimatur,
to the deuce. Hume Cook has, on every vital point, been
heart and soul with the Labor party during the first Par-
liament, while his record is one of the best in the whole
House for attendance, attention, and energy. Similarly,
there is a desire amongst a number of self-seekers to put
up a Labor nominee against Higgins for Northern Mel-
bourne, because, although one of the most brilliant
radical-socialists in the Commonwealth, he is independent
of the Lai.or caucus. Present writer was only recently
informed by a gentleman behind the scenes that the
Trades’ Hall would rather nominate a dog than a
candidate who wouldn’t consent to be a creature
of the caucus. If that is to be the policy of the
Labor organisers, the result will be that instead of
pushing the cause of Labor forward in all directions, the
movement will be forced into a narrow groove. Better
to gather under one banner all who are opposed to
capitalism and the plutocratic regime, than to have a
collection of empty-headed automata. Better to have a
cultured and attractive woman like Vida Goldstein to
identify her sex with the cause, and a radical of such
trustworthiness as Higgins, than addle-pated mem-
bers whose only use is to vote as they are told.
Any candidate of first-rate ability who is genuinely in
favor of the cause of Labor should be adopted by the
Labor leagues, and his personal following of voters
utilised to prop up the Labor cause. Where there is no
such candidate to adopt, and no such following to utilise,
then Labor should fight the battle single-handed. The
policy of officially recognising no candidate who isn’t
ticketed “ Labor ” may yet lead to the party putting up
candidates to fight against such men as Kingston, Isaacs
and Turner, and splitting the vote sufficientlv to let in
somebody of the old Duncan Gillies pattern.

Private Enterprise: A Westralian Land
Grant Railway.
“ Vigilans ” writes on the above subject :
One of the most scandalous pieces of jobbery to be
found in the Commonwealth exists in ’Groperland. I
refer to the Midland Railway Company. To the uniniti-
ated the thing is inexplicable, but by carefully going over
it you soon become convinced that there is something
rotten in other places besides Denmark. The conces-
sions consist not only of all the good land along the line
—except that which was taken up before the advent of
the Octopus but the Octopus actually owns land nearly
to Northam, on the Great Eastern Line, some 50 miles
away. One is given to suppose that a Land Grant Rail-
way has alternate blocks with the State, chessboard
fashion. This is not so here ; the co. simply picked all
the good land, leaving the sand for the State. Here you
find an uninterrupted run of 20 miles of soil; that belongs
to the company. There you find a run of 20 miles of
sand; that belongs to the State. The Octopus hardly owns
any land on the west or seaward side—that is all sand ;
but the Moore river has good land all along its banks for
20 miles, and the company owns the lot. And so it goes
on all up the line. The progress of Newcastle
has been retarded for years owing to the lock-
ing up of this land ; and there is no doubt
about its excellence, as land goes in Westralia.
As regards the railway from a settler’s point of view,
things are shocking indeed. The train is the slowest in
the States. . There is hardly any rolling stock, and the
poor cocky is to be pitied. His goods are left for five or
six weeks on an open platform, in all sorts o' weather. In
fact, hardly any of the sidings have sheds. Letters and
papers are dumped down anywhere. The first-class car
nages are dilapidated, and the second-class mere boxes
without any kind of a cushion, and you have to sit in them
for 16 hours Most of the officials do not wear any uniform,
and you can’t tell a guard from a crow. Chows, Japs,
Hindus, and whites are all penned up together in one of
these dens, and before you reach your destination you feel
sorry that you were ever born. These carriages are,
apparently, never cleaned out; the co. seems to have no
one to do it. The lengthsmen “live ” in canvas and bag
huts, with roofs made of bark and old kerosene tins, and
it’s no uncommon sight to see a careworn woman stand-
ing in the doorway with five or six children.
