The Possible Default.
The average State Treasurer hereabouts wastes a
vast amount of time explaining how small is our
public debt, after all, by comparison with our
State assets, and how thoroughly able the country
is to pay, and all the rest of it. It is getting to
be a very familiar story, and it is mostly dry rot
and vanity. What is troubling the man who runs
the great three-ballery in London is not the ques-
tion whether Australia can pay if it wants to, but
the growing doubt whether Australia seriously
intends to pay. It thinks it intends to pay, but
that isn’t exactly the same thing. The story about
all the unsold public lands doesn’t affect the
foreign Cohen so much as it did. He knows per-
fectly well that he has no lien on those public
lands, that he can’t seize them in case
of default, and that as all the proceeds
are being used as revenue, these proceeds
won’t come his way. He would be better
secured, in a sense, if there were no public lands,
for then Australia would be a place which was
paying its way by its own efforts, and where the
Sublic revenue wasn’t likely to get any worse.
ow, it is a place which is borrowing vast sums,
and accumulating large deficits, and using up its
public estate at the same time; so, bad as things
are now, they are likely to get worse as the public
estate diminishes. The existence of that public
estate only reminds our three-ball man that his
debtor is living on his capital, and that he is likely
to be a much worse debtor as the capital grows
less. The questions which the over-sea three-ball
fraternity is bound to ask itself with increasing
violence are these:
(1)_ If Australia prefers to have almost continuous
deficits and to use vast amounts of Joan money for
revenue purposes rather than pay sufficient taxes, will it
not some day, when it can’t get the loans, prefer to
default on its public debt rather than pay taxes ?
(2) If Australia reckons it can’t pay its way without
huge loans while its land revenue is still considerable,
how will it reckon it stands when its land revenue is
much smaller, and its interest bill much higher ? Won’t
it reckon that it is much less able to pay its way then ?
When any suggestion of a possible public insol-
vency is mooted there are always individuals
like Premier See who tramp about heavily
and indignantly, and call it a shocking and dis-
graceful aspersion, and say that no English-
speaking people will ever default on its obliga-
tions. But, after all, it is better to look at these
things calmly. The situation is just this : Australia
will default just as sure as sunrise unless it gets a
very different policy and a very different set of
political ideas. New South Wales, for example,
the day it can get no more loans, would probably
require, even if it practically gave up new public
works, and merely kept its existing ones in some
kind of repair, to retrench as it never retrenched
before, and to multiply its land and income taxes
threefold. Would it prefer that to going bank-
rupt ? It probably thinks it would. “Right-
thinking” and “Respectable” people say, with
irreat violence, that it most assuredly would. If
its present utter contempt for the necessity to
make ends meet even on paper is any criterion, it
wouldn't. If New South Wales is to be judged
by the sort of Governments it tolerates, and
the financial management it puts up with,
it would rather go bankrupt a dozen
times than make such an effort. Any-
how, the British creditor is growing to be a
worried man. He has the assurance of the
Government, which is borrowing £2,000,000 or
more every year, and using up the land assets as
well, and having regular deficits on top of all,
that this country would make any effort or sacri-
fice rather than default. And he wonders if the
assurance is good enough from such a quarter;
and why, with such a capacity to make £lO worth
of effort at a moment’s notice, there is such an
absence of one pennyworth of effort now ; also,
whether the country knows what sort of effort it
is talking about, and if the capacity for effort may
not be too much rusted when the time comes.
Certainly no English-speaking community lias
defaulted for a good many years, but everything
must have a beginning. And no English-speaking
community has been in the situation that we will
be in if the three-ball man stops our supplies.
Also, it is difficult to remember any other
English-speaking community which has got the
political habits we possess.

Yes-No Reid showed an Imperial hide at Pad-
dington on Saturday. He (D.T. report)—
proceeded to inquire into the “ loyalty ” of Mr. Deakin
as exemplified in three Acts of Parliament for which his
Government was responsible—three Acts of Parliament
which were the “ most mean and disloyal outrages
against the integrity of the British Empire that a colonial
public man has ever committed.” He referred to the
Act which aimed a serious blow at the marvellous chain
of mail steamers which were so important a factor in
Empire union, by seeking to prevent a few colored men
from “having billets which no white man should ever
want”; the immigration Act, which barred the entry
of their own kindred, who came across the sea in the
tracks of their own fathers and mothers ; and the navi-
gation law, the real provisions of which the Prime
Minister would not disclose.
The first Act (the Postal) was modelled, so far as
its lascar-boycotting provisions were concerned, on
a resolution come to by Reid’s own Government
when he was being supported by the Labor party ;
the second Act (Immigration Restriction) is a
direct copy of an Act which Reid placed on the
statute book of N.S.W. ; the third Bill, wrongly
referred to by the ignorant Yes-No as an Act, h%
knows nothing about, for it has not been intro-
duced and its provisions have not been disclosed,
aud seeing that Reid says they haven’t been dis-
closed it is amazing how Reid knows they are an
outrage. So these three things, one of them an
unknown quantity and two of them Reid ideas,
are, to Reid, “ most mean and disloyal outrages.”
A Confession.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The effort to maintain our supremacy would drive us
into an unwise policy of expansion in the search for new
markets; and while we were steadily getting-rid of the
most civilised, progressive and highly developed parts of
our Empire, we should be continually involved in the
“ small wars ” which have been a characteristic feature
of the Freetrade era, with a view of finding fresh
customers in savage countries. —London Times in an
article supporting Chamberlain.
Now we always thought that England had a
mission grand and bright
To wash the brownish heathen and to bear to him
the “Light”;
-That the blood of countless heroes in a noble cause
was shed,
And each hero should be pictured with a halo
round his head.
Oh ! the saintliness of England ! Oh ! the glory
that was hers —
From Kalahari’s deserts to the Himalayan firs 1
For the heathen wept for joy
When he saw the Bull dog Boy—
Crying, “ Take me as your Burden, for I idolise
you, sirs 1 ”
But the Thunderer has spoken, he has ope’d his
lips full wide,
He has made a strange confession, and we stand
electrified!
For it seems that heroes perished, and that streams
of blood were shed,
Just to sell the Briton’s calico to heathen, brown
and red,
And that, driven by necessity, they slaughtered
brother Ham
To persuade surviving kinsmen to buy shoddy
like a lamb !
And the London Times proclaims
That their objects and their aims
Were the “finding of fresh buyers” for the muck
of Birmingham 1

annually to continue the war which has been waged against “ bunny ’ for years past,

It takes Coincidence’s longest arm to hold
the plot of “ The Prodigal Parson ” together at
Sydney Palace. And yet he isn’t such a very
prodigal parson. He is, to begin with, just one
of those ordinary stage-persons who find a letter,
left lying around by the principal villain, which
seems to compromise his wife. The lady has a
father who has got into trouble, and she hasn’t
told the parson about it. The letter is really a
note from the father, whose name is Bill Jorkins
—signed “8.J.” for brevity and safety; Bills
always sign themselves “ B.” for short. The man
who delivers it is Ben Jaggins—another B.J. The
alarmed lady faints in Ben’s arms; and that, and
the discovery of the letter signed “8.J.” cooks
the lady’s goose. She is Cast Off. Just
about this time Ben Jaggins, who is a pretty
bad lot, being an actor, has a row with a pal out-
side the parson’s church, and in a rough-and-
tumble the pal is shot, with the parson’s revolver,
stolen from the organ-loft. (Presumably he was a
bad organist, and took it there to shoot back at
the congregation.) Now the villain, who is a
solicitor and Ben J.’s brother, is in love with
the parson’s wife, and also with the usual fortune,
to which he has secretly ascertained she is
entitled. The long arm before alluded to hurries
him to the church in the nick of time; he
accuses the innocent parson of the murder,
and the parson, who is the soul of honor
and truth and the embodiment of courage,
gets on a bike and escapes to Germany,
Germany being a dark place which the eye of the
police cannot penetrate. There, after eight
years, he finds that he has been living in the next
room to his wife ; and he makes the discovery on
the evening (1) which he had planned for his own
suicide ; (2) on which the wicked solicitor had
planned to carry off his old flame ; (3) on which
the lady discovers that she is an heiress. (Coinci-
dence is kept very busy just here.) Then Ben
Jaggins reforms and tells the truth about the row,
and the prodigal parson comes home to a wife and
a fortune big enough to buy a fatted calf every
Sunday for his lifetime.
* * * *
Miss Fitzmaurice G.ill is, of course, the
parson’s wife, and Chas. Blake the parson. Both
do excellent work, more particularly in the recon-
ciliation scene, though later on Blake’s assumed
character of Wiggles, what time he is waiting to
be called in to the fatted calf, is ineffective. For
the rest of the time he is earnest and convincing.
Especially in the earlier scenes Miss Hilda Fraser,
as a German beetle-gatherer, puts more character
into the principal comedy part than usual. Wilton
Power, the solicitor; John Cosgrove, as Ben
Jaggins, a part reeking with inconsistencies ;
Frank Pierce, in the small part of an old
villager; L. Walshe, as the pal who, though
he only, after all, sustained a flesh wound, yet
had a coroner’s inquest held upon hiih— these and
some others are from fair to good ; and the
mounting is by far the best of the season. Miss
Gill’s season at the Palace closes on Friday with
one night of “East Lynne.” On Monday the
Maud Lita Musical Comedy Co. appears in “Miss
Cleopatra.” The Bulletin always had an idea
that the exciting daughter of Ptolemy the Flute-
Player was married
#* * *
“Country Girl,” at Her Majesty’s,
Sydney, fills the theatre nightly, and if the
worried-looking Business-Manager Goodman, Or
glossy Stage-Manager Bracy send any Marconi-
grams to J. C. Williamson, c/o the Indian Ocean,
about the young lady the ’grams will be of a
flattering character. Miss Evelyn Scott deserves
a belated notice for the natural way in which she
laughs when advising “Johnny” to try again.
And Claude Bantock is assured that his Henry
VIII. get-up in the Rajah of Bhong turn
is a splendid copy of Holbein’s portrait
of the much-wived monarch. When present
writer wishes to build a palace the interior of the
Ministry of Fine Arts, which backgrounds second
act, will be copied—frescoes, double staircase,
yellow satin, and all.

At the Mutoscope parlors the number of
people who drop in to see the King on parade,
and by mistake turn the handle that shows why
Marie put the light out, continues undiminished.
Then they come back to see if Marie has a tired
day, when the light isn’t put out with the old irri-
tating punctuality ; but, so far, Marie hasn’t. Joe
Chamberlain addressing a huge meeting in the
Midlands has been added to the films, and is
almost as great an attraction as Marie ; and other
new ones are coming in every month or so. The
Mutoscope Co. is just now busy packing plant
and duplicates for an early opening in Maoriland,
and by the end of the year Wellington will be
able to glue its eye to a peep-hole, turn a handle,
and see enough to want to see more.
** * *
“ A Bad Character ” moves with at least
one foot out of the deepest of the ruts which
melodrama has made at Sydney Criterion. With
a really bad character in the middle of the stage,
it might have been expected that there would
have been at least one illicit infant in the back-
ground ; but there isn’t, and that is something to
the credit of playwright Scudamore’s ingenuity.
The hero, who at one part of the proceedings
seems to be running neck-and-neck with the
villain, is a Dr. Maurice, who early in the evening
joins that great majority of wronged innocents
who have got into gaol. Coming out, and
“ disbarred,” he turns into a limb-setting, fever-
nursing angel of the slums, and wins the
heart of Miss Gresham (who has just joined
the company), but doesn’t know .it. C. It.
Stanford also makes a first appearance at
this point in the rusty black of a disreputable
solicitor, and puts the Dr. up to personate another
Dr., who is heir to the biggest size in fortunes
which the Criterion keeps in stock. He consents,
and then finds that the girl he is desperately in
love with is in love with his other self, Dn
Maurice; and the rest of the evening is spent
in getting him out of the mess and comfort-
ably married to the heroine, as the gal-
lery requires. Vivian Edwards plays the
hero with an earnestness which borders
on violence. He lets himself go entirely—never
seems to be capable of another pound of effort.
C. R. Stanford and Miss Gresham greatly
strengthen the cast; Miss Gresham at least looks
all that a lady should about whom so much fuss, is
made; and Stanford is a cool, calm solicitor. Miss
Beatrice Holloway has a rest, but the other mem-
bers of the stock co. keep their ends up satisfac-
torily in a play which, taking it all round, is a
good way better than the average.
** * *
The silence which has brooded over the
Sydney Royal during the past week will be
musically broken on Saturday when Musgrove’s
Grand Comio Opera Co. will re-appear witli a
new opera, “ The Serenade.” It is said that “The
Serenade,” like “The Fortune-Teller,” revives
the old traditions of opera bouffe, and has to be
seriously respected from a musical point of view.
Short of the return of a class of comic opera suoh
as Gilbert and Sullivan wrote, and which, since
their time, has only been seen in such rare pro-
ductions as the “Rose of Persia,” opera bouffe
seems about the best refuge from the reign of
inane “ musical comedy,” and “ The Serenade”
deserves a warm welcome.
** m *
“The Favorite” will win the St. Leger
for the last time, on Sydney Lyceum stage, on
Friday, and the master in villainy will cease from
the folly of dragging his victim up several flights
of stairs to drown her in a cistern on the roof-top,
instead of strangling her quietly in his comfort-
able and roomy cellar. But the principle of such
actions will abide, for “The Favorite” will be
followed by “The Painted Woman”—for which
vast preparations have been made. It is
more or less reliably reported that there is
a demon editor in “The Painted Woman,” who
murders his victims by transfixing them with a
file as they read their tender little poems to him,
and then boils them down in the lead-pot of the
stereo room. That, and death by whale-bite, are
about the only methods of murder that have not
been exploited in melodrama; and a successful
melodrama must have a new brand of murder.
** * *
Miss Cornelia May-Little, £*n American
contralto with a hyphen and, Australia hopes,
just a suspicion of twang, will make acquaintance
with this deeply-indebted country, at Sydney
Centenary Hall, on Saturday. Many pleasant
things have been said about the lady of the
hyphen in other countries, and she will approach
Australia under the patronage of N.S.W.’s breezy
salt-horse Governor and of Premier See. Henri
Staell will apply friction to the violin, and the
piano will be smitten by the fair white hand of
Miss Constance Brandon-Usher.

Mr. Philip Lytton, or whoever keeps the
gate for the amateur combination known as “The
Players,” is to be congratulated on the admission
of the lady who appeared on the programme as
Mrs. Maclntyre when the club presented “The
Liars ” a little while ago. Mrs. Maclntyre, who
has a graceful stage-presence and a pleasant voice,
sustained her part of Beatrice Ebernoe with
simplicity and naturalness. Miss Irby Marshall,
who took the leading role, Lady Jessica Nepean,
is making good progress up along the stage road.
She has a more than common share of good looks
and stature and ought to do well in light, high-
comedy parts—by and bye, of course, when she
has learnt a thousand things, elocution among
them. Men playing in “The Liars” were all
pretty bad, but Mr. Lytton’s badness did not
equal the badness of the others.

Since the Tivoli was last referred to here,
manager Leete has been made a J.P., and the
letters of fire in front of the house have, in con-
sequence, been burning with extraordinary bril-
liance. Several jokes, which have been on the
premises for many years, have also been awarded
old-age pensions in honor of the event. Next to
this, the most important thing that has happened
is the announcement that Marzella’s birds will
migrate at the end of the week. Some of these
remarkable performers have already been 26 years
in the business (Madame’s husband used to em-
ploy them to enhance the effect of his acrobatic
work before Madaine made them a show in them-
selves) ; and, in view of their advancing years, if
Sydney doesn’t turn up to see them this week it
isn’t ever likely to get the chance again. On
Saturday the bill is to be entirely changed.

At Her Majesty’s (Melbourne) the future
Prime Minister of Italy is accidentally slain by
the boss revolutionist every night until further
notice. “The Eternal City” has attractions for the
multitudes. They take the Pope quite seriously,
and are much interested when that ancient-looking
widower turns out to be the long lost parent of
heroic Julius. All. things considered, it seems a
pity that Hall Caine’s drama didn’t bring the
Pope’s wife back from the grave, and place her at
the head of a Women’s Rights Party in Future
Italy. This would permit of a sensational episode
in the Vatican garden— the strong-minded matron,
corning along to ask his Holiness blessing on her
Political Reform Sisterhood, would recog-
nise him as her early love, and die of mental
shock to avoid complications, whereupon the
astounded Pontiff would slowly shake his head
as a signal for the curtain to descmid.
The many things which do happen in ‘ The
Eternal City ” are but few in comparison with
the stirring events that might have occurred if
Hall Caine’s imagination were equal to his
audacity. For instance, a great opportunity is
missed when Miss Jeffries smashes her brittle
plaster bust of Julius with a hammer to prevent
it being photographed for the information of
“foreign Governments,” the bust being the only
alleged likeness of Julius by which foreign Govern-
ments could identify him. If the bust were a
clay image, like those that Gallando modelled at
Rickards’ of yore, the lady might tear off lumps
from the back of the head and bang them into the
image’s face with fine dramatic effect. She might
even miss the image on one occasion and land a
mud poultice against the Prime Minister’s shirt-
front, the drum in the orchestra emphasising the
incident with a mighty boost
On Friday afternoon (27th), Miss Jeffries
and Julius Knight, assisted by the co., will give a
matinee performance at Her Majesty’s in aid of
the theatrical charities. Firstly, Julius will star
in a playlet entitled “At the Telephone ”; then
he and Miss Jeffries will undertake the balcony
scene from “Romeo and Juliet”; and subse-
quently they will produce a scene from “Monsieur
Beaucaire ” —the scene wherein Miss J. and the
other ladies strew roses for Julius to walk upon.
A break in the worship of Julius is to be provided
by Mel. B. Spurr, who will hold the floor for
10 minutes, after which there will be a further
interval of 15 minutes for afternoon tea.
*m * *
Fitzgeralds’ circus has packed up its
numerous belongings and gone in the direction
of Korumburra, where the coal comes from, so
the iron edifice by Prince’s bridge is empty once
again. A mysterious footnote to the Fitzs’.
advertisement suggested that something of a
startling character will transpire within their
permanent structure at Xmas time.
*# # *
“ The Great Millionaire ” has wallowed in
his riches for four weeks at Melbourne Royal, and
will probably continue to be a hard, masterful
capitalist for another fortnight. Some parts of
Bland Holt’s show are beyond the understanding
of the ordinary yellow-drama-public, but as a d.c.
attraction it scores more heavily than anything he
has produced thus far. In fact, it is rather sad to
find so many of Melbourne’s choicest oitizens
assembled together at 3s. per head when their
sense of what is due to tne management and
themselves should prompt them to make
it ss. Perhaps this rush of rank and'
fashion to the popular-priced Royal accounts
for “ The Great Millionaire ” being now more
thoughtfully treated by some of the company than
it was aforetime. Mrs. Bland Holt has improved
her work, likewise Miss Ireland. Generally
speaking there is a little less inattention to detail
than usual, but this remark doesn’t apply to Miss
Ross, whose habit of laughing at the wroncr
moment seems to be growing on her. Last
Saturday she giggled violently just when her
character required her to look serious and senti-
mental. Perhaps she was tickled by the thought
that she was impersonating a hungry damsel in
loose hair and a somewhat abbreviated frock.
*# * #
All this while Mel. B. Spurr has been
giving his one-man show at the Athenaeum Hall,
with good financial results. The solitary Spurr is
a mild, genial musical humorist, who varies his
entertainment, and has the personal recommenda-
tion of being his own author. Melbourne speaks
•kindly of Spurr, and would be glad to supply him
with audiences for some time to come, but it
seems the Athenaeum Hall is not available, there-
fore the indefatigable little man will close his
present season on Saturday night, and ravage
N.S.W., tinder Herbert Flemming’s guidance,
arriving at Sydney on Boxing Night.
* * *
Last Saturday’s programme at Melbourne
Opera House seemed much the same as it was a
week ago. Cirnoc insulted the majesty of the
law by getting quit of sundry handcuffs and leg-
irons without assistance from the police ; also, the
Dumonds made respectable music, the Bioscope
gave a magnified representation of the busy bees
that improve the shining hour, and an irrepressible
young man talked and sang a very long narrative
in a very long overcoat. On Monday night,
Winton and MGinty were due to reappear, after
a long absence. The return of MGinty together
with the man who pulls the string will help to fill
a large-sized want.
*# # #
Rickards made his latest series of engage-
ments under an unlucky star, seemingly. Two of
the first of his new “turns”—Jennie Grey and
the Cirnocs —left Europe by the German mail
steamer. Cirnoc, senior, died on arriving at
Adelaide, leaving his son to succeed him as
“Handcuff King,” or whatever he calls| himself.
Jennie Grey, proceeding to Sydney, had to post-
pone her first appearance at Rickards’ on account
of a surgical operation. Then Clark and Hamilton,
a married couple of comedy-sketch artists, were
landed at Melbourne with the lady seriously in-
disposed. She is at present in Surgeon O’Hara’s
private hospital, and will not be able to show for
another three weeks. Should death or sickness
befall any more of Manager ’Arry’s importations,
the “profession” will have him marked as a
dangerous person to deal with, whilst he, for his
part, will want a clean bill of health from his
“star ” before he signs the contract.
** * *
The weary-looking co. that perpetrated
“Charley’s Aunt,” at Melbourne Bijou, during
Cup week, seems to have good reason for its
dejected appearance. At Melbourne Distriot
Court, the other day, E. B. Boothman, & member
of the aforesaid co., got a verdict for nine weeks
salary due, from manager Robert Henry, up to
March last. Also, the same hopeful plaintiff got
a verdict against Mrs. Henry— otherwise Miss
Billie Howard—who took over the nominal con-
trol of the business from the time when her hus-
band laid down his burden of responsibility. Off
the stage, the Robert Henry Co. is certainly
amusing. # # # *
In the present dullness of things theatrical
in Adelaide, the Theatre Royal has done good
business, at popular prices, with a Bioscope,
which opened for a tentative four nights, and found
it well to stay on. It is advertised as direct
from New York ” —unnecessarily, for everything
about it, from Jeffries punching Fitzsimmons to
the comic business, is as American as it can be.
Elsewhere, among a wealth of concerts, stands out
a crowded Town Hall farewell to Ethel Hantke,
a young contralto of Conservatorium training,
who is going to Europe for the finishing touches.
Some recent extracts from Sydney Telegraph :
During the last ten years 110,000 more people had left
Victoria than came into it, and in spite of the natural
birth increase the population had only increased by
60,000. . . . Was there anything in Mr. Dkakin’s doctoring
of Victoria to warrant N.S.W. in following him ?
During the past few years the unprecedentedly lavish
expenditure of borrowed money by the Government had
been the means of keeping up a condition of fictitious
prosperity in the labor market of this State (N.S.W.). .. .
With the enforced curtailment of loan expenditure there
is threatened a corresponding decrease in employment.
Thousands of young and vigorous Victorians have been
leaving their native State.
In the last ten years Victoria has borrowed only
£4,679,694, while N.S.W. has borrowed £22,894,299.
It is high time the Telegraph sorted out its
feelings. One-half the paper says that N.S.W.
had, during the last ten years, a larger increase of
population than Victoria because it was Freetrade
(which it wasn’t; it was moderately Protectionist
up to 1896, and after that Reid sneaked in Pro-
tection in a small way). The other half of the
paper says that N.S.W. owed its growth of
population to the fact that it borrowed on an
enormous scale to supply work for the people and
to keep up an artificial increase of population.
One end of the Telegraph is of opinion that
Victoria’s comparatively small increase of popula-
tion was owing to Protection ; the other end of
the same paper holds that it was because Victoria
almost entirely gave up the policy of buying
population with loans. When it is a case of
cursing John See the N.S.W. increase of popula-
tion is an unnatural and bloated development
based on huge borrowings; when it is a case of
supporting George Reid N.S.W.’s increase is a
healthy growth arising out of the Freetrade
N.S.W. never had. Will the confused paper
kindly call a meeting of itself and fix on a definite
attitude ?
«
The dishonored Government cheque, which
managed to fill in a good deal of one N.S.W.
Assembly sitting last week, was not so badly dis-
honored that Parliament, which has little time
left to do something useful this session, could not
have ignored the circumstance. It is some years
now since there was a serious row about the City
Bank dishonoring a parcel of State cheques. By
a Treasury oversight a deposit was not made to
the credit of one particular public account. The
bank had some grievance to air, and promptly dis-
honored the cheques. A fierce time then ensued,
and the irate Ministry drew every penny out of
its scattered accounts and consolidated them in
the Bank of N.S.W.
Serious discussion in N.S.W. Assembly
on the Reduction of Members Referendum Bill:
Mr. O’Sullivan : The hon. member for Willoughby had
stated that he had been stopped by the bar of the House.
Why did he not jump over it ? (Laughter.) What about
Mr. Storey, of Randwick ?
Mr. Storey : I am all right.
Mr. O’Sullivan : Where was he last night?
Mr. Storey : In bed. (Renewed laughter.)
Mr. O’Sullivan : He got up at suburban meetings and
talked about reform, but when the crisis comes for
reform, where was he ? In bed 1 (Roars of laughter.)
An Hon. Member: Why not take a secret ballot 1
Mr. O’Sullivan : What is the use of a secret ballot when
men are in bed?
An Hon. Member: Let them vote by post.
Mr. O’Sullivan : How could they vote by post when they
are in bed ?
Mr. B. B. O’Oonor: By bed-post. (Great laughter.)
Jack Want starts talking fiscalism as soon
as he touches Sydney, but it can be safely said he
never fully formed any fiscal view. Henry Parkes
considered him the most hopelessly incompetent
of politicians in fiscal matters. Jack took up ad
valorem duties with great gusto one time, and the
speech with which he supported the Jennings
crowd was a flashing harangue in defence of Free-
trade and Protection. After many years he now
unbosoms himself thus to Sydney Telegraph :
I cannot see how we can possibly do without any duty
on English goods, in view of the revenue required by the
Commonwealth. But, on the other hand, I am certainly
not in favor of heavy duties on foreign goods.
And the Freetrader who can’t see a little thing
like that is being urged by hosts of people to re-
enter active politics.
Mr. Alexander Oliver, whose trip in the
Ovalau was so near a disaster, was commissioned to
do a sort of Irish-landlord work on Norfolk
Island. Hampden, when N.S.W. Governor and
suzerain of the island, had approved of certain
leasing regulations, but the islanders would have
none of them. They claim ownership of the lands
they occupy, and some of them who live in old
huts built by the Government for convicts and
other purposes, refuse to take a, notice to quit.
The main part of Mr. Oliver’s mission was to get
the islanders to conform to the new dispensation,
or failing that, to devise some cheap and handy
mode of eviction which wouldn’t cause unpleasant
things to be said about his Ex. in the papers.
The island is not now subject to N.S.W. ; it has
not yet been taken over by the Commonwealth,
and Gov. Rawson, in whose hands it is, has all the
responsibility and no satisfaction.

Hawthorn Brick Company (Victoria), have placed an order

Melbourne, for a new Double Brick Machine with Feed
and Pug Mills complete, having a capacity of 2000 bricks

Engine of 250 1.H.P., fitted with automatic valve gear and
all latest improvements. This will make the third Brick
Press Messrs. Fritsch, Holzer and Co., have erected at
their works, and is a sign of improvement in the trade.*

British manufacturers have been beaten in an
open competition by an Australian engineering firm. We
have ascertained that the Mond Gas Co. of Staffordshire
have accepted the tender of Mephan Ferguson, of Mel-
bourne, for the supply of about 13 miles of Ferguson’s
Patent Lockingbar Steel Pipes, from 18 inches to 36 inches
diameter. The machinery for manufacturing these pipes
has all been made at Ferguson’s General Engineering
Works, Footscray, and shipped to Tipton, in Staffordshire,
where the branch works are being erected. The same
firm have also erected engineering and pipe works at
Wanganui, New Zealand, and have contracted to supply
the Wanganui Borough Council with about 20 miles of
Spiral Riveted Steel Pipes for the town water supply.*

The most interesting deep-prospecting at
Bendigo just now is being done by the flew Chum
Railway, on the New Chum line ; the Garden Gully
United, on the Garden Gully line ; and the Great
Extended Hustlers, on the Hustlers line. The Railway’s
and Hustlers crosscuts are the deepest on those lines;
but there are deeper workings than the G.G. United’s.
All three have been great producers in the past.

The Queensland cotton industry is being spoken
of as a new thing. Captain Bobby Towns, after whom
Townsville is named, pioneered it in ’6B, but was not
successful, though prices were much higher than now.

Last year there was a drought, so N.S.W. nad
a loan-drunk because times were Dad and it wanted the
money. This year there promises to be a boom harvest,
so N.S.W. proposes to have as big a loan-drunk as it can
because times are good and it can afford to owe the
money. When it is dry it gets drunk because it isn’t wet,
and when it is wet it gets as drunk as the drink will
go because it isn’t dry. When money is dear it borrows
and spends because it is the State’s duty to help the
people at such times, and when money is plentiful it
borrows and spends because it would be a pity to miss a
good market. In accordance with this principle the year’s
loan estimates, tabled last week, provide for an expendi-
ture of £1,721,384 all, of course, to go, as usual, in
permanent and reproductive works. Some country hos-
pitals want new wards and morgues ; and £lO,OOO is to
be borrowed for the purpose. Additional wards mean
larger annual requirements for upkeep and larger
Government grants; still, this goes in with the other
“reproductive” works. A number of politicians are
clamoring for new railway lines, and, to close their
months for a few months, trial surveys are to be made ;
and these are “permanent and reproductive works,” like
the rest. A number of bridges and culverts, built out of
former loans, are moth-eaten, and the Owe’Sullivan pro-
poses to replace them and build some others, and £73,200
is down for that. Some ancient trees that have dropped
into the Darling are to be fished out, and this repro-
ductive work goes in with the culverts. The handles of
several hammers inCockatoo Island dock have been broken,
and a number of chisels have lost their edges; and £1728 is
down under that head. A ditch is wanted somewhere
near the Rookwood cemetery ; another £lOOO. The
Owe’See Government can’t even add a few pounds of
type, &c., to the State printing office without putting the
country further into pawn to get it. And the country
which does this sort of thing talks about its great natural
resources, and its wealth, and its “sturdy independence."
No doubt even the Owe’See Government can
get the money if it likes to bid high enough for it—that
is to say, if it puts enough permanent burden on the
backs of the people. Already, having been spoken to
severely by Bull-Cohen, it has been busy inventing
devices with expensive frills on them for raising the wind;
the bargain with the Citizens’ Assurance Co., for instance.
The latest is to entice money to the Savings Bank by
offering 3J per cent, from January I—an1 —an exceedingly
risky way of raising money because the deposits are
practically at call. Still, if the Owe’See loan policy is to
go on, the local market must be relied on, for the Vic-
torian experience proves that it would be hopeless to go
to any Bull-Cohen except the one who does the special
line of business with young gambling spendthrifts at
appalling rates of usury. Victoria had half-a-dozen
things in its favor :
(1) The papers had boomed Irvine’s “reform” and
Bent’s “ economy ” policy for 100 times more than it was
worth ; and Jack Want comes back with the assurance—
probably perfectly true—that it made a deep and
excellent impression on Cohen.
(2) It was a conversion loan There was no need to
find new lenders.
(3) Three options were offered ; there were flies for all
sort 3 of fish.
(4) Victoria had been off the English market for years
And (5) it was advertised as probably no Australian loan
ever was advertised before.
Yet, in spite of all this and more, the underwriters were
stuck with about a million and a half of the loan—with,
that is, a fourth of the lot. With that million and a half
still undigested, and with the underwriters wiser than
they were two months ago, what chance has N.S. Wales
lf Irvine, after crawling on his stomach to Cohen,
failed, even at rates which amount to nearly 5 per cent,
in some cases, what chance have See and Owe’Sullivan ?
If Victoria, which has not been on the market for years,
could not induce lenders to renew, how can N.S.W.,
which hasn’t been off the market for years, hope to
induce new lenders to lend—except as before remarked,
on the same terms as to the young gambling spendthrift ?
And still the Owe’See Government comes along with its
million-and-three-quarter loan estimates—for printer’s
type, some culverts, a ditch, and a quantity of black-
smith’s tools among the rest 1 If that doesn’t “restore
confidence ” and open Bull-Cohen’s pouch, and satisfy
him that bis money has been wisely put into profit-
producing industries, what can ?
N.S.W. has over £14,000,000 falling due during
tne next ten years. If there is local monev available
there is plenty to do with it in meeting these liabilities
which will have to be met. If somebody must be paid 4
or 44 per cent., let it be our own people. To renew
foreign loans at the high rates that seem inevitable will
drain the country for more } r ears than we can see.

The reduction in profit is no doubt due to the increase in
the cost of money. The maximum rate chargeable on
advances is 4J per cent. ; and, as the minimum fixed for the
last issue of debentures would show a return of £3 19s. 7d.
to the lender, there can be no profit whatever for the
bank in the business which is now being taken. This will
of course, affect profits for years to come as well a 3 for
the moment, as the debentures now being issued will not
mature until 1910. Since March, 1901, the cost of
money to the bank has increased by 12s. per cent. The
bank is economically managed ; but no vein of economy
struck will pay expenses on the difference between
hi 19s. 7d. and £4 10s. It becomes a question either of
declining business or of charging more than 44 per cent.,
orof furnishing the bank with capital out of the State
revenue instead of out of loans, which is, what should be
done. Assuredly anything should be done rather than
restrict the State Bank’s operations; they should rather be
enlarged. Of £224,974 advanced during the past vear no
less than £194,755 was applied to pay off liabilities to
private lenders. It is when money is dear, more than at
any other time, that the State ought to be in a position
to assist the farmer in this way, for this is just the time
when the private Cohen will use the circumstance to
demand a crushing rate of interest. Curtailment of
operations can’t then be seriously thought of.
Two heavy items appear on the debit side of
this year’s profit and loss account as presented £2OOO
writtenoff Government stock, and £BB9provision forextinc-
tion of discount on the issue of debentures. Both of these
are signs of honest finance. When a loan is floated at a dis-
count the full amount which the bank will eventually have
to pay is debited ; the discount, if there is one, is taken
m on the other side as an asset ; but an annual charge is
made tovvards its extinction, and the amount this year is
a very substantial £BB9, equal to 10 per cent, of the total
discounts. This is sound, honest, and straightforward,
and a pattern for the Government to follow in its financial
operations.

The reserves are larger, in proportion to capital, than
those of any other Australian bank ; an amount equal to
the whole of the capital has been added in little more
than six years. Yet in view of the precarious nature of
the industry which has brought such sudden wealth to
the State and the bank, the policy of adding largely to
the reserves is certainly the only prudent one. Com-
paring the position further with that disclosed 12 months
ago these results show up :

Some very heavy movements are disclosed here, which,
generally speaking, while increasing its (probably tem-

position. The liquid securities still, however, represent
about £56 10s. per cent, of the total liabilities to the
public, which is a very much larger proportion than most
banks hold. But Westralia is liable to violent fluctua-
tions, and special precautions are needed there, especi-
ally as the Westralian Bank is working on so very large
a proportion of non-interest-bearing money at call. But,
keeping in view the very large amount of non-profit
earning specie and bullion on hand (£791,623 of the
liquid assets consists of these two items), what a remark
ably profitable business it is while it lasts 1

The co., of course, computes its dividend on its total
capital, actually paid and capitalised, and therefore calls
the present dividend 8 per cent, on £75,000; but the
position so far as the original shareholder is concerned is
as stated above; The result of the year’s operations is
again extremely fine ; the profit is equal to £22 13s. 6d.
per cent, on the shareholders’ funds, and of those share-
holders’ funds only 31 per cent, was actually put up by
the shareholders. It is such a very remarkable record that
it is not surprising that the State, which is encouraged to
do so many things that don’t pay, should occasionally
look longingly this way. Nevertheless the State,
when it regards the large profits, will have to
remember as well that a company couldn’t do the busi-
ness if it did not build up a reserve—if it was not content
to make profits and put a large proportion of them by.
Besides real estate, the Colonial Mutual has £lOO 000 in
public securities and at fixed deposit; and this, while it
permits of trading far beyond what would be possible and
safe on the paid capital alone, gives a return capable of
itself paying over 10 per cent, on the original £37,500.
* * * *
No wonder the rich landlords of Western
Victoria are cutting up their estates and selling them
while the boom lasts. Here are a few of the prices
realised the other day for blocks of Warwarick and
Gnotuk, alongside Camperdown :—lO6 acres, with cot-
tage, £35 10s. per acre ; 71 acres, with cottage, £3O per
acre ; 55 acres, £3O per acre ; 30 acres, £27 per acre, and
so on, the blocks mostly running from 30 to 70 acres and
bringing from £l7 to £35 per acre. In about one genera-
tion the unearned increment on that land represents
about 3000 per cent, on its original price, whether dum-
niied or honestly bought. Ifc has been run up enormously
by the expenditure of loan money. It has been further
run up by almost entire freedom from taxation : a long
succession of Tory Governments preferred to pile up
nearly £3,000,000 worth of deficits rather than make land
carry a fair share of the public burdens. And now the
people have to pay interest on the big loans ; the people
have to pay up the deficits in some way or other ; and the
people, just because of these circumstances, have to pay
about £lO an acre more for their land.

* } * * *
~ . Pprth, on The Bulletin’s view
that the Westralian estimates for the current year show
£230,147, instead of the surplus of £1512
which Treasurer Gardiner claimed :
“ To have excluded the surplus of the preceding year
would have been a mistake in accountancy. Treasurer
Gardiner found himself in present and prospective pos-
session of so much, and he proceeded to allocate it. He
could without difficulty have arranged for a surplus of
£500,000 by the simple process of allowing the cash to
accumulate. Would The Bulletin approve of an idle
half-million? Treasurer Gardiner believes in spending
all revenue (above bare cost of administration, etc.) on
roads, bridges, courthouses, schools and railways. Thus
we find many unusual items on p. 15 of this budget
Water Supply, £27,150; Roads and Bridges, £134,838;
Harbors and Rivers, £29,170; Railways, £87,000;
Hospitals, £48,475 ; Schools, £45,505 ; Various Works,
£149,005; Public Batteries, £25,000; Development of
Mining, £22,000 ; Rabbit Fencing, £28,000 ; New Railway
Works and Improvements, £39,000; total, £635,143. It
would have been easy for Treasurer Gardiner to have
brought down a Boom Budget, relegating this £635,143 to
the schedule of a Loan Bill, and flattering the public with
the notion of a big surplus.”
And all this leaves the original contention just
where it was. The facts are simple—this year’s estimated
revenue is £3,613,200, and the estimated expenditure is
£3,843,347, That means only one thing—the year’s
accounts, standing by themselves, show an estimated
deficit of £230,147. A company, which makes a profit one
year, is credited with the profit for that year; if it makes
a smaller loss in the following year, that loss is shown
separately—it isn’t disguised as a profit by simply de-
ducting the loss from the previous profit. So with a
State ; the surplus of one year can’t be shown as the
surplus of half-a-dozen years. What should be done with
a surplus when that rare bird appears is another matter
altogether. The best use for it is to put it into the
Sinking Fund, which Westralia alone, of all the States, is
building up in a systematic way. Or it might go into
that separate Public Works Fund which every State badly
needs, and from which all construction should be paid.
Anyhow, Treasurer Gardiner is wasting an
invaluable opportunity. He ought to start a separate
Public Works Fund, to which the cost of all new works
should be charged. He should show, in his ordinary
Financial Statement, the cost of government and the
cost of the maintenance of old works only, keep these
items as low as possible, and transfer his surplus to the
Public. Works Fund. If he does not, a new Treasurer
will arise some day who, by the simple process of charging
to loans the works which Gardiner charges to revenue,
will show that he can run the State much more
cheaply than Gardiner, and all Gardiner’s explanations
won’t prevent him being branded as an extrava-
gant fool and the new man hailed as a financial
saviour. If the new man spends £300,000 more in the
cost of government, but “saves” £600,000 by charging
works to loans instead of to revenue, he will still pose as
the economist who saved £300,000 a year. Honest old
Dibbs fell in N.S.W. by just that trick. Whereas if the
cost of government and the cost of public works are
kept separate, such a trick is impossible, and the honest
Treasurer gets credit for what he has done.
“ Fluffy ” sums up the services of the Treasurer
whom Westralia will probably lose before long
“ Treasurer Gardiner has urged during his two years of
office—(a) that public borrowing should be discouraged ;
(5) that, where imperative, it should be local, and not in
London ; (c) that, as far as possible, all public works
should be constructed out of revenue. His first budget
contained an exposure of the blackmail that is levied on
the ‘ colony ’ when approaching England for funds—(l)
the ‘accrued interest' extortion, (2) the Imperial Trea-
sury’s little bit, (3) discounts, the worst of all. His second
budget gives particulars of a local inscribed loan of
£483,215. Another State, about the same time, borrowed
£600,000, the expenses being £8175. Gardiner’s local
loan cost £4493, which is cheap as loans go. As to the
W.A. debt, there is an accumulated sinking fund of
£655,069, and the trustees have also redeemed £180,400.
There will be added to the sinking fund this year
£176,681. All loans, as they fall due, will be redeemed
automatically. When the Constitution Amendment Bill
was recently under discussion, a provision was included
by Treasurer Gardiner that all future loans must provide
for an adequate sinking fund. This is probably without
precedent.”

As to some of the dividends and bonuses mentioned
above, it should be added that nearly £35,000 of the total
was only nominally paid, calls to the same amount or
thereabouts having been made as the dividends were
declared. The capital now stands at a round £70,000
the first time for years that it has been in round figures.
All shares are now £2 10s. paid. Six months ago the
reserves were represented by £5OOO contingency fund and
£13,413 undivided profit; the contingency fund has in
the last balance-sheet advanced to £13,000 without visible
cause, which is a slovenly and unsatisfactory way of doing
things. The trading profit during the past 12 months
was equal to 26J per cent, on the capital (£70,000), or to
21i per cent, on combined capital and reserves.

Having, through the State Produce Depdb in
London, made a market for the State’s wine, butter, &c.,
the reactionary S. Australian Government has now
decided to sell the business. The State sows ; the syndi-
cate reaps. The terms have not been disclosed, or the
parties ; it is known, however, that ex-Governor Kintore,
the guinea-pig about whom one of the English Judges
■said rather severe things lately, made a proposal some
time back to find capital to push the business. Gilbey,
the gin-man, has also been nibbling. The depdt, which
is chiefly for wine, has now a well-established brand, the
Orion; the testing, blending, and other expert business
at the other end has all through been in the hands of
Burney Young, who was a S. Australian vigneron himself
before Kingston picked him out for the job.

N.S.W. official Statistical Register, part
XII., just issued, gives some particulars regarding the
Australian Metropolitan Life Assurance 00., which were
not available when the co.’s balance-sheet was analysed
in The Bulletin Nov. 19. On a rough estimate it was
then assumed that the total amount assured by the co.
was £400,000; and on that basis the assurance fund,
apart from the alleged value of premises written up, was
equal to under 4d. in the £of the amount assured. That
really did the co. more than justice. The Statistical
Register shows that, at Dec. 31, 1902, the amount of
ordinary assurance in force was £77,907; of industrial
assurance, £426,383 ; total, £504,290. At the same date
apart from the writing up before referred to, the assur-
ance-fund was £4693. This would be equal to 21d. in the
£. For every £ which the co. has undertaken to pay, it
has, so far, apart from its own small capital and the paper
on the Brisbane building, saved up the price of a postage
stamp. a

After all, Dick Seddon won’t be able to hang
out his shingle in England over a mutton shop ; the com-
mittee which he appointed to inquire into the idea has
killed it. Neither Seddon nor the Produce Commissioner
proposed to keep the shops open for long; they proposed
simply to make a trade and then go out, and that seemed
poor business, anyhow. The Commission says that it is
not much good just opening one shop in a 100,000-people
city-that the only people who will ever know anything
about it will be the few who live round about it and who
rush in before breakfast for a pound and a half
of chops. But perhaps the strongest point is that
tne mutton-trade can’t stand much more developing.
Last year, in order to export 3,667,101 carcases, the
states flocks had to be reduced bv 1,340.678 (from
20,342,727 to 19,002,049); and at that rate the M.L. sheep
will soon be as rare a bird as the moa. By comparison,
the Argentine figures are striking; with flocks of any-
thing from 80,000,000 to 120,000,000 head (the estimates
y, ai T). only 2,827,439 carcases were shipped. Certainly
the Maoriland meat has been better than the Argentine’s
f° i wi .f h awful slaughter among its ewe
lambs, the Maoriland produce is on the down grade.

The Broken Hill Prop, has now spent over
£1,260,000 on plant, tramways, etc., of which about
£840,000 has been written off. Almost every develop-
ment, in either the mine or the laboratories, makes a
new plant necessary, and that usually means scrapping
an old one, even though it has been used only a year or
two. Half-a-dozen times or more the directors have
announced that “ the heavy outlay on construction
account is now almost at an end”; but it has meant
nothing, and any similar announcements in the future
probably won’t, mean anything either so long as
economies and improvements in extraction are possible
—and there is a lot of scope in that direction yet.
At present several important, though not particularly
expensive, works are in progress. Electric haulage is
being extended, both underground and at surface ; the
driers and other parts of the new chemical zinc-plant are
ordered ; and the permanent steam-condensing plant
is being built. The co. is building its zinc plant to work
the Carmichael-Delprat process, notwithstanding that
the courts have not yet decided whether those patents
don’t conflict with Potter’s. If it is held that they do
(and that is not at all improbable), it looks as though the
co. will be in trouble.

The discovery of that strangled babe in
Hyde Park emphasises the urgent need there is of
a foundling home right in the city of Sydney, at
which the mother may leave her offspring, legiti-
mate or otherwise, if she can no longer look after
it. In Sydney, once a girl has a babe clinging to
her, she becomes a slave if the father of the infant
deserts her. Wherever she works she has to work
without wages. On economic grounds alone,
every Australian city should have a free foundling
home, for the Commonwealth wants children.
And if they turn up in too great numbers at the
Home, they can be strangled there by the Govern-
ment with less publicity than when the fearsome
deed is done by the girl herself.

A quaint legal point ;
A Molong (N.S. W.) citizen has spent eight months and
much money trying to understand the meaning of
“simultaneous.” On March 26 he applied fora condi-
tional purchase at the local Lands Office. The clerk who
was attending to him, before actually disposing of the
matter, asked another Moloriger what he wanted at the
counter. Citizen No. 2 then applied for the same area of
land as No. 1. Their rival applications were then dealt
with, and No. 2 won. Citizen No. 1 appealed, but the
Land Appeal Court held that the clerk was right. He
then went to the Supreme Court, on the ground that his
application was lodged first and should have priority.
The other side maintained that each applicant entered
the office as soon as it was opened, which made the appli-
cations *’ simultaneous,” according to section 6 of the
Act and common sense. After two days’ arguing,-the
Null Court blew Citizen No. 1 out. It that
simultaneous applications were not to be made withjthe
mechanical accuracy of a comic opera chorus. If so, the
receiving officer could only receive one document in each
hand and one between his teeth, but if more simultaneous
claims were lodged at once, it would be physically
impossible to receive them. Their Honors were, no do]ubt,
right. Still, if “simultaneous” applications may be
lodged one after the other, how much one-after-the-
©therness may be allowed before the simultaneousness
is used up ?
A coffee-drinker’s groan :
Thirty-seven North Melbourne hotels are doomed. A
special Arbitration Court is now sitting to decide which
37 will have to close. And when I come to think of the
barbarism of the average suburban “pub,” I don’t feel
sorry. One public-house is typical of present-day Philis-
tinism. In Paris you go and sit down in a cafe and rest
your back; but in this heathenish country you lean
against a bar and compress your breast-bone. As a
coffee-drinker, I protest against the hotel because it sells
only intoxicating liquor. When I meet Abraham Jones,
and we both happen to be thirsty, he insists upon whisky
or beer, and I can oqly get one of those flabby drinks,
which taste like a boiled bolster. If Igo into a tea-room
I am urged to eat a stale bun or a granite scone, and I
am not allowed to smoke. In my not-at-all humble
opinion, all the hotel licenses should be cancelled and
new ones issued for cafes, which would have all the
attractions of pub, tea-shop, and oyster-saloon. Most
oyster-saloons are barbarous places, too. They never seem
to be able to get rid of their eternal frowsiness and the
smell of last night’s lobster and to-day’s Greek ; and
usually they are divided into uncomfortable little com-
partments like dog-kennels intended to hold a perfectly
square dog. lam glad those 37 hotels are closing. Some-
times I feel mad enough to close everything.

* * » *
Contrast the punishments awarded by
Hungary and Australia to the adulterator 1
Recent English papers tell of a Hungarian wine-
adulterator who, on conviction, was ordered to be
imprisoned for 20 days, and to pay all costs, in-
cluding the advertising of his punishment in the
principal papers. Per contra, two Melbourne
manufacturers whose “raspberry vinegar” was
shown to consist of “little, if any, genuine rasp-
berry juice, 48.17 per cent, of water, 23.0 per cent,
of sulphurous acid, and a little coloring matter ”
were merely fined 40s. and costs. The man who
makes and sells sulphurous acid (an irritant,
especially dangerous to children) and water as
raspberry vinegar is even more of a rascal than
the spieler who passes brummagem jewellery as
fine gold ; he not only obtains money by a trick
or by false pretences, but lie also comes dan-
gerously close to manslaughter. The Australian
law promptly gaols the jewellery trickster —the
Hungarian law gaols the other swindler likewise.
A Federal Adulteration Act, introduced and
passed by a Kingston Administration, would
probably deal in an adequate fashion with this
villainous game—which is one reason why the
importer and the adulterator throw lies and mud
at that sturdy straight-goer every time.

“ D’Harum ” : If you want to be robbed,
go to a church bazaar or fete. I was fleeced in an
original way t’other night at one of these affairs.
Was talking to some of the ladies, when one
of the church toilers who knew me by sight
came up and said, “Er—excuse me, Mr
Harum, but—er —you can obtain light
refreshments, ices, drinks, etc., at the
pavilion,” and raising his hat he walked
away. Of course I escorted the ladies to
the pavilion, and paid for ices all round.
Ten minutes later I was conversing with
two other ladies, when I was again accosted
with: “Mr. Harum, refreshments may
be obtained at the pavilion.” That meant
refreshments for three. Half an hour later
met two more lady friends, and hadn’t
been with them more than two minutes
when I heard that same voice saying at
my . elbow : “Excuse me, Mr. Harum,”
etc., etc. That meant cool drinks for about
half a dozen. Then I saw ten ladies I knew
in the distance, and I fled. If I had spoken
to them that fiend would have come up and
told me again where the refreshment tent was,
and I would have had to pay for eleven plates of
strawberries and cream.
• * • *
A quaint decision was given in a clerical
divorce case at Wellington (M.L ) when the
petitioner was granted a decree subject
to paying the respondent’s costs and £2 a week
alimony so long as she remained chaste. The co-
respondent, an engineer on a British liner, is to
repay the petitioner everything for which latter is
responsible under the order, always provided the
petitioner can manage to collect the money from the
engineer. Marine engineers are birds of passage.
The Court evidently thought the petitioner to some
extent responsible for his wife’s misconduct, and
so it took the picturesque vengeance of making
him spend his life collecting the respondent’s
alimony from the co-respondent.
* * * *
Maoriland’s whimsical Defence Depart-
ment is in such a state of chaos that its chief,
War Minister Seddon, really does not know where
he is. The latest incident productive of ribald
mirth relates to a volunteer who went to S’Africa,
gained his commission at the muzzle of the blazing
farmhouse, and returned to a billet in the Over-
staffed Defence office at £4 10s. a week. A few
months elapsing without pay, he sent in his little
account. Then there were loud explosions.
Although they were tumbling over him every day
for want of room, nobody seemed to know
officially that he was there ; and now a committee
is trying to discover who appointed him to the
fat billet, or whether he fell in through the roof
giving way when he was on it, or whether he
blew in, or what ?

is a back door way out of this position.”
Hobart’s latest fashionable fad is a
Mothers’ Union. A certain sort of parasitic
mother brings along the baby to be patronised
and pampered by the fashionable faddists. The
show is held beneath the shadow of Holy Church.
The fashionable faddists go in at the front door,
showing their ankles on the steps. The parasitic
mothers go round to the back, and are otherwise
very well-behaved, and meek and greedy. Yis,
mum !

“ Marat ” : Lay down to sleep in Sydney
Centennial Park, the other Saturday, and was
furiously and unwarrantably attacked by the
infantry forces of my native country,
who attempted to storm my position. They
evidently had mistaken me for Kruger, so
I became interested. Big fat men raced
from one tree to another, with their
% heads down to avoid the bullets, which,
* presumably, were showering on them
They dodged behind bulrushes, bits
of fern and dead leaves, and the ground
was watered with their perspiration.
There was a bit of open land in front
of my kopje, and they charged
across that at a great pace, a man
close on 16st., and aged about 60,
leading. Just when they got within
a few yards of me, the bugle
sounded, and they were called off,
and they were mad. Victory was
snatched from them ; another minute
and they would have trodden on my
corn. They formed up on the open
ground. Three officers, who had
been telling funny stories while the
scouts were rushing, kitten-fashion,
at bits of fluff, rode up without
falling off their horses, and the
senior complimented the perspiring
veterans on the spirited manner in
which they had nearly overtaken me.
Then he distributed a few certificates for
marksmanship, remarking : “ The cer-
tificates will not only be of great value to
you in your military career, but also in any
private walk of life in which you might be
engaged.” I yearned for one of them; it
would have been valuable in my walk of life
as a dog-fancier and dealer in birds. One
of the junior officers then took charge, and raced
the veterans up and down the park, changing the
movements in accordance with advice tendered
by the other junior, who observed occasionally :
“ Give ’em some moah of that form foahs, it’s got
’em tied in a beastly knot.” The old man who led
the charge got a certificate. He deserved it. I
never saw a runner’s legs fly out at such an angle
before.

“Back to Rook wood,”
said the shaky veteran in
answer to a question. “The
world’s a divil of a place
now. I left the Home
on me pension, an’ am
just half a year out, an’ I
give it best. The first
month I saved like a miser.
I hung on to me shammy
bag, an’ at night nursed it
in me bosom. I lived on
9d. a day, an’ was count-
ing on building a church
an’ having it called after
me. But 355. was stolen
from me, an I starved the
rest of the time. Next
month I was so exhausted
I took a glass or two.
Blowed if they didn’t run
me in the first day, an’
between the fine an’ some
windies they said I broke,
they copped the bloom-
ing lot. Then there was a
long, long starve, with me
hand out in dark corners
at night. Next month the
police kept me money as
they said for me, an’ I was
to go when I wanted a
bit. I went an* went, but
the man with the money
was never there, an’ divil a
ha’penny of that lot I ever
received. I told it all to a
man who had given me a
thrum many a night when
I was stony, an’ lie said he
would stand by me the
month after. He was there
right enough an’ counted
the money when I took it
out of the bank, an’ as
heaven is above me 1 never
see him since, or the money.
I suppose I went on a regu-
lar tear next month ; any-
ways they say I did. But
I couldn’t have had much fun,
for I only Jad the stuff one day when they
copped ine. When I come to, they said 1
hadn’t a penny, that I had paid it all at the
Hotel Australia for a dinner for me an'
Governor Rawson. I don’t believe them; or,
if I did, it must have been drink on an empty
stomach that made me go with Governor Rawson.
Howaniver, the beak stopped me pay for the next
month ; an’ I give the whole bloomin’ show best.”

BY NIGHT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
So dark it is ! No glimmer of a moon
Where creeping scrub shuts in the grey lagoon.
Is it Love makes you shy ? Or only fear ?
Keep silence, heart, keep silence, lest they hear,
And hush the sudden jangle of the bit,
For on the slope the station lamps are lit.
No greeting word to pass from you to me,
The saddle-leather creaks—let the horse be,
With loose rein hanging he will never stray,
Knowing we journey far before the day.
So dark 1 away from sight the whole world slips ;
But not too dark, oh, Love ! to find your lips 1
Q.
M. FORREST.

«Troubles.
A Labor member once mentioned proudly
to the writer that “in no other country did Par-
liament enter so completely into the daily life of
the people as in N.S.W.” And he was quite
right. In N.S.W., Parliament enters so com-
pletely into the daily life of the people, and con-
sequently wastes so much time arguing about a
single citizen’s 6d. of dirt - money, discussing
whether an “ improver ” was wrongly appointed
at Fitzroy Dock, and is so generally a rouseabout,
dish-walloper, odd-jobber, errand-boy and slushy,
that it has no time to establish a good local
government system, put the finances straight, or
start a workable system of closer settlement in
those parts where closer settlement would really
pay. Now there is a new prospect before it. A lady
who says her character has been maligned, has
circularised the press demanding that she should
be allowed to appear at the bar of the House, and
there indignantly refute the slanders. That lady
has accurately sized up the character of the
present N.S.W. Parliament. It has degenerated
into a gossiping, prying, old-woman, old-cat insti-
tution, and it will find its true level on the day
when it introduces the Bill to Clear the Character
of Mary Jane Jones, who has been accused of
having a black eye and a damaged hat in a public
place ; or the measure to declare that Ann
Billingsgate was not nearly so bad as she was
alleged to be.
• * * *
Melbourne is suffering from a Chinese
strike, and Lilly Buk-stleet, that spacious home
of fan-tan and the Rats’ Push, has been a scene of
wild excitement and police helmets. Until a few
days ago the strikers took things celestially,
playing fan-tan and drawing their strike pay with
peaceful regularity. But with the advent of
Chinese blacklegs from Sydney, in Panama hats
and spiked knuckle-dusters, things livened up.
The first night there was a skirmish in a fan-tan
shop, two policemen were hurt, and a number of
Chinese of both persuasions damaged. On the
release, on bail, of the men arrested, a wildly-ex-
cited mob, led by a big buck Chinaman armed
with a large hammer, gave hot chase. The air
for a while was full of curses, chisels, dumb-
bells, and damaged Mongolian. Each man’s
baggy sleeve was a small arsenal. The employers
are not too anxious for martyrdom, and stop in of
nights. The Chinese striker seems to be an alto-
gether different person from the vegetable Mon-
golian, who would sooner run than fight or pay
market-dues any day. In fact, the legend about
the peaceful, law-abiding character of the heathen
has been killed dead. The new kind of China-
man, with his yell and his knuckle-duster, turned
Lilly Buck-stleet into a scene that suggei 'led the
storming of a Chinese city, and heaps of infuriated
and blood-stained Asiatic rioters made the locality
a small Sheol. They had no respect for the rights
of property, and burst into each other’s houses and
tried to saw each other’s heads off quite promiscu-
ously.
N.S.W. Municipalities Act is a strange
animal. If a cleaner council than usual wants to
remove the dead cat, and the venerable scrag-end
of corned beef, and the decayed vegetable from the
ratepayer’s back-yard, it can’t unless the rate-
payer consents. It can make a contract with the
ratepayer, but it can’t strike a rate on all rate-
payers. An attempt has been made here and

there to get round the Act by increasing the sani-
tary rate to a size large enough to allow of the dead-
cat being removed as well as that other part of
the noisome household refuse with which the
Councils are empowered to deal; but even this
attempt has failed. The other day the Broken
Hill council, which has been trying to keep the
dry town reasonably clean, sued for the recovery
of fees based on the latter idea ; and failed on the
ground that it couldn’t prove that the amount
claimed was the amount actually expended on the
particular premises in question.

One of Australia’s very own kids got into
the Port Fairy (Vic.) express the other day under
the belief that it stopped at Footscray. When
the train whizzed through the station the boy
looked glum, and apparently realised that it
would be something of an undertaking to get
back home in time for bed if he had to walk from
Laverton, the first stopping place. So he made
up his small mind ; and as the express neared
Yarraville at a speed of about 40 miles an hour,
he opened the door of the carriage, and at the
psychological moment, grasping the bottle of
medicine he was carrying firmly in his right
hand, he jumped. He turned something short of
300 somersaults, catharine-wheels, and hand-
springs along the platform, and in the end landed
in a heap. He lay for about two seconds wonder-
ing what had fallen on him, then suddenly remem-
bered, got up, shook himself together, and started
for home with his bottle unbroken. *The station-
master captured him,, however, and had him
examined for breaks, but nothing was out of
order, and the boy was evidently surprised at a
fuss being made about such a trivial matter.

Victorian Full Court has reversed Justice
A’Beckett’s decision in the Martell case. Martell
sought damages from five delegates of the Outtrim
branch of the Victorian Coal Miners’ Union for
loss of his employment, they having intimated to
his employers that they (the Union miners)
would not work alongside of him. Under threat
of a strike Martell was dismissed. A’Beekett’s
decision in the miners’ favor was reversed by
Justices Madden, Hood and Hodges on the
ground, briefly, that the men “combined to hurt
another in his trade or business, without just
cause or excuse.” All three judges held that
“the defendants undoubtedly did combine. They
also clearly intended to damage plaintiff in his
trade . . . They were not seeking any direct
benefit for themselves.” For these reasons
Martell is awarded <£loo damages. Yet to
common sense it seems that here the primary
Judge was right and the Full Court wrong. The
miners combined long before Martell was heard
of, therefore they didn’t combine expressly to
injure him. The action they took with regard to
him—refusing to work with him—was so little
intended to injure him that when the Employers’
Federation found him a better billet as steward of
their club rooms there was no hint of any strike,
though the Outtrim mine-owners are members of
that Federation and so are still Martell’s em-
ployers. The miners’ action was dictated solely
by the instinct of self-defence. Martell had gone
against unionism once before—what time he was
Eldorado correspondent of the Argus— and had
advised the acceptance of reduced wages by the
gold miners. He was an enemy of these Outtrim
miners economically, as of unionism generally, and
the miners were merely defending their interests
and their principles in declining to work with
him. And surely a man has a right to refuse to
work beside another for a valid reason.
The following appeared in S.A. Advertiser
(H/11/’03):
Port Lincoln occupies a unique position in having the
greatest number of houses where there are no children
among towns of similar size in the State. Out of 104
houses, 70 do not send , children to school. Amongst the
Government officials only one has children. The gaol-
keeper, police officer, and postal officials are all childless.
This place appears to be the one spot where people settle
without “ encumbrances.”
A few years ago the Port Lincoln oyster beds also
gave out, on account of the pa and ma oysters
having no children.
The big Henty will case, which has
engaged the Melbourne courts on and off for
years past, and threatens to crop up again, has
swallowed many thousands of the Henty money,
and there are one or two juniors who have prac-
tically lived upon it for years by virtue of small
fees earned in the modest privacy of the taxation
master’s office. One great thing about being a
lawyer is that you get paid handsomely for pre-
senting another lawyer’s little bill, and the more
you get for the other chap the more you make for
yourself.

THE BEST MAN.
A meeting of ladies was held yesterday in N. Melb. to
further the candidature of Mr. Malcolm M‘Eacharn. Mr.
M'Eacharn made a few remarks, after which Mrs.
M'Eacharn spoke exhorting those present to use the
franchise. “They should decide who was the best man,
and then do all they could to secure his return.” — AGE.
This paper reckoned all along that some-
body would get hurt through giving way too much
to that tired feeling, and last week two Victorian
young women did it. One, at Mildura, dislocated
both jaws while yawning, while another, at
Katamatite, opened her mouth so widely, also
while yawning, that it stopped that way. This
latter damsel would make a lovely model of
Australia Facing the Yawn.
TO ONE I LOATHE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Your cart-horse tread perdition is to me,
When with your hatchet-face you hover round ;
What little spicy hours of ecstasy
I forfeit—just because you aren’t drowned.
So sure am I you never will atone,
I’ll hazard this : That, when I am no more,
My Dolly’s ma will have upon the stone
The parting shot—“ Not lost, but gone before.”
VIC.
WILL J.
A SONG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Why art thou laughing so careless and free ?
Rollicking Wind.
Carest thou not for the sorrow in me ?
Frivolous Wind.
Knowest thou not that my love’s on the sea,
Out to the westward he sails far from me ?
Mock not with laughter my soul's misery,
Unpitying Wind!
Why art thou sighing when all else is glee ?
Saddening Wind.
Knowest thou not that my love’s come to me ?
Sorrowing Wind.
Hush thy low moaning for these days should be
Season of gladness and sweet melody ;
Crown with thy laughter my soul’s ecstasy,
O, Wayward Wind!
N.s.w.
JOHN PHILIP COOK
OLD GIRL OF MINE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
One year ago, old flame of mine,
I ever thought those eyes of thine
Outshone the stars of even ;
And fondly thought the Lord above
A fairer maid, a purer love,
Had ne’er to mortal given.
That flame was last year’s flame, old girl,
This year has sent it all a-whirl—
Spread far and wide its ashes.
To-day I have another lass ;
And you have now another ass,
Charmed by your bright eye flashes.
No doubt he feels the same as I
Felt, when last year was passing by—
He could not live without you ;
To lose your love would break his heart,
The skies grow dim when you two part,
As in last year they used to.
And yet his love, the same as mine,
May in the dawning year decline —
To love and leave is human.
And rare, alas ! is human love,
The gem that travels from above
To breast of man or woman.
W. WATSON.
