THE VARNISHED BOARD
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
. „ Sydney, November 24, 1903.
Mr Dear Arini,—
Only the person who in a previous state of
existence had been a horse wenttoA.J.C. races
on Saturday. And as very few persons were ever
anything so fine as a horse in a previous state,
there were not more than a couple of hundred
visitors at Randwick course for the 30,000 or so
who were at the cricket match. People who can
sit for a whole afternoon watching a cricket
match must at one time have been vegetables.
The only interesting cricket match ever
played was that at Molonga, where they
used a paling for a bat, and M‘Swet’s dog
fielded and ran away with the ball, his master
taking runs till he dropped from exhaustion,
whilst the rival team chased the dog. A lot of
serviceable linen dresses got off the grass at Satur-
day’s races—that is, they cleared the grass. There
were also a few beautiful voiles, tucked and
guipured, but nothing that would require special
constables to guard it from being copied. You
know those belts with the two little bob-tails at
the back? Well, one lady had a bright green
satin zone and its two tails were run through
teething-rings of white bone.
But the Cricket Ground was a blaze of splendor.
There fashion—unable to walk abroad because
the 30,000 packed the available space so tightly—
stood up while the players sprawled on the grass
waiting for the new batsmen, to have their frock-
ing and head embellishments admired. They did
it so obviously. They posed with a deliberation
which was sublime in its whole-souledness, and
changed their poses so that the last sartorial
detail should not be overlooked by an envious and
admiring audience. When the new batsmen took
their places, the exhibitions sat down with sighs.
Cricket may not be interesting, but Sydney
Cricket Ground is a delightful place to spend an
afternoon. On Saturday there was a plea-
sant breeze, the band discoursed sweet music, and
the shaven lawn, ringed round with the greener
bicycle track, dotted over with the white-clad
players, was picturesque. Then behind were the
stands filled with a mass of women. Blue pre-
dominated, but there were a number of amber and
sherry-tinted diapbanousmaterials, which billowed
up like fleecy gold ; they were mostly worn by
women with dark hair, and looked effective, but
Chinese-like. The grotesque element was sup-
plied by the figure of one of the umpires, a little
man who wears a white slouch hat, a white coat
and black trousers. He is like the dwarf in an
Aubrey Beardsley picture. By the way, all the
players, in the glare of the sunshine and in con-
trast to their white costumes, seem to have black
faces, and it is hard to believe that they are not
negroes.
From a correspondent who has thought it out:
If women would only realise that every color possesses
its individual characteristics, and that to wear it success-
fully you must possess these ! As it is, they hazard for
effect and gain conspicuousness. It cannot be hard to
realise that pink requires piquancy ; never languish in
this tint— in spite of the old novelists. Wear blue (the
light shades of sky and flowers—not the dark, beloved of
athletes) only if you have eyes to match, and a manner
not blas6. You must be innocent, wistful, young, to
wear those delicate shades of green, most fashionable
among those who are quite the reverse. Cream requires
a lazy, indolent personality. When suffering from “ that
tired feeling" crawl into a frock of this description, and
you can languish becomingly; for what Philistine
would expect energy of a person garbed in cream?
For the wearing of white you must have.animation,
to save you from mediocrity, otherwise you dwindle to
the washed-out unnoticeab'le, familiar of the summer.
Beware of black—it is the unconscious bane of the dowdy,
and the sanctuary of the smart. You need a figure to wear
it —not a clothes frame —and a neatness that is not
apparent. There are so many things necessary, such a
multitude to avoid, that only one in fifty survives
the ordeal. Black and white, wherever it goes,
whatever it covers, always conveys an air of respecta-
bility not always genuine. Approach yellow and
its tints warily. If your eyes are clear, your skin
healthy, your spirits frivolous, slip into your gown of
maize, and go forth to conquer. There is one color for
those alone whose dark eyes and complexions are born
of the South, whose ways are tinged with languor, and (if
possible) shyness—amethyst, or as it is hideously called,
heliotrope. Its delicate, dainty shades are most fascina-
ting of all. Perhaps in the future woman will see the
wisdom of wearing a color of-- her own individuality, and
will triumph accordingly.
Marquess of Donegal!, who is in his 82nd year,
and to whom, as the cable informed us, a
son was born some weeks ago, had been twice
previously married. His first marriage was an-
nulled, and in 1865 the Marquess married again—a
lady who died nearly three years ago. The
intrepid noble, who was very anxious for an heir,
married at the beginning of the present year a
beautiful Canadian girl of 22.
‘ The first issue of the Monthly Molar and
Australian Hollow Tooth is to hand from
its editor and proprietor, Mr. Charles Hall,
of North Sydney. I may mention that I have re-
christened the scarlet-black-and-gold booklet. Its
real name is the Commonwealth Dental Review.
And it is a well-written bantling—“everything of
genuine interest to the profession will find a
place in these pages.” There are capital sum-
maries of two papers read at the Dental Hos-
pital of Sydney by Dr. Richard Arthur, M.D.,
and Dr. Horace P. Godfrey ; also, a review of the
good work done by the hospital, which was opened
so late as October, 1902. Since then if has treated
8410 cases, and like the Rajah of Bjiong is still
going strong. A “Personal Column” lends
human interest to the Review. Certain dental
gentlemen, specified, are either visiting Sydney
or getting married, or have become chuckling
fathers.
Saw Miss Nellie Stewart, on a day last week, in
a black sun-ray-pleated voile skirt and blouse
with black pelerine, fringed, the pointed back of
whioh was held into the waist by a belt under a
cut steel clasp, quite half a foot in height. Never
saw anyone else in fashion-plate or flesh with such
a buckle, or with a pelerine so caged. Suspended
from her waist by a long gold chain was a small
golden notebook. It was very smart and un-
common—Nellie is no more afraid of originality
in her dressing than in her playing. She still
looks, even in uncompromising daylight and
without a veil, no older than the youthful heroines
she impersonates on the stage. Make-up has
nothing to do with it either; she does not make-
up for daylight wear. Miss Stewart left for
Melbourne on Sunday night, but will return next
week to proceed to America.
An interview with George Rignold is in last
issue of the attractive little booklet, “The
Players,” published in Sydney. Rignold’s house,
called Braybrook in honor of his late wife, is at
Middle Harbor, and to get there you go by boat
to Mosman’s, then take two trams, one after the
other, of course, to the Spit road. Then you take
a boat and row up the stream, and finally climb a
lofty hill, on the top of which is perched Bray-
brook. “ Henry Vee ” lives there with an Italian
servant, whom ne calls Balbus, not because the
man is bald, but because he is building a wall
round the villa.
Edwin Geach, now in Maoriland, is said to be
m treaty with Mr. F. B. Dunne (“Mr. Dooley,”
the American humorist and philosopher) for a
lecturing tour in Australia. It is to be hoped the
treaty will get as far as signature. Also to be
hoped, if he comes, Dunne will not lecture in
Anglo-Saxon, * but English-Irish. But perhaps,
though, he can no more speak the Dooley dialect,
which he writes so delightfully, than one of the
Cranbrook A.D.C’s. If Mr. Geach secures his
Dooley man he ought to have quite as successful a
tour with his prize as he had with Donald Mac-
donald.
Mr. Yes-No Reid, at his Paddington meeting,
had to appeal for the removal of a woman voter
who showed her ill-feeling towards the great
wriggler by frequent interjections. It becomes
more clear every day that the woman policeman is
the necessary corollary of the woman voter. Public
opinion won’t tolerate the removal of the interrupt-
ing lady by a male authority, and even if it did he
would probably try to remove her so mildly, in
order not to arouse the hostility of the meeting,
that he would meet with little success. On the
other hand, the police-lady could take her dis-
turbing sister by the back of the blouse and rush
her down the hall at a gallop, with her hair flying
in all directions, without arousing hostility.
“If I do get into the Senate I will not stop
until the convents are opened to inspection, and
the industrial homes have regulated hours, the
same as factories, etc.” Thus a certain Nellie
Martel in a letter to a local “religious” editor.
Being a Fire Worshipper myself, I had
a humorous mind, until I read that letter,
to cast my vote for Nellie. I really wanted to
see her in the Senate, so that I could have some-
thing fresh to laugh at, but now I’m afraid she
would only irritate me. Get a political primer,
Nellie, and learn that the inspection of convents
and regulation of factory hours are not
matters for the Senate at all. It simply could not
legislate on them. The Federal Parliament can
no more open the R.O. institutions to the inspec-
tion of Mrs. Martel, and Dill-Macky, and all the
rev. Orange gentlemen than it can open Protestant
schools, and refuges, and institutions to the visits
of Mrs. Inspectress O’Flaherty and Fathers Duffy
and Mulcahy. It doesn’t worry about such small-
fry jobs. Mrs. Martel might as well promise that
if elected she would have the kerbing and gutter-
ing of Balmain attended to.
A silly season discussion has been going on m
Fogland about the degenerate boarding-school
girl. “She leaves school,” says the writer, “and
tries to boss her father’s house. She bullies her
mother, scares off the old family friends, refuses
to give her mother any assistance, and spends her
time golfing, fencing, cycling, or at clubs. She is
anywhere but at home.” Most probably this
isn’t true, except in rare instances. But the prin-
cipal of a fashionable girls’ boarding-school, not
far from Sydney, is very pessimistic about
the tone of her pupils. When a new girl
arrives, unless she be the daughter of a
moneyed Somebody, whose name is already
known, she is treated with silent contempt
or open insolence by the other girls. There
is a marked absence of generosity and courtesy
towards her, and this treatment continues till she
has made a standing for herself among them—
found herself, so to speak. The newcomers, most
of them, suffer keenly under this form of persecu-
tion, yet, curiously enough, when they have an
assured place among their school-mates, they take
their _ turn at persecuting later arrivals. The
principal says that the outfits with which some of
these girls come to school are most snobbish. A
girl of 12 will have her dressing-table and wash-
stand laden with silver-backed brushes and silver-
backed combs, and sun-burn powder boxes, hair-
pin trays, and pin boxes and jewel caskets of
solid silver. You may imagine the “side” the
silverite puts on to the girl with the celluloid
outfit.
All can tell at least one good story, if only
against themselves, care they to be frank and un-
affected and candid in the telling. This story
from an ingenuous girl correspondent has a naive
simplicity that is charming:—
Last Sunday my best boy was coming to see me. He
belongs to the bank, is a tall, handsome fellow, very
refined and gentlemanly, and has a very keen sense of
humor. We are farming folk, well-to-do, with a good
home and a fine, large house with a wide verandah
running all round. We daughters of the house received
a fairly good education and learned all the necessary
accomplishments of the present-day lady. Pa hails from
Tip’rar.v, and—with all due respect to pa, for he is a good-
natured soul—we find it hard to refine him, or to give
him an idea of the fitness of things. Last Sunday my
heart, naturally, began to go pit-a-pat when I awoke
in the morning and remembered. I hustled the dinner
through, had all the house spick-and-span, a bowl of
roses on the dining tab'.e, various specimen glasses of
flowers dotted round the drawing-room, pot plants and
ferns here and there, and everything just lovely. Then
I dressed myself in my new spring costume—a pastel
blue trimmed with Paris lace and chiffon, and a long,
sweeping train. As I took a long, last survey of myself’
I decided that I looked—well, just as nice as I possibly
could. He was to arrive at four, so, while I waited, I
thought I would practise my violin, and I felt how
interesting I would look, when I responded to his knock
and opened the door, with my violin tucked under
my angel sleeve and that far-away musical look
in my eyes. He could not fail to be impressed.
Pa came in, and sat for a time listening to me play. Then
he got up, took his chair and sat out on the verandah
smoking. I plaj-ed on, for a quarter of an hour or so,
when I heard the gate click. I glided to the window.
Yes ! it was he coming up the path leading his bike, and
looking so manly snd handsome, his face beaming with
amusement. I wondered why he was smiling so, and
following the direction of his gaze, my eyes rested on Pa,
who had got off his chair and was sitting on the floor,
with his head bent—paring his corns 1
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ A politico-social upheaval:—
Usher of the Black Rod to Vic. Assembly got an un-
expected job the other night, when the State Parlia-
ment gave a farewell dinner to Governor Clarke. Two
young men, who didn’t seem to know any other members
of the august assembly, were discovered in their midst —
a pair of uninvited guests punishing the banquet on their
own with unparalleled audacity. No immediate steps
were taken to check their ravages. They ate on and
drank accordingly, whilst the officers of the House made
secret enquiries which cleared the strangers of all sus-
picion of being legislators, aides-de-camp, or press rep-
resentatives. At last they were solemnly and officially
desired to step into another room and explain their sacri-
legious intrusion. They couldn’t explain. Then arose
the. question whether they should be handed over to the
police for obtaining banquet by false pretences, or be
simply turned out. ‘ Jack ’ Murray decided that the
Usher of the Black Rod should conduct them off the
premises in a Parliamentary manner and leave them to
their remorse. It was a proud moment for Black Rod.
He had found an opportunity to serve his country during
the recess.
“The general belief is that Miss Mignonette
Clarke, daughter of the retiring Governor, has
rehearsed her top notes with Marchesi in order to
■ing professionally. She acknowledged so herself
the day of her departure with Melba, and for
encouragement got a pab on the back from the
Diva when that mighty woman was not drying
her copious tears on the shoulders of Toorak’s
doughtiest. It was said at the time that the
Clarices were not able to save up for a rainy day
out of their expenditure allowance, and that the
Srofessional platform was approved of for the
aughter.
“ A few Melbourne yacht-owners are trimming
their sails for a wide cruise these days. Rupert
Clarke has left his stables and his theatre-boxes
to go to Maoriland to see to the finishing polish
of his expensive boat. Young Charlie Wallace,
whose wealth accrues from the John O. Wallace
estate, also prepares for the yachting season.
“ A considerable dame at present looming in
society hereabouts is Mrs. Ross-Soden. It was
this wealthy widow who supplied bountiful
refreshments for three days at the recent Town
Hall bazaar—a large item. She is a pretty
plump little body who has given more in charity
and public philanthropy during the last three
months than the ordinary Lady Bountiful gives
in three years. She was a Tyson, and has settled
down at Manhattan Hall, Toorak, or somewhere
that sounds like that. It was Joseph Clarke’s
residence.
“ Vida Goldstein has had aspersions cast on her
religious and moral opinions. She was forced to
stand up and declare on the platform that she did
not believe in free love, and that whoever said
such a thing lied with malicious intent. She
announces that her views on divorce are stringent,
and she has no sympathy for the facile putting
away of the bonds and the wholesale slackening
of the traces.
“ What a fine plump Governor and staff we’ll
have when the Waxcellency and followers drift
into office again. There’s himself like a fine
trussed table bird. There’s Lieut. Guy, whose
proportions can only be properly comprehended
when he walks abroad in his Scotch regimentals,
and there’s the well-primed and well-waxed,
urbane Selwyn Campbell, whose light grows
strong whenever Mr. Madden comes into power.
What a corpulent staff ! The ordinary aide is a
lean joke. Even the cherubic Browning lost his
dimples in the anxiety of his cloak-carrying job.
“There is, at least, one reverend gentleman
who can see the beam in his own eye. In the
sacred presence of Melbourne Baptist Union, the
other day, Rev. H. Collins, of Fitzroy, asked the
weary, old question why people don’t go to
church. ‘ They don’t,’ said the rev. gent., answer-
ing himself, ‘ because the edifice is mostly a dirty,
dreary, and depressing place, conspicuous for its
lack of good taste and culture, and renowned for
the singing of hymns to low, slow tunes, accom-
panied by an organ that has seen better days.
What is there less likely to fix the pious attention
of a congregation than dreary hymns and a
wheezy organ ? ’ asked this pastor with warmth,
and his audience, having recovered itself some-
what, could only feebly echo, * What, indeed ! ’
After all, Pastor Collins might have been a good
deal warmer without spontaneous combustion
arising. Even if the sacred edifice were painted
like the tail of a bird of paradise, the public might
still keep away. The democratic public has an
impression that the church nowadays is a very un-
progressive institution, and while that idea re-
mains even the reddest-painted conventicle with
the greenest front might suffer occasionally from
lack of support.
“Corsets of every shape and variety were in
evidence before Vic. Full Court recently. Not
only the married but also the single members
of the bar disclosed a remarkable knowledge of
the intricacies of the subject, but even Justice
Holroyd, with his birthday gaud fresh upon him,
was not quite sure as to the meaning of ‘ pair ’ in
connection with stays, and there was considerable
debate as to whether or not 70,000 pairs would
supply 140,000 ladies 1
“All ears are strained to catch the thrilling
accounts of a theatrical fracas. The rumor runs
that two bosom pals, twin souls, have had a falling-
out. The man hails from a hot clime up North.
Yes, of course it was about a man. At the recent
races, each fair one tossed her head whenever the
tide of the throng bore along the figure of the
other.
“ The belt fad becomes a joke. The latest
adopted by the advanced frocker reaches a point
up between the shoulder-blades. The next will
pot-hook on to the boned collar. After that the
belt will probably extend right down to the feet
and be a frock. The Vandycked is the approved
shape. The figure gradually goes back to the
hour-glass shape. The straight front never
pleased, though fashion kept it going for a season.
Now the original lines are being recognised. The
fashionable figure owns a waist, a gradual slope
and rise. The hips are not so protuberant. Those
who struggled with the pneumatic pad may get a
rest from its wicked vagaries.
“Melb. Paunch has been busy the last few
weeks pulling up the blinds and opening the
windows in the homes of Vic.’s Noblest and Best.
It is touching to note the simplicity with which
our aristocrats live. I used to imagine them at
meal times sitting down to a table ‘groaning
under the weight of the viands’—capons,
asparagus, pork sausages, bullocks’ hearts, save-
loys, and cream cakes, and all that, while tall,
gorgeous footmen in dazzling liveries flittered
hither and thither in the landscape bearing big
bottles of champagne. But, according to Paunch,
this is incorrect. No groaning boards—no-
flights of flunkeys—no viands worth mention-
ing—only sausage and mash and suet dumplings.
So simple and unassuming is it all, one can almost
imagine S’Malkum M'Eacharn passing the mutton,
cutlets to his missis, or Sammy Gillott helping his
mother-in-law to a slice of German sausage. Mrs.
M'Eacharn tells the interviewer, ‘ldo my own
correspondence.’ No staff of secretaries!—no.
team of typewriters —but Milady, in her sim-
plicity and morning-wrapper, inking her fingers
to write and tell the poulterer on no account to.
forget the fowl. I am awaiting, with feverish
anxiety, the interview with Mr. Robert Reid.
Think how interesting that will be—to know
what he does in his home-time! We have found
out what he does during business hours.
“Nurses, like doctors, are in honor bound to
secrecy, but a fair pillow-patter, who is about to
fly her post, threatens to issue a book—‘The
Confessions of a Nurse.’ ‘Does passion-fruit
really cause appendicitis?’ asked an innocent,
recently, of this nurse. ‘Yes,’ she. replied, with
a Sphinxy smile, ‘appendicitis is very often
passion fruit.’
“ The fortune-teller vanishes and gives place to
the palmist and the she-phrenologist. Their
‘ads.’ read whimsically, since massage is thrown
in with the character delineation, so that at one
sitting the bumps are deciphered, the horoscope
readjusted, and the wrinkles ‘of time and
trouble erased by the modern prophetess. But
the majority of gullible females are not likely
to relish massage in the dingy parlor where
mysterious charts blot out the wall and
a charnel odor prevails. Those who favor
massage like it in a rose-scented atmosphere,
where sponge and soap are handy. Most of the
silly clients of the palmists have the good sense to
fumigate their hands after the clawing.
“ Two of the most flourishing florists in the city
are men with decorative tastes and a great femi-
nine following. Well, why not? If women are
going to sit in Parliament, why shouldn’t tasty
and harmless young men design bouquets and
wire up forget-me-nots?
Yours affectionately,

A sewerage scheme has been approved of
for Parramatta (N.S.W.), the frowsily picturesque
old town on the side of the smelly creek which
the Government defiles with much sewage; and
the inhabitants, who have lived among smells and
mouldy historic associations and fusty memories
for these many years patiently, took heart and
reckoned that their borough—which ought to be
one of tbe most beautiful in the world—was at
last to be made clean. But
The question has been under the consideration of the
Cabinet, and the project is approved of, but like so many
undertakings at the present time it is hung up owing to
the lack of funds. Mr. O’Sullivan stated on Saturday
that when sufficient loan money was available he would
proceed with the work.
So Parramatta must fester in its dirt a while
longer. Australia has got down to the point at
which it can’t be clean without borrowed money.
The Launceston Examiner has a quaint
Freetrade idea of its own, which it uses at all
times and seasons, —
The Protectionists, on the other hand, demand high
duties to check the import trade. Their notion of trade
is one-sided, for while they are eager enough to secure
markets abroad for Australian products, they say, in
effect, that it is injurious to receive goods in exchange,
as it injures the home producer. We are not prepared to
demonstrate how our exports are to be paid for, unless
by imports or gold—and we have more of the latter than
we can use as a medium of exchange—we leave that for
Protective friends to explain.
The “we” of Launceston Examiner actually
can’t see a small thing like that. The idea that
Tasmania’s exports might be paid for by the
return of its IO U —that the value of them would
all be required for many years to clear off the
island’s foreign debt—is quite outside its know-
ledge.
Chamberlain put the issue straightly to
the Scotch workmen at Greenock :
If you allow Freetrade to go on what will follow ? If
these foreign goods come in cheaper, you will either have
to give up the conditions you have gained—either abolish
and repeal the Fair Wages Clause, your Factory Acts, and
the Compensation to Workmen Acts; either take lower
wages or lose your work. You cannot keep your work at
this higher standard of living and pay, if at the same time
you allow foreigners at a lower standard and lower rate
of pay to send their goods freely in competition with
yours. . . . What about the working men f What about
the class that depends upon having work in order to earn
wages or subsistence at all ? They cannot do without the
work, and yet the work will go if it is not produced in
this country. This is the state of things against which I
am protesting.
An English court has wisely committed
a faith-healer for trial, and, most unwisely, has
allowed him bail. This ruffian's little girl broke
her shoulder, and spent about a fortnight in
agony while she was humped around from one
chapel to another to be prayed over. The sect
also cabled to Dowie to pray for her. The results
were nil, but though the fact was sufficiently
evidenced by the protrusion of the broken bone,
the callous crowd of frauds simply went on
praj’ing. In the end an unconverted policeman
had the father arrested for cruelty, and then the
child was attended to. Most faith-healers exhibit
this same tendency to practise their faith on some
one else. The world is full of large, robust
healers who do things like the above to theii
children, yet who get medical aid for themselves
on very slight provocation. An individual of
that sort should have certainly been allowed to
get out of gaol by faith. Bail is a worldly
contrivance.


NEURALGIA.
If you have Neuralgia, that’s your fault. You can blame nobody but yourself when you know
positively that Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People have cured the very worst cases of Neuralgia in all
Australia. There's no doubt about that. You can find cases in your own town—among your neighbors—
among those whose word you would accept as readily as their bond. Here, for instance, is proof from Sydney :
«I suffered from nagging Neuralgia for 11 years,” says Mrs. Mary Wiseman, 27 Hartley-street, Bald Rock, Balmain,
Sydney. “My whole health was wretched. Some days I was too bilious to hold my head up. But the nerves in my
face and head were always jumping and twitching, and the continual pain had me completely worn out. Many and
many a night I walked the floor trying to ease the hot, fierce pain that shot up my head at the back of my neck. Unless
you suffered like I did, you’d never think that Neuralgia could be so agonising. I went to the doctor, but the medicine
he gave me only eased me a little, and soon I was worse than ever. Then I saw that Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale
People, by actually making new blood, soothed and steadied the nerves, and so cured Neuralgia. I got them at
Pattinson’s the chemist’s, and at the end of a week I felt better. Gradually the tender soreness left my face and head,
and the new blood began to strengthen me. I got a splendid appetite and slept like a top. After a while the Neuralgia
stopped altogether. My spirits rose, and I felt a very different woman. I could not feel better than Ido now, and I
can’t understand why people should go on suffering from Neuralgia or general ill-health when they know they have a
sure and certain cure in Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People.”

PERSONAL ITEMS.
There were 13 new solicitors admitted in
N.S.W. last Saturday in this devil’s year 1903 ;
and may Heaven have mercy upon them.
Warner, the English captain, is a barrister
who at one time “ devilled ” for Alfred Lyttelton,
Chamberlain’s successor at the Colonial Office.
Mat Larkin, once o,f the South Melbourne
Building Society, has re-opened business as a
house, land and estate agent in South Melbourne.
Glasgow Evening News :
Does the athletic record of Mr. Alfred Lyttelton really
justify his appointment as Colonial Secretary ? Black-
ham was a better wicket-keeper.
Dick Seddon now holds the belt for longest
continuous term years) as Premier in Austra-
lasia. Till a couple of months ago Forrest led.
Kingston holds third place for long continuance.
Mrs. Sarah Elizabeth Harran, who died
the other day at Fitzroy (Vic.), aged 89, arrived
in Sydney 86 years ago, her father, T. Thatcher,
being botanist on the staff of Governor Mac-
quarie.
The father of the late Count Rossi was
appointed police magistrate in Sydney in 1825.
He was generally credited with having been one
of the “engineers” for George TV., “the first
gentleman in Europe,” in the trial against Queen
Caroline.
The late Charles Nicholson took part in
the separation of Victoria from N.S.W. and in
the separation of Queensland from N.S.W., and
twelve years ago accepted the office of London
representative of the Central Queensland Separa-
tion League.
It is alleged that Insolvency Commissioner
Russell (S.A.) was decorated with the Imperial
Service Order as a solatium for not being selected
to succeed Judge Bundey. Russell isn’t so young
as he was, and at his age a glass bead is a poor
substitute for a good billet.
Major J. F. Thomas, of Tenterfield,
demands, by newspaper notice, apologies from
those who wrote congratulating him on winning
the Glen Innes election. It was another Thomas
who formed the tail of Carruthers’ squalid
battalion, and this Thomas isn’t going to be mis-
taken for that Thomas with impunity.
Donald Macdonald tells the electors of
Corio. (Vic.) that he was baptised in the Pres-
byterian Church, was subsequently, under the
influence of relatives, connected with the Angli-
can and the Roman Catholic Churches, and that he
is entirely free from sectarian bias. Mac. in fact
appeals on the ground that he has inspected a
whole lot of creeds, and found them all equally
unsatisfactory.
THE IMPROVING YEARS.
“ Men don’t marry so young as they used to.” “ No, the world is getting wiser every
day—it is only a question of time when we won’t marry at all.”

Importers’ ticket in N.S.W., is the son of a
Chartist. It doesn’t seein, however, that Gray’s
father is much use to N.S.W. If the son of young
Gray’s father were a little more like the father of
old Gray’s son the situation might be different.

public-spirited North Coast (N.S.W.) doctor,
whose name disappeared from the list of J’s.P.
because, it was alleged, he was a pro-Boer, has
been re-gazetted now that it is proved he was
the sane man and the general public was the
lunatic.

R. H. Solly, one of the Victorian Senate
Labor candidates, was to have delivered an address
in Rush worth recently from the band rotunda, but
was prevented from doing so by a hostile bands-
man who insisted on his right to use the place for
practice. Solly spoke in the open, standing on a
water trough.
The Victorian Labor candidates for the
Senate are stumping the State under disadvan-
tages —principally through lack of funds. “ Open
air” meetings have been the order of the night—
while battler Barker has had a broken arm to
nurse during his discourses on the aims of the
Commonwealth Labor party,
The first woman candidate for the Repre-
sentatives is Miss Selina Anderson, who opposes
Yellow Pup Wilks in Dailey (Sydney) electorate.
And as anybody is better than the pitiful poli-
tician who tries to get himself a billet in the name
of God and religion, the public had better vote
enthusiastically for Miss Anderson if no stronger
anti-Pup candidate appears.
Nearly passed out recently, as result of
pneumonia, the popular Melbourne press veteran,
“Alf.” Wilson, who was doing work for metro-
politan and country papers what time some of the
present day Melbourne-editors were in swaddling
clothes. The unassuming Alf. lias probably a
bigger store of reminiscences than any other Mel-
bourne scribe, but these are lost to the world
through his habit of sticking closely to the busi-
ness of the passing day.

mitted as barristers —Hughes, of the Reps., and

Lands Minister), of N.S.W. State Assembly.
And W. M. Daley, also of the State Assembly,
starts as a solicitor. N.S.W. State Labor party
had one solicitor in it already—Sullivan, returned
at last elections. It seems in danger of having
too many solicitors.
One Victorian pressman, who strayed into
the House of Rich Landlords during the Factories
Bill wrangle, was so greatly struck with, the
patriarchal appearance of C. J. Ham that he went
round making kindly inquiries after other mem-
bers of the family. Ham must have been rather
astonished next day when he received a letter
asking what had become of his brothers, Shem
and Japhet ; also whether Noah, his father, was
enjoying a ripe old age.
“Cheetah,” from a place of sleep :
The advantage of being a hero of some kind is seen in
Corio electorate, where Donald Macdonald is seeking to
wrest the seat from Crouch in the Aequs interest. There
is a third nondescript in the field, but he doesn’t count
just now. Mac. has ladies on his committee, and ladies
canvassing all and sundry, and his meetings are full of
ladies. They call him “Dr.” Macdonald, having heard
vaguely that he helped to relieve Lady Smith, who,
presumably, was ill. Donald seems to know about as
little of Federal politics as he does of Hittite swear
language, and his views on White Australia are as clear
as his ideas on the origin of the Bunyip ; hut the Boer
war racket may carry him through.
A Walcha (N.S.W.) old-age pensioner
wrote, the other day, to the local registrar saying
that his health had improved so much that he felt
he could now earn his own living. “I do not wish
to be a burden on the State, and I herewith sur-
render my certificate,” he wrote. N.S. Wales is
still waiting to hear from that pensioner who gets
about 10 times more than the Walcha old man has
been drawing, and who has recovered his health
sufficiently to carry on a bustling business in the
city, and between times to make nasty remarks
about the rapacity of the working man.
A legal man writes from Brisbane :
A quaint trouble is vexing some people up North.
Justice Cooper having been appointed Chief Justice, Real
becomes Senior Puisne Judge. But Justice Chubb, of
the Northern Court, is, by a few months, Real’s senior.
So long, however, as Chubb stays in the North, though he
is the Puisne Judge who is Senior to Real, he can’t be
Senior Puisne Judge. But if he gets down to Brisbane it
isn’t very certain what will happen ; for, though one law
says that a Northern Judge can’t be Senior Puisne Judge,
another says that a Northern Judge shall have “ and
shall be deemed to have always had” the same “juris-
diction, powers and authorities ”as any other Judge. To
get out of the tangle it is proposed that a Bill should be
put through making Real Senior Puisne Judge—which he
is already, so long as Chubb is kept up North. And
Chubb’s friends don’t know what to do ; for if the Bill
isn’t passed, then Chubb, who wants a shift South, will
probably be kept up North, and if it is carried then
Chubb will have to sit as Junior to the Judge who was
appointed after him, and i adi man will be both Junior
and Senior to the other mau.
Narrated by the Yahram Chronicle :
Soon after her arrival in Melbourne Miss Ada Crossley
arranged to sing at her mother’s home, and her brothers
and sisters had assembled in a room with her for the
purpose. Mrs. Crossley, who is an invalid, was wheeled
into the room in a chair, and the sight of her was too
much for Miss Crossley, who hurriedly retreated from the
room to give vent to her feelings. On several occasions
since she has essayed the task, but every attempt has
been a failure.
Perhaps if the old lady would pay 10s. admission
at the door, it would give a familiar air to the
proceedings and restore the great contralto’s
presence of mind.
A “PUSH" SOCIETY INTRODUCTION.
CIGARETTE CHARLIE: “My tart!—A bloke I know.”
Mr. G. W. F. Addison, just deceased
was the last of the old-time N.S.W. Gold Com-
missioners, his ancient colleagues, Whittingdal
Johnson and O’Malley Clarke, having passed away
a few years ago. Mr. Addison entered the public
service in 1857, Mr. Johnson in 1849, and Mr.
Clarke in 1854. There is but one N.S.W.
stipendiary on pension now, but a couple of
others are on leave preparatory to retiring.
Arthur Griffith, now a candidate for the
Senate, has resigned the secretaryship of N.S.W.
State Parliamentary Labor party after nearly ten
years’ service. Griffith went into Parliament
with the second batch of Laborites in 1894. There
were 14 besides him, and of them only Cann,
Macdonald and M‘Gowen are now in the House
and the party. Of the original 35 who were swept
in in 1891 only Cann and M‘Gowen have been
Laborites right through to the present. Neilsen,
who came in in 1898, on Mackay going to the
Legislative Council, succeeds Griffith as secretary.
“ G.H.” about a colored brother :
A West African negro, educated at Cambridge, and
intended originally to assist in spreading the Light, runs
a midnight pie-and-coffee stall in Sydney. The pie-
spreader’s vocabulary is ornate, for the educated savage
loves gaudy words even as the plain ditto likes furious-
tinted raiment; and occasionally his florid language
displeases customers. A bluejacket lurched into the
stall one night, and demanded something to eat in a loud
tone of Beer. Sambo nodded ; and, reaching for the
coffee-pot, said : “ Yes, sar, in precisely five seconds I
shall set before yoh such a feast as yoh will ever look
back upon in reminiscential delight.” The elaborate
phraseology angered the tar, who suspected a hidden
insult; and, heaving a chair at the University man, he
yelled : ‘‘Take thet! yer black, grinning grampus, and
see how you’ll ruminiscentilate ” —bash —bang—blanky—
blank ! Sambo carries less linguistic sail nowadays.
The Bulletin receivea this remarkable
contribution :
Wm. May, who died the other day at Sandford (Tas.),
aged 87, was Speaker of the House of Commons when
Disraeli made his maiden speech. He emigrated to S.A.
in ’39, and to the Flyspeck in ’7i, settling down to farm-
ing in the latter place.
Has Tasmania been getting enlarged head over
the idea that an ex-Speaker of the Commons was
growing cabbages in its midst? There was a famous
Erskine May who was clerk of the Commons, but
nobody of that name was Speaker either when
Disraeli entered Parliament or since. And the
ancient agriculturist must have been a boy of 21
when Disraeli made his first speech, and 23 when
he emigrated to S.A.
“ War E.” on “ Scotty the Wrinkler ” :
Beyond a doubt Phil Moubray’s inordinate love for a
dog or a cat hastened his end—he died under an opera-
tion for the removal of a hydatid cyst. As a raconteur
the “ Wrinkler” had few equals, and for hours by the
camp fire he could spin yarns drawn from every phase of
life in every continent. Once remarking on his possession
of a marvellous memory, he replied : “ It’s my curse ; I
wish I could forget everything.” From this and other
remarks I am inclined to think that Moubray, despite his
panegyrics on the bush and its freedom, often reflected
with sadness on a lost career. Scotty was a cynic, and
his pet aversion was the canting hypocrite and humbug.
As a democrat he has been accused of toadying to titles.
Wrong. Scotty was not the shallow-pated democrat who
refuses to see any good in a man who has had the mis-
fortune to be born into the “purple.” To the poor
Moubray was a practical friend, and many a one will miss
his timely aid. He had his weaknesses and failings.
Who hasn’t? But they only affected himself. His
peculiar pen-name, “ Scotty the Wrinkler,” was not ori-
ginal. Hearing an old Scotch shepherd on the ’Bidgee
who posed as an encyclopaedia of “wrinkles,” so desig-
nated, Moubray purchased the copyright of the name
there and then. A little mound of earth in the
Narandera cemetery now marks the remains of Philip
Moubrav, Imperial officer, Royal engineer, surveyor in
Abyssinia, deck hand on the Mississippi,tutor, journalist,
draughtsman, whaler, cook, rouseabout—in fact, every-
thing but a member of Parliament.
• Ex-Speaker Norton, of Queensland,
now dozing out his days in the Leg. Coun-
cil, has probably the State’s finest stock of
reminiscences. Born near Sydney just on
70 years ago, he was one of the very few
buslnnen of that time, or .any other time,
who kept a diary. In 1859-60 (the year
before he overlanded to Queensland to
finally settle there) he went out past
where Bourke now is, with one mate;
Dubbo was then the limit of settlement
in that direction, until the river coun-
try was reached. Afterwards, young Nor-
ton rode in to meet the first mail that was
delivered at Walgett; he remembers that
he had a 400-mile horseback ride for one
etter. Mr. Norton has a valuable store
of old letters, too, including some (copies)
describing the journey of the first free
family that travelled from Sydney to
Bathurst.
Even Max Hirsch's warmest ad-
mirers admit that the fussy, little, dark
satellite of Flinders Lane, in leaving Vic.
State Parliament to reach for a Federal
seat, is clawing at the shadow, with every
chance of losing the substance. Max
makes little headway with the masses.
The excitable little man, who dances with
frenzy and pours forth torrents of garbled
figures about Freetrade, to an agricultural
audience is a bore. His constant rain
of decimals sends the farmers to sleep.
The first things that a now pro-
minent Australian politician did when he
saw office ahead were to marry the girl and to take
a Presbyterian church pew.
t About two Crow-eater Judges :
Almost the only thing in common between the Chief-
Justice and the senior puisne Judge of S. Australia, is that
they are both 70 or thereabouts. Way, the Chief, is short
and has been chubby Boucaut is long and angular.
Way is full of compliments ; Boucaut hates them. Way,
a Methody parson’s son, still patronises tea-meetings and
bun-struggles; Boucaut, son of a sea-captain—well, he
doesn’t. Way got into Parliament one month, was
Attorney-General the next or thereabouts, and climbed
into the Chief’s chair before t'he year was out; Boucaut
was Attorney-General, Minister of Lands, Minister of
Works (twice), Treasurer, and Premier (three times)
before he consented to go up. Way still puts on an
extraordinary amount of frill, and every one of his
thoughts is tied up with 18in. of red-tape : Boucaut
wants to get through his work, and be off home to his
books (he reads all sorts of stuff) and his grandchildren.
A lawyer started the other day with a long opening
speech. “Don’t!” chopped out Boucaut. “Call your
evidence. I don’t want to hear it twice.” Later, when
the young advocate spoke softly—“ Speak up. Think
you’re a bullock-driver swearing at bullocks.” Which re-
calls that, even if a very prominent S. Australian, who
has been Acting-Governor several times, never drove
bullocks, his wife boasts that she used to be able to send
along a team with anybody.
THE LATEST CHAMPION.
Mr. Donald Macdonald, putting up for Corio (Reps.),
professes White Australia convictions, but is confronted
with the piebald sentiments he has contributed to the
Argus. At Drysdale he undertook to state his religious
platform. “.Baptised,", he said, "in the Presbyterian
Church, he had afterwards been attracted to both the
Church of England and the Roman Catholic Church, and
for 20 years had visited them all and assisted all without
favor or choice."

your my master, you White-Black- Protestant-
Homan-Catholic-Presbyterian, you!”
“ G.H.” writes:
Met Tom Mann for the first successive time, the other
day. Bather liked the episode. Mann’s boss ambition
is to grow pumpkins and tomatoes and things—on the
co-operative system ; says 30 himself. Like many others,
and vice versa, he is a better speaker than a writer ; the
first page of his pamphlet, “The Labor Movement in
Both Hemispheres,” starting off with stereoed blather
anent reclaiming the interior Australian arid waste per
the old irrigation cure-all. Might as well try to reclaim
Sahara with a bottle of scent; and what’s the good, any-
way, of “ millions of acres, gloriously fertile ” (vide T.Jlij,
when the millions of inhabitants won’t get born to culti-
vate them, and if they do get born, won’t trouble to put
up a strong enough tariff-fence to keep foreign manufac-
turers from tramping everything flat? Tom Mann, l.y
the way, is at present forbidden to enter Germany,
France or Belgium, on pain of being decorated with the
X of the Legion of Honor or having the Emperor Bill’s'
poem read to him.

Aboriginalities.
“ Myall ” writes :
Notwithstanding general denunciation of backblock
lands for purposes of closer settlement, there is a big
future before that portion of N.S.W. within the artesian
area—or at least there would have been had not nearly
all the land been locked up in improvement leases. In
Walgett district, this year, the crops are magnificent,
and a yield of from 30 to 40 bushels per acre is estimated.
One enterprising cocky expects to garner about 5000
bags, which will be shipped direct to Adelaide. Every
known cereal and fodder plant thrives wonderfully with
the aid of bore water.
This seems less convincing than it might be. The
fact that crops look beautiful in a year of very
respeotable rainfall doesn’t prove much for bore
water. Anyhow, in that same region 70 percent,
of the live stock have perished in five dry years,
and if bore water is going to save the country it
seems to have put up a very poor record. The dry
years are the ones in which the blessing of irriga-
tion should assert itself.
» * * *
The weather banshee still pursues the
demented cockie ! Just as the crops were topping
the fences an equinoctial squall swept northern
Victoria and laid the paddocks flat as bowling
greens. Then the scared cookies rushed in to the
blacksmiths and ordered them to cease scraping
the rust of three seasons past off their strippers,
as they were going to cut with up to-date reapers
and binders, which they sprinted to order on
Eternity bills. Then another storm came along
the reverse way, and lifted the crops up again,
and the implement orders had to be counter-
manded and the rust-scraping resumed.
“Nym.” : A striking example as to the
prolificness of the roach was to be seen in the
Yarra at Dight’s Falls (Abbotsford, Vic.) the
other week, when both sides of the river were
lined with excited people “jagging” for this
pretty little fish. They were in shoals making up
stream to spawn, as they do on the same day in
the second week of October every year. Their
progress being blocked by the falls they' fill the
river to overflowing, and one alleged angler (!)
jagged over 100 fish, weighing up to a pound-and-
a-quarter. Bait is of no use, even if the roach
were inclined to delay their business. The Yarra
roach were not turned into the muddy stream like
most of the other imported fish caught now and
again in the vicinity, notably salmon trout; they
were washed out of a pond in the old Cremorne
gardens at Richmond during a big flood years ago
along with some species of carp. The golden carp
are much in evidence (with brobdignagian eels),
floating dead in myriads during the periodical
poisonings that afflict the lower Yarra waters,
while black carp from the same source are caught
in the lagoons adjacent to the Yarra. After
spawning the roach shoals return to their haunts
in the deep holes in the stream around Abbotsford
and Kew, where they afford the languid angler a
dish occasionally.
* * » *
“Penguin”: Western Port (Victoria) is
the great meeting place of the mutton birds on
the mainland. Their date is on or about (gener-
ally on) the 15th November. The Schanck, Cape
Wollomai, and all the ocean front of Phillip
Island swarm with them. It would take millions
to count them. Melbourne confectioners send
down gangs of men to collect eggs, and local
fishermen get oil. Seize the bird by the neck
with one hand, strip the body down hard with
the other and the oil drips out. It is nasty work ;
so is schnapper fishing. As to eating, the man
who could tackle mutton bird in any form would
be fit to go south with Borchegrevink or to be
wrecked on a desert island. Tried them once
plain, once again with sauce. Sauce would silence
asafcetida, but it wouldn’t disguise mutton bird.
» * * *
“F.E.P.W.”: During the drought in
W.Q., sheep, in jumping to reach leaves of the
scrub, were often caught in the forks of the trees,
and died suspended there. Also, deaths of rains
occur fairly frequently from interlocking of their
horns. If not found and released, they die. By
the way, the resisting power of a ram’s head must
be very considerable. A knock on, or near, the
point of the horn I have found most effective in
separating the tied-up combatants.

“ T.P.0.” : In Ronald Macdonald’s book,
“Gum Boughs and Wattle Bloom ” (page 9), he
says: “Another fruit of fraudulent type growing
on the plains is the quandong.” Up this way
(S.W. Queensland) the quandong doesn’t grow on
the plains. It is not to be found on the east side
of the Maranoa and Balonne rivers, although the
soil and conditions in many spots east of those
rivers seem just as favorable for its growth as
where it grows plentifully, viz., the west side of
the Balonne. I have asked many whites and
blacks, but they couldn’t solve the problem.
What have we done to aggravate the fruit and
cause this aloofness ?

“Nymsie” : Be the pelican’s nest, A. J.
Campbell’s book on Australian birds says :
Nest. —Almost flat, and about ISin. across ; roughly
constructed of dry herbage, etc. In some instances the
nest is merely a slight hollow scraped in the ground ;
placed in a small rookery on the highest part of small
islands at sea, or in a lake.
Eggs.—Clutch, two to three; elliptical in shape;
texture of shell coarse ; surface glossy ; color, white,
with a thick coating (sometimes smooth, in other in-
stances rough) of lime.
“ Paddy Melon ” : Have often
noticed that rabbits are of a very excitable
temperament. At hare-drives have seen
them when surrounded by horses, dogs,
and howling cookies, become utterly be-
wildered—so bewildered as to be powerless
to seek safety in flight. Have often dis-
mounted and caught them when in this
condition. Self and three mates were
shooting at Shookaroo (N.S.W.) some time
ago. Rabbits were there in droves. Had
just “potted” a duck and several rabbits
from tne one standing position. Mates
were also shooting in every direction, and
the dogs were playing havoc among the
bunnies. Just then I observed a black
rabbit, apparently overcome with fear,
hiding under a log. Sneaked up, rested
my gun against the log, and cautiously
locating the exact position of the quarry,
made a grab. Bunny still made no effort
to escape. From appearances he had been
dead about a month. Believe, though,
that he had simply become decomposed
with terror as I came up.
* * • *
Who said there is no humor in
Australians? In western N.S.W., where
rain is a phenomenon of as frequent occur-
rence as a solar eclipse, guide-posts have
been placed across the creek-beds on the
coach routes, to direct drivers in times of
flood. Also, Government has employed
men (generally some sort of relation to the
sitting M.P.) to dig trenches and build
embankments alongside the roads to keep
back the water. In turn the water becomes
sarcastic and washes away guide - posts,
gutters, embankments, and roads. The
coach-driver then makes a new road for
himself, and the “maintenance man”
puts up more posts, digs more trenches,
&c. For the money spent in getting these roads
ready to be washed away a really good, durable
road could be imported from Germany or some
such place. Then we could at least boast about
the increase of our commerce.
* * * *
“ Polstan ” : Things are looking up in the
Vic. Mallee. During a recent two weeks no fewer
than 32 pianos arrived in Nhill. Nhill, with its
fires, its dust, its cloggy mud, its utter desolation,
and its sixpenny beer—and 32 pianos on top of
all. Irvine calls the residents of Nhill “bis own
people,” and, alas ! they look like it.
The wholesale destruction of the tui is
causing a sentimental outburst in Maonland in
favor of the preservation of the species. Press
prominence is being given to the fact that the tui,
which is now supposed to be protected by law,
should be fenced round, so to speak, with a hedge
of sentiment, so that, as is the case in England
with the robin redbreast, even the small boy won’t
touch it. The tui should be invested with an air
of sanctity. But the trouble is that the stodgy,
greasy Maori has no sentiment worth mentioning,
and would cheerfully boil an angel for breakfast if
he could get one. He is the principal and worst
slayer of the tui.

wild pork. That’s the party’s yarn, anyway. A
big brother of the mythical taniwha or kumi
probably, or a sea-serpent that got bushed.

“ J.C.” : Mutton-birds’ eggs are very much
like the eggs of the common or farmyard duck,
only a sharper oval in shape, and the shell is
almost transparent. They are not nice boiled,
but are all right disguised in an omelet or fried
with bacon, as many . boarding-house keepers
know. The mutton-bird thoughtfully lays its
egg on the 25th of November (it also lays it on
Phillip 1.-land and other places), in good time for
the Christmas cooking ; and the Melbourne con-
fectioner seizes the opportunity, likewise the
eggs, for Christmas comes but once a year, and
so does the mutton-bird.

“D.H.R.” : Thus a witness in a recent
cattle case at a Western N.S.W. town :—“ Have
been working for Mr. nine months. Know
the heifer in dispute. Know it is 15 months old
by size of it. Can swear Mr. never sold any
cattle. Can’t swear Mr. - never sold any
cattle. Know heifer is Mr. ’s because
I know it is (repeated 17 times). Don’t know
that heifer is Mr. ’s. Know most of Mr.
’a cattle. Don’t know most of Mr. - ——’s
cattle. Can swear that bull broke fence. Can’t
swear bull broke fence. Can swear Mr.
never sold you (lawyer) the heifer in dispute
before I came here. Can’t swear Mr. never
sold heifer before I came here” (another three
columns). After this, what is evidence ?

Photo, by Cecil Studios, Sydney.
The Acting-Administrator of Papua, in a
despatch to Premier Barton, writes interestingly
of the ape like Ahgaiambo tribe, who, for longer
than even native tradition wots of, have lived on
the fringe of a morass on the left bank of the
Musa river. Thick-necked, deep-chested, long-
armed, those people have lower limbs of utterly
disproportionate smallness —centuries of marsh-
dwelling have almost deprived them of the power
of walking. Feet are short and broad, very thin and
flat; toes long and slight, standing out rigidly
from the feet, as if jointless. When placed on the
ground the feet look like nothing so much as
wooden feet, and the whole app< irance of the
native is eminently ape-like. Tribe moves about
in small canoes; houses are built on piles 12ft.
high, and the food consists of wild-fowl, fish, sago,
and marsh plants. Their dead are placed on
small platforms roofed with rude matting.
M'JUMBUCK’S CHRISTMAS PARTY!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was on M'Jumbuck’s station, Christmas nineteen-fifty-
three,
In as fine a part of Queensland as a man would wish
to see.
Old M'Jumbuck was a squatter who would treat his
station hands
Like a white man ; and they loved him, and were faithful
to commands.
Don’t forget that it was Christmas—Christmas nineteen-
fifty-three,
And M'Jumbuck’s men and others had foregathered for a
spree.
They awoke on Christmas morning from a sound and
heavy sleep,
And the snow lay crisp and even and some sixteen inches
deep I
But they troubled not in Queensland (not in nineteen-
fifty-three)
For heavy frost at Christmas was a thing they’d looked
to see !
Why, it didn’t seem like Christmas then, without a fall of
snow.
And the robin-redbreasts tapping on the window-panes
you know 1
Did those hardy Queensland bushmen fear the snow and
dread the cold ?
No, for they were reared in Queensland and acquaintance
made t hem bold 1
Yet, they’d heard from Daddy Hardface many a yarn and
many a rhyme
Of the very sultry weather there was once at Christmas
time.
Well, it was a gorgeous Christmas and a truly gorgeous
spread,
Sixty people at the table, with M'Jumbuck at their head.
It was just to please M'Jumbuck and to send the ball
along
That old Hardface (when the toasts were reached) arose
and sang this song :
“ I remember, well remember,
Though the time has long gone by,
When Australia’s bright December
Was much warmer than July !
“ Though, of course, you won’t believe me,
And the statement seems absurd ;
If my memory don’t deceive me
Christmas picnics once occurred !
“ I remember, well remember,”
Went on Hardface, looking up,
“ ’Twas a very hot November
When The Victory won the Cup.
“ But the next year (now I time it
That was nineteen-nought-and-three)
Brought the change in this here climate
Which at present you can see.
“ It was loyalty that planted
All the changes which have come.
The Australian climate wanted
To be just the same as ’Ome ! ”
Here old Hardface poked the fire,
And they said the belt he’d won,
As the most accomplished liar
On M'Jumbuck’s Queensland run !
But M’Jumbuck told his bushman ’twas the truth old
Hardface told,
That the Christmas came in summer in the curious days
of old.
’Twas his personal opinion that the vital change was
made
Through Australia’s loyal climate and through preferen-
tial trade!
N.S.W.
W. T. GOODGE.

FAIRFAX & ROBERTS,
The Oldest Jewellery
House in Sydney,
23 Hunter Street*
Forthefirsttime, N.S.W.
Forestry Department lately
made an attempt to prepare
a timber map of the State.
It divides the territory into
five zones—the coastal, the
highlands, the zone of brush
timber, the interior, and the
timberless. The timberless
part, which includes every-
thing west of an irregular line
drawn from Angledool on the
north to Balranald on the
south, includes about 40 per
cent, of the whole of N.S.W.
For its area, the brush-timber
district, which takes in the
middle reaches of the northern
rivers, contains by far the
greatest number of timber
trees ; Moreton Bay pine, red
cedar, silky oak, rosewood,
red bean, black bean, bogum-
bogum, beefwood, cudgerie,
white beech, tulipwood and
sassafras grow in the same
neighborhood. The ironbark
is the most widely distributed;
it grows practically wherever
timber _ grows in N.S.W.,
except in the extreme south,
part of the Riverina, and
the brush-timber district.
Stringybark takes second
place; but it does not grow
along the coast to the north
of Newcastle, and it is rarer
also along the western rivers.
Outside the brush-timber dis-
trict there are only three
varieties growing to any ex-
tent which are not eucalypts—
the turpentine, the brush-box,
and the cypress pine. Botani-
cally, of course, the blackbutt,
tallow-wood, red mahogany,
messmate, mountain ash,
blood wood, and woolly-butt,
as well as all the gums—blue,
grey, spotted, and red—and
all the boxes except the brush
box, are eucalypts.

JJOAD BLOCKING, NEERIM TOWNSHIP.
For Chief of Victorian Police Department, Mel-
bourne, as against Shire Council of Buln Buln, if it be
concerned, and road blockers and their sympathisers.
—ln a public matter police protection was asked for,
and payment offered. The only apparent answer has

down, thus throwing the paddock open to the public.
Neerim, Gippsland,
13th November, 1903.
TOO ESTHETIC.
THE PAKEHA LADY : “ Well, Mrs. Taipara, and
how is your daughter getting on at the
college? ”
THE MAORI LADY: “Oh, I tink not much; I
tink kool no goot, she get too prout. She no
let te pig sleep in te whare now"
The Australian kid takes to the track
early. One of him, aged 3| years, was discovered
15 miles from home in the Goulburn district,
t’other day; he had done the distance in two days.
When found, he was peacefully sleeping beside a
stump, none the worse for his exposure. That
was his second turn “on the wallaby,” too.
A SLOW ROAD.
PEDESTRIAN : “ Where does this road take me, my man? ”
SWAGGIE: “ You’ll do a bloomin’ perish, mister, if yer wait fer
this road to take you anywhere."
“ The Myrtle ” swears to this :
Know of an emu at Tungamull (0.Q.). Taken there as
a.chick some years ago, it has grown to be a fine, stately
bird, able to hold its own in the station yards. When
the drought was at its worst the vicissitudes to which
birds and beasts with all their senses were exposed were
very trying, but those of an old blind horse were
extreme. There was only one little hole on a wide area
of country where the horse could get water, and for
weeks while the drought continued the emu led it there
as its necessities demanded. It also guided the steed
about the bush, warning it in intelligible language of
fallen trees, and even leading it to where food was to be
had. Since the rain, the bird has somewhat relaxed its
attentions.

“ Ed.” : A farmer in the Western District
(Vie.) was always troubled by one crow which
stood by while he milked.
One cow had a sore on the
udder; to soften this he
brought every morn-
ing a small piece of
lard and placed it
on a post, intending
on apply it after
milking. The
crow regularly
swooped down
and flew off
with the
lard, spite of
the dog sent
to scare it.
To get even
with the
crow, the far-
mer brought
out a piece of
fat sprinkled
with strych-
nine and
placed it as
usual. The
crow swoop-
ed down and
flew off with
it, the dog
folio win g.
Getting to a
tree the
crow found
the fat not
so agreeable
as usual, and
threw it
down to the
dog; the dog
swallowed
it, came
back to the milking
shed, turned three
somersaults, and rolled over. The
orow came back, and looked on well satisfied
with the dog’s capers, but he never called again.
There is something demoniao about the intelli-
gence of a crow.
* * * »
Passengers on the Tumut-Cootamundra
(N.S.W.) line, the other day, had their train held
up by caterpillars. The pe3t swarmed across the
rails in overwhelming numbers, and the wheels,
after crushing through the mass for a considerable
distance, became so greasy that they would not
“bite.” Sweeping was tried, and the sand-boxes
were freely used by the driver, but the wheels
still flew round whilst the train stood still.
Meanwhile, the caterpillars swarmed into the
carriages, and walked in a horrible green fashion
over the passengers. Finally, bagging was tied to
the cow-catcher, so as to sweep the rails before
the front wheels, and, getting a start by this
means and the use of sand, the train ploughed
slowly through, and went away 40 minutes late
and covered with caterpillars.

“Glenara”: Reports from the Victorian
Mallee show that the much-dreaded rust has
taken a big hold on the wheat crops, and is likely
to lessen considerably the mighty yield that the
Melbourne press has proclaimed as “assured.”
On Tyrell Downs some thousands of acres will
give Only three dr four bushels per acre instead of
the predicted four bags. Something seems to be
always striking the Mallee with a blighting biff,
and that portion of “our vast natural resources”
hangs like a never-lifted dust-box round Victoria’s
neck. John West and J. L. Dow and various
other ink-bottle farmers talk about “saving the
Mallee,” but the aforesaid Mallee is like a
Chinaman : what good is it when it is saved ?
* • * •
4 4 Deeby ” : Sleeping out on hot summer
nights in the west the mosquitoes, ants, centi-
pedes, scorpions, and—in lignum country—snakes,
made life a perfect hell till I hit on the device of
sleeping in a large calico bag drawn well over the
head. The relief and safety afforded by this
simple wrinkle are simply heavenly. It once
afforded me protection from a much larger enemy
than the usual bush night-pest. Sleeping one
night on a plain near the Merri-Merri I was
awakened by feeling a warm blast of air coming
on my head through the mouth of my sleeping-
bag. Instantly I sat up, and the largest wild boar
I ever saw fairly collapsed with fright. He left
off breathing on me and fled for all he was worth
across the moonlit plain, and till the distance
swallowed him up he was still putting up all the
record there was in him.
WATTLE BARK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
O glad bush, gay with golden sprays
Whose sweet breath fills the breeze;
Grey winter’s gone, and warm spring days
With new sap glads the trees.
The magpie’s chiming joyously
Unto its friend the lark ;
And men and boys go gladly forth
To strip the wattle bark.
Our cash is short, so’s flour and tea,
And Daisy wants a dress,
And summer clothes would welcome bo
To mum and Jim and Jess.
There’s thirteen hours good daylight now
From early morn till dark,
We’ll soon financial be again
By stripping wattle bark.
So grind the axes up with care,
Bring out the strap and chain,
None knows what lies before us ere
The wattle strips again.
But work and love and life are good,—
Who dreams of care and cark
On dewy mornings when we go
A-stripping wattle bark ?
VIC.

E.E.M. : Transvaal Yolksraad consisted of two Houses,
each of 27 members. The Upper House was elected by
first-class burghers (Boers, and foreigners who had served
in the Boer army, and any other foreigners who might be
admitted by the Volksraad). Naturalised foreigners had
a vote for the Lower House (naturalisation being allowed
after two years’ residence and payment of £2). Britain
has also a Parliament of two Houses. No foreigner, even
if naturalised, can qualify for Britain’s Upper House, but
a naturalised foreigner may get a vote for the British
Lower House. Naturalisation in England requires five
years’ residence, proof of an intention to remain
in British territory, and payment of JE6

Arthur Griffith, the Labor candidate for
the Senate in N.S.W., was, as a member of the
N.S.W. Assembly, accused in different quarters
of being the extremest Radical and the best-
dressed man in the Chamber. A big. forcible
figure, Griffith is cut out for public life, but
wasted many years as a teacher at Sydney
Grammar School, the most fashionable of the
Sydney seminaries. Even there his radical views
profoundly shocked the judicial set from which he
Sprang; since he has been in Parliament that set
views him with utter horror. Griffith’s return
would raise the intellectual average of the Senate
appreciably and retain for N.S.W. at least one
real man in its representation.
ALICE WICKHAM , a lightning swimmer.
Photo, by W Johnson, Sydney.
A few months ago one of the unfinished
works of the Victorian Government was brought
prominently under public notice by establishing a
record in the way of small-boy killing. In con-
nection with the Yarra improvement scheme an
open excavation 30ft. deep and half full of water
was left. When three lives were lost in five days
the Government decided to do something, and
enclosed the water-hole with wire netting through
which only a very thin small boy could make his
way. Small boy mortality was thus checked, but
stagnant water soon developed smells. The
netting which kept the small boy out was unequal
to the task of keeping the microbe in, and resi-
dents in the vicinity are urging the Bent depart-
ment to either complete the work or fill the
cesspool in. But the Irvine Government isn’t
worrying. It is used to smells.
The best Yuletide greeting to send to
friends in other lands will be the Christmas
number of Thb Bulletin, to be issued on
December 10th. In its pages will be found the
best presentation of Australian life and thought
ever issued, and no effort has been spared to make
it the best Christmas number up to date.
