SYDNEY


THE FAMOUS ZETZ=SPA.
Australia’s Great Mineral Water.
Queen Of Natural Beverages.
TITHE Famous Mineral Waters of many lands
' I ' have played a highly important part in
JL the social life of civilized mankind. The
Health-giving properties of the Springs
revealed themselves long before science in
chemistry became sufficiently advanced to deter-
mine exactly the reason why certain troublesome
complaints were relieved through the consumption
of the physic, dispensed in the great laboratories
beneath the surface of the earth. No more con-
vincing proof of the wholesomeness and the value
of such waters could be advanced than the fact
that afflicted men and women turned naturally to
the sparkling fluids for relief. Domestic animals
eat medicinal vegetation through the promptings
of a natural instinct, and it is asserted, by
FILLING THE DRUMS AT THE SPRING.
professors of natural history, that certain reptilefe
turn to a herbal antidote .when infected by the
venom of their enemies. Man being possessed of
a higher instinct, properly termed reasoning
power, turned nature’s resources to greater
advantage. For centuries past it has been the
custom for people well equipped in worldly goods
to travel long distances to places where curative
waters have been obtainable. Such places have,
indeed, become fashionable resorts, and it is highly
probable that many votaries attend in the season,
mainly because their attendance is commanded by
the inexorable laws which compel men, fat and
scant of breath, to dance in hot rooms, and weak-
chested, fragile women to sit in cold places and
sip ices. The waters, however, do nobody injury,
while they contribute to the earthly days -of
thousands.
For many years, only those who could attend
the places where the springs Were found, could
obtain the benefits of the beverages supplied by
kind old mother earth. But modern met ods
have brought the mineral waters within the reach
of all people. We have them now, in most
ZETZ-SPA TANKB AT TOOTH’S BREWERY.
palatable and attractive forms, at the soft drink
shops; in places where one may improve the
flavor and add potency to the draught by the
addition of stronger waters ; and in our homes.
Australians were dependent upon supplies from
older countries while the resources of this new
land were being exploited. Foreign waters be-
came popular, and the consumers did not look for
any other, being for the most part under the
impression that the customary spa water was
either made in Germany or raised by hand in
foreign parts, and could not be produced else-
where.
This deeply-rooted conviction has been torn up
and dispelled by a discovery made at Ballimore,
in the. Dubbo district, some 16 years ago. Balli-
more is a farming district 23 miles from Dubbo. It
is reachedby a pretty road, through slightly undula-
ting country, which brings one to the Talbragar
River. This small river has a deep, narrow
channel, which, at Ballimore, sharply divides the
level wheat country on the Dubbo side from some
rough ranges, which terminate in steep bluffs
overlooking the stream. The place presents all
the features of the sites of some of the most
famous mineral springs of the world. There are
the ragged auriferous hills and the sudden descent,
at the foot of which the springs are usually found.
In such places the valuable waters are tapped at
considerable depths ; indeed, these are considered
to be the best of all spa waters. The spot at
Ballimore was not selected as the probable site of
a mineral spring. The discoverers of the wealth-
producer were boring for coal when the sort of
thing happened which took place when two
broken-hearted diggers struck the largest nugget
of gold discovered in Australia :
** Nobody more suprised than the striker."
The directors of the Great Western Coal Mining
Company had acquired a Crown Purchase area,
and were boring close to the river. A seam
sft. 2in. thick was passed through at 540 ft. ; at
550 ft. the bore struck water, and the water
promptly made its way to the surface. There it
rose some feet in the air and continued to flow, at
the rate of over 1000 gallons an hour, a rate of
discharge which has been maintained to this day.
Somebodv tried the water and found that it had
a mineral taste, b..t the company, being compelled
to abandon boring on account of the aggressive
spring, were not in a mood to regard it as a
friendly force. The people of the. district, how-
ever, soon discovered that the spring was a most
unusual one. They drank the water and found it
good, and they endeavored to carry off supplies,
But the fluid resented attempts to imprison it.
Like Anstey’s geni, having escaped from its
captivity, it was not in a hurry to be again im-
prisoned, and it burst out of corked vessels in
blusterous indignation. Then it occurred to
somebody that the vyater was highly charged
with gas, and presently Mr. W. H. J. Siee,
(Superintendent of Drills, interested himself
in the great discovery. Mr. Slee, in 1887,
reported to the Under-Secretary for Mines
on the spring. He stated that when first taken
from the bore the water contained gases and
tasted like German seltzer water, and the dis-
coverv was, in his opinion, one of the greatest
im'oitance. The water would ascend through
tubing to over 30ft. in the air, and was therefore
most accessible. Mr. Slee went on to remark
that lie saw no reason why Ballimore should not
beoome an Australian Carlsbad or Gastein, and
the water be used for drinking and bathing by
people afflicted with rheumatism, dyspepsia, and
kindred diseases. It was a great pity that a
spring yielding over 1000 gallons of such water
every hour should not be turned to its true com-
mercial value. He was of opinion that if .the
curative qualities of the water were brought pro-
minently before the public the community of New
South Wales would be benefited.
The crystal fountain of the spring continued
flowing into the river for several years. Picnic
parties assembled at Ballimore from time to time,
and the Zetz-Spa became renowned in the Dubbo
district. Meanwhile Mr. James Samuels, chair-
man of the G.W. Gold Mining Company, caused
analyses to be made, and reports were received by
him from eminent medical men in Sydney. Each
authority in turn was delighted with the water
and impressed by the immense commercial value
of the spring. Professor Ludwick Brack, having
had an opportunity of examining the Zetz-Spa,
prepared a paper which gave eloquent testimony
of the superiorty of the water. The water is
clear, wrote Professor Bruok, free from odor, and
highly charged with carbonio acid. Professor
Brack stated that the water compared favorably
with the far-famed bi-carbonate of soda springs of
Vichy, France. Gout, gravel, stone in the
bladder, and diabetes, also splenic and hepatic
disorders, were benefited by such waters as that of
Ballimore.
What attracted the attention and compelled the
admiration of the experts was the fact that, while
Zetz-Spa contained all the essential constituents
of the best mineral waters, it was,, at the same
time, a most palatable and refreshing beverage.
Doubtless this quality first caught the apprecia-
tion of-the public when the Zetz-Spa Proprietary
Company in 1897 placed the water on the Sydney
market. Its advent is very fresh in the memories
of the thousands who welcomed the generous
sparkling fluid that popped the corks from the
bottles, and gurgled and bubbled joyously in the
glass. The limb-weary and brain-fagged city
man who sought a stimulant after a long hot day,
suddenly discovered an elixir of energy. Were
his favorite tipple spirits, claret, hock, or any-
thing that came quickest to the hand of the bar-
THE WAGGON LOADED WITH STEEL DRUMS CONTAINING SPA WATER.
lady, Zetz-Spa gave it new life, and made the
drink a reviving influence that shook off the care
and weariness of the day like magic.
The company was at this time backed up by the
reports which had been obtained by Mr. Samuels,
Mr. Will A. Dixon, F.1.G., F.C.S., lecturer at
the Technical College, Sydney, was warmly in-
terested in the remarkable spring. He likened it
to Vichy after a careful analysis, and declared it
to be the best of Australian mineral waters. After
stating that Zetz-Spa must be highly beneficial in
such disorders as those mentioned by Professor
Bruck, Mr. Dixon’s report concluded: “I find
that the Ballimore Water is an agreeable and
invigorating beverage, containing in its virgin
state natural carbonic acid gas, and is a healthful
addition to wines, spirits, &c., with which it
blends remarkably well. In my opinion, it is a
table water of the very highest order, for whilst
containing properties essentially health-giving, it
is very agreeable to the taste.” Zetz-Spa became
a very customary drink, and the people have at
length realised that Australia has produced a
mineral water unsurpassed by those of foreign
THE ZETZ-BPA BOTTLING DEPARTMENT, TOOTH’S BREWERY.
land 3, which had hitherto maintained world-wide
reputations.
During the present year, the wonderful Spring
at Ballimore, and the freehold of the land from
which it spouts, were purchased by Messrs,
Tooth and Co. The water is now being trans-
Sorted in new improved drums of tinned steel to
ydney. These drums are used to conserve the
natural gas in the water, which is bottled in the
splendidly equipped Factory at Tooth’s Brewery,
Red fern. Extensive arrangements have been
made for the distribution of the Zetz-Spa, under
the direction of Mr. H. C. Forrest, the manager
of the department, and large quantities are being
brought from the spring. There are immense possi-
bilities in Ballimore. The supply of the valuable
water is sufficient for baths on a magnificent scale,
and, the climate being warm, and the air delight-
fully rare, a great sanatorium could be established,
Meanwhile, there is hardly a more interesting trip
in this State than that from Sydney to the Zetz-
Spa Soring. The writer was one of a party which
recently set out by the night mail train, and
arrived in Dubbo next morning. We were driven
to Ballimore, through the drought-stricken coun-
try, by Mr. George Gilles, the mail contractor,
and were most comfortably housed and sump-
tuously fed at the farm house of Mr. J ohn Gilles.
A revelation awaited ns at the river, about a mile
from the homestead. The river had not received
a drop of water from its natural catchment during
many months, and, save for a quarter of a mile of
its course, it was as dry as the lips of a wooden
image. But the quarter of a mile was a winding
mirror of Zetz-Spa water, reflecting the pretty
foliage that fringed the banks of the river. The
spring ever rising had filled that length, and we
were looking upon a veritable river of Spa water,
which was 7 feet deep in places. The pipe through
which the water formerly ascended had been
taken down, and the water turned into the river
by a horizontal pipe, and at the elbow the gas was
escaping with a gurgling “glug, glug,” but there
was enough coming through with the water to fly
up our noses when we tried to drink it at the end
of the pipe. A tumbler and some of the contents
of a glass bottle with a very interesting label on
the outside, and the remains of a capsu'e round
its neck, arranged matters very satisfactorily for
all concerned. When we let it bubble into a
glass and held it to the sun it was crystal clear
and sparkling like a goblet of diamonds. That
flow has been coming up from the ; earth for over
16 years, and there is every indication that it will
rival Tennyson’s brook in everlastingness. The
photographs which illustrate this article were taken
by the writer and another member of the party,
Some of the local residents bathe in the Spa, and
there is at least one who has been transformed
from a rheumatic cripple into a hearty, active
man. This old gentleman has turned 70, yet he
rides with the seat and suppleness of a youth of 20,
and is constantly engaged in bush work. Taking
the Zetz-Spa water, one finds all digestive
troubles vanish, and at the same time enjoys a
most refreshing table beverage. The only virtue
which we found noticeably lacking in the Spa,
while we stayed at Ballimore, was in connection
with the sport of the place. The water would not
make the guns shoot straight, although we tried
it neat and otherwise.
It goes without saying that, in the hands of
Messrs. Tooth and Co., the Zetz-Spa will be
placed before the public in the perfect condition
in which it flows from the earth. Extensive
arrangements have been made for perfect bottling,
and the people are now able to obtain that greatest
of boons, a curative water, which is at the same
time a most delicious drink. Before purchasing
the Zetz-Spa, Tooth and Co. obtained analyses
from the most reliable authorities, and this sketch
may be fittingly closed by quoting the latest
analytical report obtained from Dr. Helms, M. A.,
F.C.S., who has twice visited Ballimore to test
the waters. It runs as follows:—“The water as
discharged is brilliantly clear. The temperature
of the water was 78.5 F., the air temperature being
102 F., the specific gravity is 10041. Organically
the water is pure, though the free ammonia is
high, as often happens in deep-seated springs,
The water contains 368.01 solid substances per
gallon, consisting of : Bicarbonate of soda, 287.901
grains per gallon; potash, 35.263 grains; lime,
18.604 grains; magnesia, 15.841 grains; iron,
traces ; manganese, traces ; strontium, traces ;
lithium, traces; chloride of sodium, 7.012; sulphate
of lime, 0.294; alumina, 0.112 ; siliceous sub-
stances, 1.064.

POLITICAL
POINTS
in N.S.W. Religion is the last refuge of
Calico.
George Turner, in his first campaign
speech: “Australia first. Great Britain next,
and let the foreigner look after himself.”
At Rookwood (N.S.W.) asylum foraged
men are 700 electors who have been enfranchised
by recent legislation, Varney Parkes addressed
a meeting of the ancients lately, on behalf of the
Cheap Imported Coffin Party.
Dr. Mackellar will not be a candidate for
the Senate at the coming election; nor will B. R.
Wise run. Griffith, the Labor candidate, will
have the Ministerial support; the other two
vacancies still lack contestants in the National
oause.
Brewer Gray, M.L.C. (Q.), made a fatal
mistake when he referred to Treasurer Kidston’a
financial troubles of some years ago. He has set
people thinking about the financial affairs of
one or two of his own colleagues in the late
Government.
Miss Vida Goldstein, the lady candidate
in the Senate sprint down South, has introduced
a nove) feature in electioneering tactics. Being
unable to bear the expenses of an election, she
takes up a silver coin collection after her meetings
to defray the incidental expenditure.
There is some talk of the Queensland
Labor party running a candidate against Lyttle-
ton Groom, who has well represented Darling
Downs since the death of his father. Groom is a
sterling Protectionist, and a Democrat to the
marrow ; and, if the Labor party opposes him, it
will be a bad move.
“ Vindex ” : Malcolm M‘Eacharn would
get in again for Melbourne if the Federal franchise
was the same as that on which he was previously
elected ; then he scored 4985 against Maloney’s
3212. With adult suffrage and no absentee
property vote Maloney will either arrive this time
or be very close up.
There are nine Westralian Senate candi-
dates in the field—three Protectionists, three
Labor men, and three Freetraders. The Pro-
tectionists want to secure work for the people first
of all. The labor men want first of all to make
sure that the work is done under fair conditions
and for a fair wage; and between them the Free-
traders, who want to give the work to a cheap
foreign nigger and leave them both in the lurch,
will probably get in.
BROKEN UP.
“ The State Governor somewhat startled a number of men working on the road near Gloucester by alighting
from his carriage and shaking hands with them.” —Daily paper.
The fiscal situation : We know that Pro-
tection must be a good thing, else Bruce Smith
wouldn’t have practised it so extensively when he
was Treasurer, so we vote for Protection ; or else
we know that Bruce Smith is a hopelessly bad
character who practised Protection in order to
injure the country, in which case we vote against
B. Smith.
Minister Bill Lyne is to be opposed in the
Federal elections by F. E. M‘Lean, who repre-
sented Marrickville in last Australian Parliament.
His decision not to again contest his old seat did
not, despite press assurances to the contrary, cause
any surprise locally. And it’s a mighty poor
recommendation for the new constituency which
he wooes.
Four extra-tough Tories have decided to
run for the Victorian Senate seats as “anti-
Socialists ” —Factories Act Derham, E. E. Smith,
of the House of Fat, old Jock MTntyre, and Col.
J. M. Templeton. It is a hopeless cause that has
to depend on old tenth-rate Jock, who perished
politically, even in small State politics, very long
ago, and would hardly secure election as honorary
patron of a suburban football team.
“ War E.” : N.S.W. Lands Department
has always been associated with smells; and
every new Minister is going to “ clean up,” but,
somehow, he doesn’t quite get there! The
present action regarding the leasehold on Gan-
main holding (N.S.W.) demands some expla-
nation. This leasehold comprises an area of
about 38,000 acres, splendid agricultural land,
right against the South-Western Railway
(which, by the way, doesn’t pay). The lease
expired some 16 months ago, and, though
there is a continual howl for land, no effo 6
has been made to cut it up for settle-
ment ; on the other hand, nothing has
been left undone to hand it bodily over
to a financial co. A proposal to ex-
change this block for several “pea-
cocked patches ” on the back of the
run was only averted by a threat of
exposure in Parliament. At pre-
sent, there is under consideration
a “report” recommending the
retention of the whole of the area
as a “ forest reserve ” J Yet there
isn’t a stick on the whole of it L g
enough to make a whip-handle.
The last session was the emptiest and
windiest in the history of S.A. The Government
decided that if it did nothing the Opposition
would have no pretext on which to empty it out.
Its unanimous sigh of relief when the prorogation
found it still attached to its salary caused much
damage to the young fruit crops.
London St. James’s Gazette says that for
the past 20 odd years three towns in Lancashire
have been gradually sinking, in the face of
foreign tariffs and oheap importations. These
are Prescot, St. Helens, and Widnes, seats of the
watchmaking, plate-glassmaking, and chemical
manufacturing industries, respectively.
The Morgan Government in Queensland,
coming in at the tail-end of a session, had time to
try to wipe out only one of the Philp blots —the
poll-tax. And the Leg. Council defeated the
attempt. When one crowd has for 10 or 12 years
been filling a nominee House with its cast-offs,
the next crowd has a hard row to hoe.
The United States authorities, under their
law against the importation of contract labor,
lately arrested five English cotton spinners (four
men and a woman) and sent them back to
England. The United States shuts down on
imported contract labor with a thoroughness
which would make Australia’s hair stand up on its
sunburnt head.
On the same page on which the Argus
prints the Vic. Employers’ Federation pronounce-
ment against socialistic legislation it publishes an
article on the Melbourne City Council electric
works. This “socialistic experiment” has been
a splendid success, and the council is making a
profit for the ratepayers on a capital expenditure
of over £400,000.
Miss Rose Scott, at Brisbane :
Vote for decent men with brains—no matter what
party they belong to. Avoid sectarianism. What room
is there for faction in a patriotic mind ?
Which means : Vote for Kanakaphiles, alien-
coddlers, Philpite opponents of the female
suffrage—vote for anybody so long as he doesn’t
get drunk and hasn’t been through the divorce
court.
Brisbane’s new Daily Mail can’t find a
name bad enough to throw at the Federal Labor
candidates. One day it publishes a fac-simile of
a voting-paper with instructions as to how to vote
—and brands the Labor men as “Trades Hall”
candidates. Next day it dubs them “Socialists.”
Probably “Communists,” “Anarchists,” and
“ Atheists ” will follow. And the public grins.
Lord Hugh Cecil, spouting at Birmingham,
the other day, uttered a large truth. Said the Cecil,
who is a fierce Foreign-trader, “ Protection merely
takes money out of one pocket to put into
another.” Which is the real beauty of Protection.
The man always has the money about him, in one
pocket or the other. Freetrade takes it out of all
the pockets, and passes it on to the foreigner.
The Queensland Boodle party has made a
fatal mistake in raising the exclusion of colored
aliens question during the present Federal cam-
paign. It means that the White Australia issue
will be fought all over again, with the white
worker and his wife on the side of the Govern-
ment. There is no vote on the other side to
counter-balance the white worker’s wife. There
are 15 niggers to one wife.

HEAVY TRADEDY : “ Yes, good-

The Sydney Telegraph reports with glee
that, at one of the woman candidates’ meetings,
somebody made “a sympathetic appeal to the
audience ” not to ask questions beyond the candi-
date’s depth. Just the same plaintive appeal was
made when the selected Freetrade candidate for
Wentworth spoke at Rand wick ; but the Tele-
graph missed that—it was busy just then saying
what a brilliant, thoughtful, well-informed young
man Mr. W. H. Kelly is.
Parliamentary candidates who never visited
N.B W. old men’s or women’s homes in a private
capacity are now collecting the names of the
inmates, and laying themselves out for vigorous
canvass. As private individuals they would be
free to distribute tobacco or other “comforts,”
but the Federal Electoral Act prohibits candi-
dates indulging in such gifts. Going as a candi-
date costs nothing; indeed, a man is able to plain-
tively regret, while soliciting the votes, that the
electoral law is so strict.
About 40 Freetrade politicians are going
round at present talking about the “selfishness”
of Protection. It is a curious slab of impudence—
that talk about the selfishness of Protection.
Every Freetrader says we should adopt Freetrade
because it will benefit us, make US rich, cause us
to be jolly and fat. It is all “we’’and “us.”
If there is one unselfish Freetrader who advocates
Freetrade because, even if it injures us, it will do
a lot of good to some other country—well, where
is that unselfish person ?
Thus one H. Herfczberg, chairman of
Brisbane Chamber of Commerce :
He did not think it a good thing for Great Britain to
put up a fence around itself in order to shut out the rest
of the world. Mr. Chamberlain’s scheme would have
the effect oj increasing wages and the cost of manufacture,
and he questioned whether this would be a good thing
to do.
The importing crowd’s objection to Protection in
any shape or form expressed in a few words—“ it
would have the effect of increasing wages.”
Queensland Tory organs continue to break
out occasionally about the shameful way in which
their State has been neglected in the matter of
Federal appointments. Up to date Queensland
has supplied the Commonwealth with a Chief
Justice, an Attorney-General, a Crown Solicitor,
a N.S.W. Commandant, a Chief Secretary to the
Post and Telegraph Department, a Naval Com-
mandant and a host of smaller fry. It is the
States like N.S.W. and Queensland, which have
done really well in the way of billets, that raise
the injustice cry.
Federal contest promises some excitement
in Brisbane North. The National Liberal Union
has turned its back on the gushing McPaterson
and has endorsed a solicitor named Morse—after
the election he will probably be known as re-
Morse. But Mac announces that he will stand
against all comers. Then John Arthur--the
violent Kanakaiteand black laborist —threatens to
wipe the floor with every mother’s son who stands
in his piebald path. The man who is making no
fuss, but who will probably get home, is Dr.
Culpin, the Laborite.
Caldwell, Copley, and Senator Charleston
are the National Ass candidates for the Federal
Senate in S.A. The two former are recently-
defeated State politicians who profess to be
“moderate” Protectionists. The last is a rene-
gade Labor member who affects philosophy and
Freetrade, but has extremely nebulous views in
regard to both. The Labor party is running
against them Senator M‘Gregor, Bob Guthrie,
and W. H. Storey, who, man for man, are in
every respect abler and more eligible candidates
than their antagonists.
M‘Millan, arguing against Preference,
gave the whole Freetrade show away the other
night:—
In years to come Australia would probably produce
wheat equal in value to her wool. Well, there was
Canada within seven or eight days’ sail of Great Britain.
She could produce enough wheat to supply the whole of
England. What could we in Australia do, 12,000 miles
away, when Canada was able to supply the whole of
England's grain?
The blessed Freetrader has been telling us all
along that we needn’t trouble about factories—
that if we look after the great natural pro-
ducing industries we shall be all right. And
now M'Millan tells us that if we depend upon
the great natural industry of wheat - raising,
Canada will knock us into ruins.
The discussion in Queensland Leg. Coun-
cil on the Bill to abolish the poll-tax showed the
depths which that chamber of political cast-offs
and unknowns has reached. T. J. Hannear was
against the Bill because he wanted to see the
income-tax abolished altogether: “the Govern-
ment could have sold land to bring in far more
than the tax.” And E. J. Stevens was for the
Bill, because the loss of revenue “ could be made
up bv the sale of land before next year.” Neither
side of the House can think of any way of feeding
a dog except cutting his tail off and throwing it
to him.
“In the fifty years from 1853 to 1902,
inclusive, nine millions and a quarter of people
emigrated from the United Kingdom ... It is
at least a striking gloss on the question which
Mr. Chamberlain has raised that they have all vol-
untarily turned their backs upon the only paradise
of Ereetrade in the world, and have sought to live
under Protection for the sole reason that they can
there secure a higher standard of individual com-
fort. Amongst all these millions there is a
percentage of failure and disaster, but it is
evident that this percentage is very much lower
than it is at home.”— London Referee.
Kingston is aching for a fight in his dis-
trict of Adelaide, and has pathetically invited
some importer or other person who has lavished
abuse on him for the last three years to come out
and oppose him. Editor Sowden, of the Foreign-
trade Register, is alleged to have Federal aspira-
tions, and Kingston has tenderly beckoned to him.
But he seems to be doomed to an unopposed
return. The National Ass. says it holds aloof
lest, by throwing Kingston out of Federal politics,
it should drive nim back into the State Legisla-
ture, which is very much like the reason the fly
gave for not throwing the elephant over the fence.
George Reid is still Yes-Noing. He is.
going out to theßiverina to yelp at Chanter mainly
because Chanter's party wouldn’t illegally suspend
the fodder duties during the drought. Reid can’t,
sufficiently express his disgust for a man who
wouldn’t make allowances for a district smitten,
with misfortune. But the same drought
temporarily depopulated Riverina ; and when, in.
consequence, Chanter’s party refused to largely
disfranchise the district, as Reid wanted it to do,
he is more horrified than ever. It was a crime-
not to make allowances for the drought, and it.
was just as big a crime to make allowances for the-
drought.
Sydney paper item :
Mrs. M. Moore Bentley, who announces herself as a.
real Freetrader, is vigorously prosecuting her candi-
dature.
A real Freetrader—one who is prepared to raise-
as much as possible of the necessary revenue by
direct taxation (and considering how much per
head Britain raises in direct taxation Australia,
could, without taxing its people more heavily than
Britain does, abolish all customs except the spirit
duty); who is prepared to protect nothing, neither
beer, wine, sugar, cigars, newspapers, agricultural,
produce, nor anything else ! Truly, angels rush
in where the Yes-No fears to tread.
The National Black and Liberal Tan.
Union of Queensland, which is running John
Bartholomew, John Murray, and the martial;
Tunbridge for the Senate, has resurrected the old
wheeze with “the liberty of the subject” in it—
“ legalised interference in industrial disputes is a.
violation of that liberty of the subject which has-
been the boast and glory of the British race.”
The Black-and-Tan Union ought to turn up its-
dictionary and learn that a subject is. a person-
under control, and that it might just as well talk
of the blackness of white, or the coldness of heat,
or the fullness of vacancy, or the freedom of?
bondage, as of the liberty of the subject.
South Australia’s Legislative Council is
paid (its members get £2OO a year, same as the
Assembly men), and that will be the death of it.
yet. The country would
put up with it so long
as it merely played
the deuce with
legislation ; but as
it costs something
directly, and as
South Aus. is look-
ing round to save
sixpence, it is
doomed.

Cabinet are largely interested in various private-
schemes to cut up land for closer settlement ?If
so, is it likely that that Cabinet will push on with
State efforts to cut up big estates, and thu3 bring:
the Government into competition with its own
private profit-seeking speculations ?

Warwick, Queensland, May 28th, 1901.
s * r > Doubtless you will be glad to receive another
-timony to the efficacy of your valuable remedy VITA-
About 12 months ago I went to the Ebagoolah Gold-
fields, and hence to New Guinea, full of health and hope,
but it was not long before I became a victim to that fear-
ful climate which completely undermined my health. I
Was treated in Northern Queensland in two hospitals
without success, finally and with difficulty that would
take too long to detail,l reached Brisbane when 1 secured
your remedy, which I am glad to say at once produced a
magical effect and ultimately restored me to health and
vigor.
I am delighted to be able to write thus, and would
suggest that you publish this in the papers circulating
in New Guinea where I am so well-known.
I am not a doctor, but I believe my trouble was a
severe disorder of the kidneys caused by and attended
with malarial fever.—Yours truly,
„ (signed) ANDREW JAMIESON.

BRISBANE.
| In 1872 Great Britain sent to Protectionist
countries (some of them had only just begun to be
Protectionist) £116,000,000 worth of manufactured
goods. Last year the total was down to
£73,500,000, and the fall between the two points
had been fairly steady. On the other hand, Pro-
tectionist United States, which in 1870 exported
£81,000,000 worth of goods to Great Britain, in
1901 exported £141,015,000. In other words,
.Freetrade Britain, in 30 years, lost 37 per cent!
of its trade with Protected countries, while in
30 years Protective United States increased its
trade with Preetrade Great Britain by 74 per
cent. A country that can’t take as big a hint as
f jhat must be a very dull country indeed.
The man on the Land is always in trouble, but
his troubles end when he places his business in
the hands of Land Agent W. N. Willis, of 21
Bligh-street. No results no fees, is his business
shingle.*
N.S. W. acting-Chief Justice Stephen has
spent his last day on N.S.W. Bench. With a
couple of intermissions, he, his father and grand-
father held N.S.W. Supreme Court Bench since
1825. Alfred ascended, the Bench six years after
John, and M. H. climbed up 14 years after
Alfred. The three, among them, spent 58 years
on the N.S.W. judgment seat. The next of the
family in legal order is Cecil B. Cecil is the
oldest practising barrister wearing stuff, and some
think that now that Matthew Henry quits,
symmetry would be satisfied by Cecil mounting
the Bench. Cecil has been 33 years at the Bar.
Justice Boucaufc, on circuit at Port Augusta
(S.A.), having given a youth five years' hard
labor for horse-stealing, proceeded to hear a
further oharge against him for a brief breaking
out of a country jail while awaiting trial. The
Adelaide dailies of Nov. 19 say that counsel
“made an eloquent appeal on behalf of the
prisoner, who is only 17$ years of age ”; where-
upon the gentle Boucaut added on to his five years,
two years more, with 6 months of it in irons!
Some day an Australian Judge, in his haste to get
the work finished and get home to his pictures or
books or collection of beetles, will pass a sentence
of 1000 years solitary confinement in chains.
M‘Eacharn’s thanksgiving dinner to his
fat and greasy citizens of Melbourne displayed
the art of public dining—the technique of which
is to tip the waiter half a dollar, hide a bottle of
wine under your chair and lead the strenuous life.
Prominent citizens, for solid battle after food and
liquor, can beat Sunday-school picnickers every
time. It was a charming sight to see Fitzroy
Smith beaming with joy because he had one more
currant than Councillor Backache. The menu was
a Skinner masterpiece. English was too common
and French was too difficult for the citizens, so
there was a compromise, thus: “ Boudin of
Chicken Printanniere,” “BoiledTurkeyau Blanc,”
“ Almond Gateau, ” etc. There was a special note
requesting the guests to remain for the speeches.
It was only fair that after they had taken the
boiled oiseau of Skinner they should wait for the
half-baked speech of the gaud mayor.
Says George Reid, in his appeal to the
people of New South Wales: “I make no secret
of the fact that I cannot get reform unless I get
more strength, and unless New South Wales is
prepared to stand by me to get the policy that has
prevented the emigration of her people ... I must
abandon the contest.” Presumably George Reid
means the policy for which he was responsible
when, in 1895-6-7, he had all the strength that any
Premier could wish for. And this is how, accord-
ing to Coghlan, Reid’s own statistician, he “pre-
vented the emigration of the people ” :
1895-6-7 .. Excess of Departures over Arrivals .. 4980
And this, according to the same statistician, is the
movement during the two years ending December
31st, 1902, under that policy which George Reid
now wants low South Wales to swop for his
own:—
1901-2 .. Excess of Arrivals over Departures .. 77
It isn’t much of a gain, perhaps, but there was the
drought working against anything better, and
there were the evil consequences of the Reid
rigime to be got rid of; and yet, in spite of all, it
was. about 5000 times a better result than the Reid
period shows, anyhow.
While the N.S. W. Freetraders (who never
practise Freetrade when in office) are proving so
conclusively that Protection and Preferential
trade with Britain can’t go together, Seddon has
just shown in highly protected Maoriland that
they do. Seddon takes the bull by the bowsprit
by keeping the tariff rates against Britain just
where they were, but raising them against
foreigners. It is reckoned that this will divert
£300,000 worth of trade from foreign countries to
Britain, and for a community of 800,000 people
that is a large concession. Whether Seddon
is wise or not he is at least attempting real
imperialism ; first he looks after the part of the
empire called Maoriland, and next after the rest
of the empire. The Freetrade idea is to put all
the empire’s enemies on an equality with the
empire, and to look after the Chinaman and the
Russian equally with our own people.
From London Daily Excess on tha
Chamberlain tariff proposals:
There is talk of the men in the lower grades of the
civil service, like the postmen, opposing the tariff because
they will not get higher wages out of it. Such opposi-
tion would indicate shortsighted selfishness. How do
they know that their wages»will not increase ? Their-
wages are regulated by the general level of wages in
other employments, and if that general level rises, their
rates will also rise. Even if they did not, since the cost
of living will not be increased, these civil servants would
have no object in opposing the tariff. Compare this
alleged attitude with the attitude of trade unionists
when some other trade union is engaged in a strike.
When the colliers are striking for higher wages, the
engineers contribute to the colliers’ strike fund, notwith-
standing that the success of the coal strike means that
the engineers will have to pay higher prices for their
coal. They think of the general interests of labor rather
than of the price of any particular commodity. How
much more then, should they support the tariff,
which is in the interests of the men wlmse wages
are kept down by foreign competition, since the result of
such support will not increase the cost of living, and will
most likely result in indirect benefit to those who give
their support ?
A fine analogy—that between Protection and
Trades-unionism !
Stated by Sydney D.T., with an incom-
prehensible air of triumphant argument, that, in
the Australian Parliament,
Temptations to vote for duties in favor of industries in
their own States were altogether too strong. Mr.
Solomons, of South Australia, was a Freetrader on
everything but salt. And he voted for a duty on
salt to protect an industry in his own ' State.
He made no secret of it. “ I see other members,”
he said, “ voting for Protective duties on goods
manufactured in their States, and why shouldn’t I for
goods manufactured in mine?" Some of the Queens-
landers, while professing Freetrade, voted for duties on
bananas and coffee, because both are produced in Queens-
land.
Exactly 1 Solomon, as a good Freetrader, howled
that it was Freetrade which made industries
grow, and supplied work and wages, and kept the
worker’s wife and children from starvation. But
he knew all the time that it was a lie. When it
was a question of his own State’s industry he
didn’t vote for Freetrade, so that his own State’s
industry would boom ; he voted for Protection.
Either Solomon voted to wilfully destroy the salt
industry of his own State by Protection, or else
he voted to wilfully and maliciously destroy the
industries of other States by Freetrade. One way
or the other he was a traitor and a public enemy.
Questions tabled in the N.S.W. Assembly
illustrative of the difficulty which an M.P. finds
nowadays in hustling for prominence:—
Mr. Mackenzie to ask the Colonial Secretary,—
(1) What were the reasons that Mr. Mackenzie was
requested by his Department to limit the number of
nominations for the position of Justice of the Peace to
six?
(2) For what reason were only four of the nominations
accepted, while one of the two not accepted was a nomina-
tion at the request of the police for the requirements of
public business?
(3) For what reason did the Government appoint six
gentlemen who were not nominated by Mr. Mackenzie
after requesting Mr. Mackenzie to nominate only six ?
(4) On whose recommendation were the six following
gentlemen appointed Bell, Robert, Albert-road, Strath-
field ; Bell, William Fife, Strathfield; Cutler, William
Edgar, Oampsie, Canterbury; Perry, J. S., Liverpool;
Gibson, William John, Padstow Park, East Hills?
(5) Is he aware that Mr. (one of the Jay-Pays) at
the last municipal election at Bankstown, made accusa-
tions against the Returning Officer, for which he had to
apologise in all the leading newspapers to save legal pro-
ceedings?
It’s to worry about J.’s.P. and other insect
matters that the N.S.W. member neglects his real
duties.

The Inky Way.
The S.M. Herald actually makes a move at last with-
out getting a lead from the D.T. Both have been running
a “ Bicycles for Sale and Hire” advt. heading- for years.
The Herald now adds “and Motor Oars.” This daring
will yet lead the old lady into trouble.
* * * *
The snippet craze has reached London financial jour-
nalism now—a Financial Tit-Bits has appeared. It
promises Bull-Cohen that it will provide him with all the
money-market news he needs, reduced to sausage-meat
and spiced with jokes. And all for a ha’penny.
In the old days of the country press there was a subtle
freemasonry, which extended from the editor right down
to the junior devil. This was—it is no more. In one of
the larger Queensland towns the organ of the great
Liberal party was owned by an irascible politician, who
thought himself a statesman, and compelled his paper to
record the lie every issue. The rival paper was run by a
syndicate of Conservatives, with cash, but not much
intelligence. The politician hired a smart editor, who
drank ; and the enemy secured the services of a yet
smarter pressman, who also drank. One Monday morn-
ing the enemy came out with a blistering leader on the
Liberal organ, its proprietor, and all its appurtenances.
And the proprietor enjoined his servant —he never called
members of his staff by any other term —to “ scarify that
beardless boy across the street." The editor felt tired
that day, and went down the street for a preliminary
drink, and there met his rival on the same errand. How
the day was spent neither combatant could tell, but the
man who had the contract On hand was awakened by a
sleepy voice at his side muttering, “ Wonder where the
devil I’ve got to now?” “D if I know; but”—
striking a match—“it looks like the Tattersall’s stable.
Wonder what day it is ? ” They crawled round to the
bar and had another, and then sought information.
“ It’s nine o’clock of a Monday night,” related the
publican. “ Immortal Caesar 1 ” exclaimed the drunker
of the two, “ and old G told me to flay you alive in
to-morrow’s issue. And I’m too drunk, old man; too
damnably drunk to flay anybody." “ That's all right,”
said the less drunk of the two; “ I’ve nothing to do
to-night. Come along and I’ll flay myself in your paper.”
The article which he wrote about himself was a monu-
ment of vituperation, and the politician was a glad man
that morning. Things like that don’t happen now, as a
rule. The wild irresponsibleness of the early days is
wearing off in the struggle to keep the Public Debt
afloat.
* » • *
“ D’Harum ” : Advertised in a typical country rag for
three months once, and then wrote the proprietor telling
him to cancel my ad. A few days after I received a note
asking me to allow him to continue it gratis till he got
another in its place.
* * * *
Remarkable how little the character of Sydney Morn-
ing Herald’s London correspondence has changed in 50
years. Dr. Lang wrote to the Herald on January 13,
1851 : “ Your London correspondent, Mr. Michie, whose
general style and standing as a writer strongly remind
me of the compositions of a lady’s waiting-maid, has
thought proper to make himself very merry at my ex-
pense.” And it is still a waiting-maid at the task.
• • • *
11 Jones ” : Wanted—a stand made against the demand
of the churches—especially those run by country sky-
pilots—to have their printing and advertising done at
cut rates. Have just got an order for a local concert, to
be run on show-night ; tickets 600 at Bs. 6d. the lot;
and advt., two insertions, that pans out at the rate of
Is. Id. an inch, all told 11 Why do Idoit ? Well, if I
don’t, the chap in the next town will jump at the chance
to lower the dignity and prestige of the local organ—
that’s mine —at an even lower rate. Then, to add insult
to injury, I am expected to buy a ticket in the concern,
which increases my loss some more, besides inserting
sundry puff pars. Now, if that goggle-eyed reptile in
the next town only had some principle about him, our
papers might both be a credit to the district where we
hang out, besides being decently dressed ourselves ; but
the spectacled reptile won’t listen to reason. He is a
snake by nature, and has all the dignity of one.
• * * *
The fourth commandment of the daily paper journalist;
Remember the Sabbath day to keep it for labor wholly.
Six days shalt thou grind out copy and do double work,
and the seventh is the Sabbath when the sermons come.
On it thou shalt do the churches and write up the matter
of the bygone night. The Lord rested on the seventh
day ; but the Lord did not have to go to press for Mon-
day’s issue.
* * • *
Black-and-white men receive poor encouragement from
most papers in Australia. In Victoria the weeklies use
photographs ; and the “ great country weekly,” the
Australasian, which has no excuse on the score of
poverty, not only disdains to publish local drawings,
but actually clips and reproduces drawings with jokes
from English and American papers.
* * * *
“ Rhaupo ” : Re accident to a galley of type in the
Eden Propeller’s office. Dungog (N.S.W.) Chronicle
presented a particularly pallid appearance as it struggled
into print one night some four months ago. A pet cocka-
too, on a Sabbath morning, effected an entrance to the
composing-room and scratched on to the floor eight
columns of brevier reading-matter and seven of standing
advts. Excited comps, and pressmen spent half next
morning in a chase after the cockatoo, bent on wreaking
vengeance, but their efforts met with cocky’s exasperat-
ing, oft-repeated phrase, “ There is no luck about the
house.” Resurrected stereos, of bygone customers graced
the pages that night, patent-pill vendors got free advts.,
while local advertisers’customers were told to “Watch
this Space.” [“Watch this Space” is a very dull and
commonplace way out of the difficulty. Why not state
honestly “Smith’s Advt. destroyed by Cockatoo”;
“Jones’s Notice wrecked by Malign Fowl”; “this Par
obliterated by Bird ” ; “ Pretty Cocky been Here ” ;
“Advt. destroyed by Flying Rooster”; “this Item sus-
pended owing to Reprehensible Animal,” and so on ?
Variety has its charm. —Ed. B.]
* * * *
There are two combinations of pressmen at Christchurch
(M.L.)—a Journalists’ Union and the Journalists’ Insti-
tute. One crowd entertained the other lately, and it
might be imagined that among such a galaxy of geniuses
there would be a feast of reason and a flow of soul. The
nature of the flow may be judged by the chief toasts,
which were: “ Sport and Sportsmen,” “ The Boxing
Association,” and “ The Arts and Sciences,” last being
responded to by a horse doctor.
A “ valued subscriber ’’ writes to the editor of Mudgee
Guardian (N.S.W.): “ Would you please go to the Court
House and register my two dogs ? I haven’t any money,
but if you pay the ss. it will make £3 I owe you, and you
can stop my paper, and I’ll pay you when I can.”
* * * *
Recent yarn in Sydney D.T., “ My Princess Inez,” was
from the pen of Mr. Hickie, one of the sub-editorial staff.
It was cleverly plotted and moved briskly to its end—
good enough to encourage Mr. Hickie to try his hand
again in the same line.
* • * •
“Barney": Apropos the commercial reporter who
quoted the price for seed pollard, and the “ stud wether”
gentleman (8., 3/9/’O3). South Australia was once cruelly
deprived of an even better sensation. The shipping re-
porter was young and fresh, and his slow, mild bleat used
to aggravate the profane and case-hardened shipping
agents. One of them, in a burst of rage, added to the
list of imports by a certain vessel, “4000 post-holes for
the S.A. Government, and 100 doorways." If the old man
hadn’t chanced to look at the proofs S.A. would have felt
better next morning than it had done for years. “I
wish,” said the agent, sadly, when he heard the facts,
“ that I’d added a dozen long holes to go in the centre of
cannon. Then the old man would have killed that fellow
for certain."
* * * •
“ Minion ” : Should a new daily paper become a
fact in Melbourne, let’s hope that it will not have
its chances of popularity damned by the hideous
practice adopted by the Argus of printing its
paragraph ads. in the same type as the news matter, and
continued on in the same columns. Have heard more
blasphemy vented on the proprietors, managers, and all
connected with the Freetrade journal than would fill a
gasometer over those catch ads. that start off by announc-
ing a revolution, or something of the sort, and then
switch off suddenly on to the merits of Quack’s Quaffles
as a sovereign cure for Blue Devils.
« • • * -
Recent prison statistics reveal the strange fact that
editors play no prominent part in the world of crime.
Members of Parliament, clergymen, bankers, &c. —not
a trade or profession has escaped undesirable police
attention except that of the editor. In neither England
nor the United States is there an editor “ doing time ”
for a felony.
• • • •
“ Vic." : There are probably few press men remaining
who have any recollection of Wm. Jaffray, a distin-
guished Melbourne journalist in the early sixties. He
was a good dramatic and art critic, and one of the
prettiest writers the Melbourne press has known, also a
Bohemian without the physical strength to play the part
successfully. When in the House of Commons gallery
he took the last “turn” of Robert Peel’s famous
speech in moving the repeal of the Corn Laws, and was
afterwards sub-editor of London Daily News. An agent
of David Syme’s engaged him as sub-editor of Melb. Age,
and he came out to the position, notwithstanding the
advice of actor G. V. Brooke, then in London, not to
accept it. No man ever took his goods to a more unsuit-
able shop, and the connection was broken in less than a
year. Jaffray, when sub-editing, once said to me:
“ I hate coming down to this work just as heartily
as Albert Smith hated going to the Egyptian Hail
every night to deliver what he called his accursed
lecture on “ Mont Blanc." Argus proprietor Ed. Wilson,
arrived in Melbourne at this time on a visit. He had
known Jaffray’s father, a Church of England parson, in
the old country, and Jaffray had also written a pamphlet
for Wilson in London. This acquaintance led to appoint-
ment as Argus sub-editor, but Jaffray could not stand
the hard grind of Melbourne morning newspaper work,
and he eventually drifted back to Fog City, where he
died of small-pox a couple of years later. Before leaving
Melbourne he distinguished himself by falling in love with
Emma Howson, then in the first flush of her youth and
vocal talent, and by inducing Barry Sullivan, at the
old Royal, to produce his blank-verse tragedy, “ The
Gladiator of Ravenna,” translated from the German. It
had what is called a success of esteem for a week or a
fortnight, and there was a decent benefit for the author,
who returned thanks with his eye in a sling, in conse-
quence of having fallen out of a cab subsequent to a ban-
quet he gave to the actors and actresses.
In North Adelaide there is a house called
with a patent-medicine name. How long before
“Blue Beans Mansion” or “Pale Pills Villa”
will be built ?
Since the Yankee went in for practical
Imperialism he has shown an aptitude for robbery
which promises a successful career at the business.
The Yanks wanted land for a canal in Colombia ;
Colombia’s price was too high. Now the State of
Panama (Colombia is a Federation) has started a
revolution against the Federal Government, and
the Yankee promptly “recognises” the ,Pro-
visional Government and will smash up Colombia
if it attempts to quell the revolt. By-and-by'e the
State of Panama will give the Yankee the canal
land he wants at his own price, and all will be
well. Britain or Germany or Russia couldn’t
have done the job more neatly.
Mr. MacNaughton, a suckling barrister of
Hobart, is out for the Reps, and has original
notions of politics:
The teaching of history and the teaching of science
most emphatically and clearly prove that, as the black
man cannot compete in manual labor with the white man
in a temperate clime, so the white man cannot compete
with the black man in a tropical clime. To me White
Australia seems a puerile fancy. I thought as a child
what a splendid thing it would be if all horses could be
painted blue. It seems to me that that was an absolutely
puerile fancy. It is not much more puerile than the idea
of White Australia.
Blue-Horse MacNaughton was evidently an ex-
traordinarily silly child, and he doesn’t seem to
have learned much since.
Tasmanian Parliament has, grudgingly
enough, passed the Women’s Suffrage Bill. The
speeches, especially in the Upper House, were up
to the usual Dodery standard. Many members
stated frankly that they didn’t think women had
brains enough to vote, but as the Federal Parlia-
ment had given women the franchise, and Van-
demonia’s Dry Bones never are able to do anything
on their own, they supposed they must follow suit.
Then these admirers of the Federal Parliament
refused to abolish sixteenth - century property
qualifications and tagged on a clause declaring
women ineligible for seats in either House of
Parliament. The Assembly showed a disposition
to shy at this bog legislation, but after considering
the hopelessness of getting any really sensible Bill
through the Dodery House it accepted the
measure. So a number of M.’sL.C., who have
declared that women haven’t brains enough to
vote, will shortly address them as intelligent
electors and ask them for their allegedly bnuuleaj
suffrage.
THE MODEL JOURNALIST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
(Referring to the proposal of Pulitzer, the American
newspaper proprietor, for the establishment of a School
of Journalism, the S.M. Herald says “If a School of
Journalism can further the honorable traditions and
principles of journalism as it is known to Britons and
fostered bv the atmosphere and traditions of the British
press, it is surely welcome to try the experiment.")
Young men who would succeed
As pressmen, unimperilled
By foolish notions, read
The Gospel of the Herald—
Be safe, be slow, be sure,
Take nothing upon rumor,
And evermore be pure
And wholesome in your humor.
Be sparing in your jests—
Tis safer to be solemn ;
For Vested Interests
There is no Funny Column.
Take views as calm and high
As Newton did, or Herschel;
But always keep your eye
Upon the Things Commercial.
Cast Creeds of Change afar,
No quarter to them giving ;
That things are as they are
Is our excuse for living.
Go dead against Reform—
We stand or fall together—
While we are snug and warm,
Who cares about cold weather ?
Remember Property,
Like the great goddess Isis,
“ Is, has been, and shall be ”
The crux of every crisis.
But let the Public see,
When you have abstract reasons,
Ilow liberal you can be
In proper times and seasons.
Proclaim undying war,
In leaders fierce and murky,
'Gainst Russian Horrors, or
The Tyranny of Turkey.
Write boldly, cut and thrust,
’Gainst wrong in some unseen land ;
D.nounce the Blubber Trust
That paralyses Greenland :
Say ’tis the fault of kings,
The Balkan Tragic Drama ;
And write some bitter things
About Thibet’s Grand Lama.
Be hard on Ancient Rome—
Things dead, or at a distance,
Are safe —but take at home
The line of least resistance.
Pose as the People’s Friend,
Its candid, calm adviser—
But never dare offend
The Lord God Advertiser.
Then you will happy be
And will fulfil your mission,
And well uphold the free
Old British Press Tradition.
N.S.W.
V.J.D.
AN INKY WAY-FARER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Humping a swag in the old bush way,
Chasing the trade of his ’prentice years ;
Monolines ruined him, so he’ll say,
Humping a swag in the old bush way.
He comps on my paper for p’r’aps a day,
And getting his cash to the north land he steer 3,
Humping a swag on the inky way,
Chasing the trade of his ’prentice years.
GORDON BENNETT.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Is Warner going to repeat the offer Maclaren
made for equalising, as far as possible, the advantage
obtained from the first choice of bat or ball in the Test
matches? Mac proposed that the two captains should toss
for the right to take the wicket or the field in the first
game and again in the third, the other side having the
privilege in the second and fourth games. Under this
arrangement the luck of the toss would be equally
divided until they came to the final, and then they would
toss again. There is absolutely no reason why the
captains should permit the possibility of one side profiting
by the result of the toss in every one of the so-called
Tests. Truly, a side sometimes loses a match through
winning the toss owing to an unexpected change in the
weather, or a miscalculation as to the future conduct of
a wicket which promises to improve, or otherwise. But
in a general way the side that gets first choice of a hard
wicket on a fine day gains a big advantage. It is,
perhaps, a greater advantage to be able to send the other
side to bat first on a sodden wicket when the glass is
rising and the sun is busy. Under the old conservative
cricket rule of One Toss One Match the luck may be all
on one side through a series of contests.
* * * *
Some of Our Boys are nob as young as they
were. Wicketkeeper Lilley (36) is considerably the oldest
among the Englishmen, yet he is a trifle younger than
Kelly. Noble has been alive a little longer than Warner,
Knight, Tyldesley or Relf. Laver and Howell are two
years older than Hirst and Hayward. We think of Olem.
Hill and Trumper as mere boys, but they are men of fully
the same age as Rhodes, Arnold and Bosanquet, who were
all born in 1877. Foster is 25, Strudwick and Fielder not
yet 23. In the matter of new blood the visitors will be at
no disadvantage with the Australians.
* * * *
If it is even money on the Englishmen winning
the rubber in the tests, it ought to be at least 5 to 4 on
them for the first game of the series. In Sydney next
month they will have the advantage in point of condition
over their comparatively unpractised opponents ; the
strength of their bowling will not be familiar to the
enemy, and the chances are that the first selection of an
All-Australia Eleven will be improved upon later in the
light of experience. Each of the three last visiting teams
succeeded in winning the initial test-match, though two
of them were subsequently beaten four times each.
Strangely enough, Stoddart’s 1894-5 team, which won the
rubber, would have lost the first match in Sydney under
fair weather conditions. Peel and Briggs pulled the
game out of the wet on the morning of the fifth day.
* * * *
Warner, by declaring the Englishmen’s innings
closed at Adelaide and Melbourne, before the weak,
tail-end batsmen had gone in, was practically falsifying
the team’s aggregate batting average. Eight wickets for
443 at Melbourne gave them an average of 65, whereas if
Strudwick and Fielder had come and gone whilst 10 runs
were added to the score (a fair allowance for two very
inferior batsmen) the average would have been reduced
to 45. Evidently Warner is bent on accomplishing the
best possible figures for the eleven for the whole tour.
His policy of closing the innings to suit the average-
provided, of course, that he is master of the situation—
is legitimate but very misleading in its results on paper*
* * « *
“B.”: The result of the three State matches
leaves Australia wondering where to get a bowling side
for the tests. The S.A. and Vic. trundling was tripe to
the visitors, and though N.S.W. got Warner’s lot out
cheaply enough, all things considered, they had some
luck. Want of variety is the weakness of N.S.W. bowlers.
Fisher, by the way, was picked as a bowler, and an
experiment at that, and Noble should have attempted to
justify the experiment by bowling him right out. But
he was used sparingly—M’Beth 38 overs, Howell 20,
Noble 34, Hopkins 21, and Fisher 11, in the first innings
of the visitors.
* * * »
It is alleged that a quaint piece of snobbery
was perpetrated at the Melbourne Cricket Ground on the
occasion of the recent match. A kingly-looking pusson is
Baid to have come up to the press-box with tickets of in-
vitation to lunch, and inquired which paper eaoh man
represented. If it was a Tory paper a ticket was pre-
sented with great impressiveness, but when it was a
democratic paper the kingly-looking pusson cleared his
throat and passed on. The Bulletin has this tale on
middling authority.
* * * •
Nine thousand witnessed opening day’s play of
N.S.W.-England match Friday. Home side lost the toss,
and batted first on a wicket which, consequent upon rain
over night, w'as decidedly bad, and though it improved
quickly, batsmen generally shaped poorly. Trumper
and Duff did well at the outset, but, bar “ Sid” Gregory,
none of the others rose above mediocrity, and most were
bagged through making miserable swipes at Rhodes.
Tactics generally were poor, for the policy was not to
open shoulders, but to hold wickets till the queerness of
the pitch had disappeared. Runs totalled 108, of which
three men—Trumper (46), S. Gregory (25), and Duff (14) —
contributed 85. Though Rhodes brought more work to
bear in his deliveries, they didn’t appear quite the
puzzlers Arnold’s were—rising very awkwardly from the
pitch. Englishmen did the right thing, when they came
along, by not bothering about run-getting so much as
keeping bails up till the wicket dried. Things conse-
quently dragged weightily during the first hour of play
(once only 11 runs were made in 35 minutes), but when
the time arrived Tyldesley and Foster made things merry
to such an extent that at stumps-draw scores showed
runs had been piled up at the rate of one a minute, and
totalled 178 for three wickets—Tyldesley (not out) 76,
Warner 46, and Foster 35. Warner was lucky in being
missed frequently.
* * * •
England-N.S.W. match ended Monday in de-
cisive defeat of latter by an innings and 10 runs. Visitors
have now thoroughly beaten all three crack cricket
States in succession, for S.A. game was unquestionably
England’s. Despite that winners of latest match had the
luck from toss to conclusion, they out-played their oppo-
nents always. Rarely is it that one fieldsman gets the
chances Lilley had on Friday, and rarer still is it that he
makes the most of them—four catches from four opportu-
nities is something decidedly uncommon. Catching on both
sides was a treat, and fielding generally reached high-
class, as evidence those three brilliant one-hand holds by
Howell, Trumper, and Braund in one day. Lesson of
three struggles already witnessed is that the Englishmen
are here to win, and they’ll take no risks at all; when
they are out they’ll be so because the bowling or fielding
beats their well-iaid plans, or through some stroke of ill-
luck. Tyldesley, Braund and Warner stonewalled with
depressing monotony till the waited-for ball came along
—and it had to be soft indeed.
• * * *
The desperate situation and some friendly pres-
sure will probably bring bowler Trumble into the Com-
monwealth Eleven to defend those ashes which Warner’s
combination threatens to get away with. It isn’t disin-
clination which causes Trumble to hold aloof, so much as
a promise to his emplo3’ers that he wouldn’t again seek
leave for cricket purposes. The crack bowler holds office
in a leading Melbourne financial institution.
» * * *
Clem Hill’s first innings for S.A. against Vic.,
Friday, was a remarkable display quite equal to best he
has shown. For 3h. 24m. Hill batted freely and baffled
the bowling badly ; seven fours were scored. The com-
mand of the batsman over the ball, as well as the variety
of strokes from opening to close, were a treat, especially
as he never gave the merest semblance of a chance till
Monfries stumped him cleverly for 147. Efforts of other
10 were completely overshadowed, albeit Jennings
notched 37 in taking fashion.
* * * *
Jones —the ex-Australian XI. “ express ”
trundler—is reaping a harvest of wickets amongst the
less-experienced batsmen in Westralia. Recently, he
captured 11 wickets for 14 runs, and in addition scored
60 not out. Of course, it was in a Brewery Association
competition, which, to a certain extent, perhaps, put
fire into the “demon” trundler’s eye, and Herculean
strength to his arm.
In front rank of Australian cricket comers
(Judged by recent performances) is 19-year-old S.A.
colt Claude Jennings. Jennings’ stock of smites is
unusual—cut, cover, and on-side strokes are particularly
good, while behind it all there’s a deal of enthusiasm and
heaps of energy.
* * * *
Melbourne C.C. is again bringing out H.
Carpenter, of Essex, as coach to the club. There are
scores of waning players in Australia who undoubtedly
know as much as the imported one about cricket —
especially as regards play- suitable for Australian
wickets. When it was noted that Carpenter had been
engaged, Harry Graham took on a billet as coach to
Dunedin (M.L.) High School; thus Victoria loses its
best batsman, mainly for the want of recognition of
Australian worth
* * * *
If S.A. should beat Victoria (match is unfin-
ished as The Bulletin goes to press) it will be a refreshing
change for the Crow-eaters—as not since '97 have they
tasted of the sweets of a triumph over their neighbors—
Victoria having won all intervening 10 matches.
• * * *
Trustees of Sydney Cricket Ground won’t have
N.S.W. A.A.A. foot races run during half-time of impor-
tant football matches —the practice being, they say,
“ undesirable.” No doubt because onlookers stay in their
seats to watch the race instead of giving the bar a turn.
They are astute people, who never lose a chance to rake
in the coin, those S.O.G. authorities. Rugby Union,
which rents the ground for the time being, considers
A.A.A. events anything but undesirable.
* » * *
The recent arrest of a Melbourne sweep-pro-
moter produced some instructive results. Evidence
shows that investors had Buckley’s chance of getting a
prize for their “ bob,” the 1000 tickets on sale being num-
bered from 4001 to 6000 in an alleged “ consultation of
5000 members,” and the “prizes” being allotted to the
numbers ranging from 1 to 4000. In one case the
detectives discovered that one prize on the results-list
had fallen to a ticket which had actually been printed,
but as to the whereabouts of that ticket there was no
evidence. It probably stayed in the promoter’s possession,
or was given to his mother as part payment for his board.
* * * *
When even the Arous began “slinging off” at
Irvine for his Tote suppression fiasco it must have dawned
on that cool, calm cove that his masterly coup had
utterly gone adrift. For 10 long days 60 policemen were
kept twiddling their thumbs at Wren’s tote-shop whilst
that individual ran his business in new premises round
the corner. Twelve constables and a sergeant—thirteen
men, no less —still hold the fort and keep their batons
ready to subdue the obstreperous Wren the moment he
comes to claim his vacant woodyard. It would be a
shrieking joke but for the fact that the Old Age
Pensioners, the charities and the sweated public servants
will have to pay for Irvine’s incapacity.
* * * •
Wren’s tote, which the Melbourne police are
quite unable to discover though everybody else knows
where it is, did good business on Williamstown races.
Earl of Oarrick, which started at 7 to 1 on the course,
paid on the “ tote ” 256 to 1 ; Billali, the Cup winner,
quoted at 15’s, returned 38 to 1, and Rustaff, laid by the
bookmakers at 10 to 1, started at totalisator odds of
240 to 1.

Breeding has received a great fillip in Maori-
land during the past two or three years, and now another
stud is to be established, styled the N.Z. Stud 00. A
sum of £2500 has been spent on 320 acres of fine land at
Kereru, Wellington district ; a thousand or so set aside
for a stallion at the English December sales, £4OOO for
brood mares, and a Maoriland stallion with a record will
complete the outfit. The directors are all Wellington
men.
• • « •
This year’s M.L. Cup will long linger in memory
owing to the sensational incidents associated with it.
Firstly, quite a number of well-backed neddies brought
joy to the festive heart of Mo by breaking down. Then
the bona-fides of favorite Roseal’s entry was inquired
into. It wasn't until the eve of the race that the owner
of crack Achilles decided to start his flier, and the great
crowd who follow the brainless pursuit of gambling were
kept on tenter hooks. Then on the da3', bookmaker
Moss’ grey nag smashed all records (3.27 1-5), and his
jockey, M‘Coombe, and Cameron (up on Roseal) were
passed out for two 3 - ears and one year respectively for
foul riding. For some inscrutable reason the erudite
stewards decided not to disqualify the winning horse,
and violent remarks flew through the ambient air in con-
sequence. It was a most glaring case; M'Coombe cannoned
on to Achilles in the final rush, and latter struck Wairiki.
The rule on the subject lays down the law clearly, but the
word “may ” gives stewards discretionary power and they
clutched it for all it was worth. The rule is practically
the same as that under which Tim Swiveller was dis-
qualified in Caulfield Cup years ago, and Maorilanders
have been so used to seeing prads passed out under
similar conditions that the decision caused heated
argument.
The unspeakable folly of asking 4-year-old
Achilles (carrying 9.6) to make the running in a long two-
mile gallop was explained subsequently by the fact that
rider Jenkins had been ill and wasn’t strong enough to
control him. The horse’s performance, and that of the
other 4-year-old, Wairiki (9.2) was great—all things con-
sidered. Four-year-old Patron won Melbourne Cup under
9.4, but this year's M.L. event was a great deal swifter.
They put up Newmarket Handicap time in the first six
furlongs, and went along at a proportionately strong bat
to the finish.

* * * *
Tyro Stakes, at A. J.C. September meet, Satur-
day, induced a field of 18 well-bred youngsters. Punters
sorted brilliant Bee Bee’s stable chum, Vitula, out as
best of the field, and took 5 to 4 about his chance, while
3’s and, later, 2’s were available Sarto, who had won
twice at Fleminglon recently, and carried a 101 b. penalty
in consequence. Barrier rise saw Sarto away with lead-
ing division till, singling out, he galloped home, lengths
ahead of Vitula (second) and Mr. H. C. White’s Simmer
colt Hadrian, a poor third. Trainer Kelso (with the idea
of not putting the colt into work till later) didn’t enter
him for coming A.J.O. December Stakes, otherwise, on
form so far exhibited, he would have given Bee Bee and
co. a great shaking. Sarto is by Positano (imp.) from
Morisca, and only cost 50gs. as a yearling.
* * * *
South Sea Islander Alick Wickham, whose
portrait appears in this issue, promises to be a big
swimming factor this season—over sprint distances only
Atop of breaking his own world’s record (25 3-ssec.) for
50yds. by 3-ssec., he, on 31st ult., got through the
hundred in 59}, and Saturday last, at Rushcutter’s Bay
baths (Sydney), essayed the task of wiping out Dick
Oavill’s world’s best (58 3-5), put up at Hornsey, England,
last year. After a great effort he finished l-ssec. worse
than the figures assailed and in time (58 4-5) which
parallels the fastest ever accomplished in Australia —
Oavill’s swim at Farmer’s Domain Baths, Sydney, last
March. A prominent English authority prophesied a few
years ago that the minute would never be broken for ICO
yards, but Cavill has already done it four times, Wickham
twice, and Lane once, while the best to the credit of a
swimmer outside Australia is Britisher .1. H. Derbyshire’s
lmin. dead, achieved a few months after Lane had done
the same thing—also in the Cold Country. A great
struggle is looked for when Cavill and Wickham meet,
but those who know what Cavill is doing express confi-
dence he will respond to anything not phenomenal.
* * * *
The re-engagement of Major Taylor for the
Sydney “Thousand” meeting is a good move by the
promoting body of that carnival, since it will afford
Australians a peep at Lawson and Taylor—two of the
greatest exponents of cycling in the world —of whom over-
sea papers have frequently said so much. Good as
Lawson is proving himself to be, still Taylor’s riding in
Australia last year was so brilliant that it seems impos-
sible to put it in the shade. Taylor’s form on the
Continent on his return from Australia was of such kind
that the organisers of the big meeting were intent on
bringing him out. The dusky flier’s record for the
recently-concluded Continental tour was 28 firsts, 21
seconds, and 7 thirds, being unplaced but once in 57
starts —and this against the cream of the bike world.
* * * *
A. W. Cutler, the N.S.W. shootist, who put up
a world's record in winning the King’s Prize at Vic. Rifle
Association meeting, last week, is a non-smoker, like W.
G. Willoughby, who captured the Syme Prize (£100) for
grand aggregate score. Abstinence from tobacco and
alcohol will presently be recognised by everyone as
essentials to excellence at all games of skill where the
eyes and the hands are equally concerned. Champions
have been quoted who went in for moderate drinking and
smoking, but they were merely examples of the fact
that good nerves are very little affected by reasonable
indulgence in pipe or bowl. Besides, habits of strict self-
denial were formerly rare among sportsmen (out of train-
ing), and the moderately steady man was then the
steadiest man on the field. Lots of boys nowadays are
too manly to worry about learning to smoke and drink.
Especially the Australian boys.
• • * *
Inter-State bowling rubber in Melbourne (Mon-
day, Tuesday, Wednesday next week), ’tween N.S.W. and
Victoria looks sure for the home combination- for while
visiting side is the weakest team ever sent away, others
are the strongest lot Victoria has at any time got
together—all exceptionally good bowlers, champions in
their respective positions, and mostly winners of big
events.
• • * *
Bathurst (N.S.W.) crack shot, Private A. W.
Cutler, staggered Melbourne rifledom last week. He
gathered “The King’s” with a grand total of 325 —con-
siderably ahead of last year’s winning figures, and a world's
best. Conditions of competition are exactly the same
as at Bisley. Cutler’s aggregate is 14 better than that of
the winner of “The King's ” at Bisley this year, and 19
ahead of the successful total in 1902. New record-holder
is 20 and has been shooting six years Value of each of
Cutler’s shots was 4.58 out of 5 allowed for a bull’s-eye.

Bob Turner (holder) and Lyn. Truscott (most
promising of second pug flight) fight for light-weight
supremacy at Sydney Gaiety A. C. Monday night went to
former. Through half the trip it was a fairly even and
very exciting thing, but subsequently Turner assumed
mastery and kept adding to his stock of points till the
20th round closed, despite other man’s desperate efforts
in the last 3min.
• • * •
Yankee middleweight pug., Frank Nicholls,
bumped the anticipated set back at Sydney Golden Gate
A. 0., Saturday night. One “Spider” Kelly accom-
plished the task in the fourth round, and never allowed
his man a look in once from the jump. Despite his
vanquishment Nicholls shaped much better than any one
of the three or four from the bounder Republic who have
visited Australia during the past several months.
• * * *
Cabled Monday that whilom English top-
notcher and once much-heralded sure world’s middle-
weight champion bruiser, Ted Pritchard, died in his 35th
year. Eleven years ago Australian Jim Hall burst the
Pritchard bubble in the open, four miles from Brighton
(Eng.), for £lOOO a side and some ring bets at 7 to 4 on the
Britisher. Arrangements were elaborate and luxurious.
Roped square was pitched in the yard of the training
establishment of Hall’s backer, the late Squire Abingdon
Baird ; and Baird provided champagne luncheon in one
room cigars and wine ad lib. in another. An offered
£lOOO purse didn’t tempt the pugs to fight in London.
Battle was sensational and punishment severe on both
sides, but Hall crushed his man during the 4th round.
Then Pritchard sank into obscurity.
* * * *
Bruise at Kalgoorlie (W.A.), the other day,
between forefront Australian lightweights, Bob Rollo
and Tim Hegarty, didn’t reach expectations. Hegarty
was top-puncher and tactician all the way till he spread-
eagled Rollo in the seventh round and won. Rollo never
was a possible champion, there being too much make-
believe about his love for the game. At the Sydney
Gaiety A.O. once, Jack Blackmore challenged Rollo, but
latter declined, alleging Blackmore “ smothered” (i.e.,
held his arms as a shield for his face without attempting
to hit) too much, whereupon Blackmore : “ Oh, you always
want to cuddle.” The house roared.
* * * *
“ B ”: There will be a falling-off in the atten-
dances if the tactics of the first N.S.W. match are
persisted in. The batsmen are not alone to blame for the
tedium. M‘Beth, for instance, got Foster’s wicket on the
Friday by the long amateur hitting at an off ball and
getting caught in the slips. So he got it into his head
that all the side could be disposed of similarly. I took
the trouble to count, in eight successive overs, the num-
ber of balls he sent down that Braund, Hirst, Arnold and
others just looked at without attempting to touch. They
ran, in successive overs, 5,4, 4,5, 3,4, 4, 5. No wonder
he sent down 14 maidens. Noble was a like offender, and
the crowd yelled at both to keep on the wicket. Now,
Arnold, Hirst and Rhodes seem to bowl at the wicket
continuously. I don’t count Bosanquet ; he’s a freak.
* * * *
