“Marat”: Wonderful the number of persona
one meets who have drawn prizes and horses in iatt. s
sweep. Canvassing door-fasteners in Woolloomooloo, a
few days before Melbourne Cup, I struck a man in
dirty flannel and patched trousers, who was being
admired by eighteen men and women. His feet were
bare, and his appearance created the impression that ne
had just recovered from a mighty lapping bout. Askea
a bj'stander if he was an escaped lunatic, and he informed
me, in an awestruck whisper, that “ the bloke had drawn
an ’orse, and had sold the ticket for £5OO. Down the
next street I struck the man who had drawn Abundance,
and three doors further I sold a door-fastener to a woman
whose husband had drawn Lord Cardigan. round
seven or eight more during the day who had drawn cash
prizes. When I got home, the wife said Mrs. ormth, in
the hack street, had struck Abundance; and Kelly, at
the brewery, had Hauturier ; and she bemoaned our bad
luck in never being able to strike a lost threepenny bit.
So I got annoyed and said things, and there was trouble.
* * * *
A sad tale of the misinterpretation of a. dream
comes from the back country. A bushman, lodging at a
wayside pub, dreamt that the winning ticket in a bup
sweep was held by the barmaid. The dream got on his
mind, and he pestered the beer-maiden so much that at
last she sold him her ticket at double the cost. Then she
sent the money away for two more tickets, and one of
these scored second prize, while the bushman s ticket
drew a blank. And now a sad and dazed bushman
wanders along the track trying to puzzle out the
whyness of the thing, and how much it would have cost
in persuasiveness and cash to have kept on buying up
the barmaid’s tickets until it was too late for her to
send for more, and whether it wouldn’t have been cheaper
to marry her.
* * * *
“ Eph Gee Ell ” (Kalgoorlie): Triple dead-beat
between Highflyer, Barindi and Loch Lochie in Moore-
field Handicap on Caulfield Cup day prompts following
reminiscences which hunters after instances of triple
dead-heats should find in Turf Registers. In June,
1897, at Bendigo (Vic.), Ironsides, 9.0., Revenge, 8.5,
and Eva, 8.3, dead-heated in the five and a half fur-
longs Diamond Jubilee Purse in 1.12}. In the run-off
Ironsides was favorite at 6 to 4 against, and he won by a
neck from Revenge, Eva a bad third. At Brighton,
Tasmania, on May 24, 1899, Lena, 11.3, Fidelis, 9.7, and
Melody, 7.12, dead-heated in the Lady’s Bracelet Welter
Handicap. The tote paid out on the three horses, and
then Fidelis was scratched from the run-off, his owner
being one of the donors of the bracelet trophy. Lena
went out a 2 to 1 favorite in run-off and won by a length.
At Mansfield, Victoria, in March 1894, Pride, Barmaid and
Shamrock ran a dead-heat in the Hurdle Race. Run-off
Pride won. In Kalgoorlie triple dead-heat in 1899, run-off
was won by Iscariot from Yale Lock and Onslow, the last
named being dead lame when saddled up the se oo ™*
time. Also the comparatively recent quadruple dead-
heat on the Barrier might be cited.
* * * *
The proprietors of Wolfe’s Schnapps are offering
some monetary encouragement to Australian cricketers
in the first test match against England. Each Australian
batsman to score 50 runs will have the privilege of
schnapping a sovereign, and 6d. for every run over 50,
with an additional five sovereigns on attaining the
coveted centur3 r . They also encourage fieldsmen to make
a little extra effort by offering a sovereign for every catch
dismissing an Englishman. Thus every body is urged on
by patriotism and schnapps except the bowler, who is
only urged by patriotism.*
* • * *
“Yarrum”: Short time ago I entered a little
moke at a country meeting for what was called a
scrubbers’ race. The prize was £2, and many trained
horses were let in. I never bet, and was surprised to
find my humble, struggling neddy made favorite. He
battled, with about 29 others, over three furlongs, and
got home in front. I gave the jockey—disqualified boy
from Sydney— £l for his services. “ Wot’s this?” he
asked. Told him half the prize. “ Why, strike me
pink,” said he—“ I could have got three quid from the
books to pull the cow ! ” Whereat I laughed a fiendish
laugh. If my horse had been stiffened I believe they
would have all been stiff, and the spectacle of 30 horses
all trying to lose a £2 race would have been something
worth seeing.
A certain Captain Pearce has been writing
to the Times in scurrilous terms of Australasia —
of its awful labor conditions, the curse of demo-
cracy, and the rest of it; and he endeavors to
show that the people are falling over one another
in their hurry to get out of the blasted country.
Says this person :
Happening to be on board of a ship bound from Maori-
land to England, via Argentina, and finding that among
the passengers a large number were emigrating from
Maoriland and Australia to Argentina, I took the trouble
to question them to find out why they were leaving the
so-called working men’s “Paradise,” and the reply was
the same right through. They were leaving because
there was no chance of their rising out of the ruck of
labor. . . . The condition of these colonies is pitiable,
and the vast emigration which is taking place to Canada,
South Africa, and Argentina is a proof that our energetic
mechanic and artisan recognises that democracy means
ruin to him, and he must try some other country.
Just here W. P. Reeves, Agent-General for
Maoriland, dropped heavily on the bombastic
little Captain and wiped him out in one act. He
enlightened the Times thus :
Let us see how this impressive and melancholy picture
agrees with fact. I fancy that lam not astray in identify-
ing your correspondent with A. W. Pearce, a saloon
passenger in the steamer Rimutaka, which left Wellington,
Maoriland, in August last, and touched at Monte Video
on its way to London. The Rimutaka is a roomy steamer,
and might easily have held the large number of ruined
hut energetic mechanics and artisans referred to by Mr.
Pearce. Unfortunately for him, however, the ship
appears to have done nothing of the kind. Indeed, as
for Maoriland emigrants, a careful scrutiny of the list of
her third-class passengers reveals amongst them only one
Maoriland resident booked for South America. This
solitary person, moreover, was a woman —an Irish
woman.

“ T.H.” from Melbourne :
The yellow and green squabble has been fairly launched
at, the Victorian Federal elections. A week ago Dill
Manky had his health proposed at a breakfast, luncheon,
sandwich, bun or some other such feast, and in response
talked yellow politics until the whole country seemed
turned into a great lemon. A few days later Archbishop
Carr was given a breakfast by the Catholic young men of
the community and talked green politics till everything
looked like an unripe apple or a mouldy umbrella. And
thus a miserable sectarian squabble is projected into the
national politics of the Commonwealth.
Thank heaven it is only the churches which have
this moral disease. If stamp-collectors, foot-
ballers, cricketers, chessists, weather prophets,
dog-fanciers, pugilists and so on got down to the
clerical level, and insisted on subordinating the
interests of Australia to the advertisement of
their pet crazes, this would be a dreadful place.

Moses M'Tavish, ye ken ! At the St.

Gilded candidates who think that they can
easily dodge the Federal electoral law which
limits their expenses will probably fall in with a
splash. The idea seems to prevail in some places
that they can permit some dear, devoted friend
(to whom they have lent a trifle) to pay for them.
But section 171 covers even the dear friend’s pay-
ments :
Electoral expenses include all expenses incurred by or
on behalf of or in the interest of any candidate.
The maximum amount allowed a candidate for
the Reps, is .£100 ; for a would-be Senator, £250.

The average appeal to the woman voter
runs something like this (from the Launceston
Examinee) :
May not the wife of the unprotected worker justly
complain at having to pay 10s. for a pair of boots which,
possibly, if there were no duty, she could get for 7s. ?
Which can be answered with another :
Is it better to be able to pay 10s. for a pair of boots or
to go barefoot even if boots are marked at 7s. ?
It isn’t much satisfaction to see boots (made in
Germany) marked at 7s. if, the wages being spent
in Germany, the woman voter hasn’t the 7s. to
spend—if, in short, the German has got the 7s.
instead of her own husband. As for the “unpro-
tected ” worker, there isn’t any such person. The
miner isn’t directly protected, but his wages de-
pend upon the demand for the coal or the metal,
and upon the competition for his work ; and Pro-
tection protects him by increasing the demand for
the products, and decreases the competition for
his job by providing other jobs.
“ Marque Reel ” from Wesfcralia :
Kalgoorlie Miner has dug up a new grief, and is now
twirling it wildly above its head and “ hollering ” to the
crowd. According to its way of reasoning Westralia is
only to be represented in the next Commonwealth Parlia-
ment by four Senators. This calculation is based on the
probable return of one Protectionist Senator, whose vote,
the Miner argues, will counteract that of one Foreign
Shoddyist, thus leaving an effective vote of only four
Shoddies. The fact that other States are in the same
situation, so that things are about even, is evidently
unknown at the Miner office. Anj-how, the difficulty
could be surmounted by electing six Protectionists. The
same journal unearthed a contributor in Perth who sup-
plied one column of loose notes, and then it is surmised
was drowned in his own tears shed for the sorrows of the
bootmakers under Protection. The burden of his mourn-
ful notes was that ship-loads of foreign boots meant
plentiful work and high wages, while, if Australia made
its own boots, that meant little work and low wages.
Such is the drivel the readers of the great goldfield daily
have to put up with.
Australian Treasurer Turner made one
statement at St. Kilda that was utterly unworthy
of him, and which is a blot on his honest repu-
tation :
They realised that to develop the vast territories of the
States there must be borrowing in the future. A reason-
able amounf of loan money, if well spent, brought them
back—not directly, but indirectly—all the interest they
had to pay for it.
The Bulletin says “No” to both these super-
stitions. If, after spending over £200,000,000 on
“developing the magnificent natural resources of
the country,” these resources are not paying for
their own development and yielding something
over to pay for the development of the rest of the
resources, then the rottenness of the whole business
is apparent. There’are any number of resources
in Australia which justify development out of our
own revenues, but there isn’t one that justifies
borrowing—not one, from the great Australian
Bight to Carpentaria, or from Matthew' Davies'
defunct bank in the south-east up to the murdered
nigger and De Rougernont’s flying wombat in the
farthest north-west.

Premier Irvine, of Victoria, talks of hiSi
approaching resignation in the tones of one who
announces a great public calamity. Irvine’s head
has grown so large now that he probably fancies
the sea will dry up round Australia and leave a
lot of dead whale on the coast when he is gone.
Sydney Telegraph, lying wildly about
Queensland political feeling:
While Mr. Edmund Barton was, from the External
Affairs Department, stirring up dissension and creating
irritation at the prodding of the rabid anti-Philp mem-
bers of both the Federal Houses, Mr. Kingston was pin-
pricking the mercantile classes with a series of petty
interferences in the interests of his extravagantly self-
extolled political rectitude. . . . All these difficulties and
disturbances, coming one after another, and sometimes
altogether, disgusted the vast majority of the people
with Federation. So intense did the bitterness become
that at one time a serious secession movement was
threatened.
Disgusted tHb vast majority 1 And yet Kingston’s
tour of Queensland, from North to South, was a
triumphal progress. Queensland couldn’t be
fooled on the question of Kingston’s Customs
administration ; the Senator Reid case was tried
there. And the sore-headed State Premier who
babbled about secession hasn’t been able even to
keep his State job.
Sweet are the uses of euphemism ! A
cablegram (9/11/’O3) announces that a German
bounder, in command of a fort in S’Africa, lost
his temper and executed (not murdered) a Hotten-
tot chief. The tribe, in retaliation, massacred the
garrison—didn’t wipe it out in battle.
“ T.D.” : Retiring Vic. Premier Irvine’s
friends, who pretend that he isn’t set upon getting
a judgeship, allude to the great sacrifices he has
made in letting his bar practice slide and taking
the Premiership for 18 months. But Irvine was
well aware that he would drop about half his
income, and no doubt calculated that he was
getting a pretty rich advertisement which would
help him later. At any rate, his retirement is a
nasty slap in the face for the Kyabram push,
which asserted that all public servants, from the
Premier downward (or upward), were being paid
too much. Irvine, after cutting down other
people’s salaries, says he is going to retire because
his own salary, which hasn’t been cut down, is
insufficient.

A Maoriland story by “N.J.K.” :
In a town in the Maoriland South Island a well-known
citizen died, and his better half buried him with great
pomp in the local cemetery, so to all appearances he was
laid in his last resting place. But the widow tired of the
fn r n,f n v n fv,’ a r n i d j ft th , e t 0 ta - e u ?, her abod f
m the -North Island, and had the remains taken up at
great expense and transferred along with her. After 18
months, not being in good health, she decided to go
south to a new town, and the poor old departed went
with her and was buried again. Then the widow tired of
this new place, so she had the coffin raised once more,
the nTd n m,n?n & • - o T nal r f esiden “; and re-buried
ine old man in hrs original cemetery. The only person
who cordially approved of these proceedings was the
original sexton. “Yes sur,” he said, “the best job I
ever lad. First we buried him ’ere, and I was paid for
*hat. T , hen we dn £ him up. I was paid for that. Then
ad the vacant lot to sell. I got a bit out of that,
And novv they have brought him back again and buried
him, and I got paid for that. But I’m afraid he’s going
to stay this time." Other people mostly held that the
constant rushing of the old man up and down the
country didn’t give his bones a chance to settle down.
Nowadays if you happen to
have some slight facility in the art of using a
brush, or a lump of charcoal, or spinning a story,
it i® the correot thing to have a studio. At first I
rather scorned the idea, but eventually decided to
geo what it was like> and now -well, there are
p o j n^s a b ou t having a studio. I had a iook round
P d j d ir fg t . Plug ’ s Buildings, and an
artist friend came round and designed the fixings,
c h mostly consisted of some spangled stuff
made in j ap / n . Then I settled down to write,
By-and-bye it chimed ten, and almost immedi-
ately after there was the faint sound of “rustle”
ascending the stairs. A whiff of perfume floated
through the half-open door and a key was softly
fitted into a lock. For an hour it went
o n, and then odors of freshly-made coffee
came stealing through the atmosphere with
„ , • T ° i , A i «
a spgffestion of beer between. A soft laugh
eddied up out of the rooms below. Ihe
building was full of women. They interested me.
Next day I investigated. There was a whole
community of them, living in a sort of modified
d restraiued Bohemianism. They had part of
~ • jrt-j.iT

and their walls made gay with art muslins and
tiny pictures, and some of them ran to wall
vases and plants. They were nearly all young,
reasonably pretty, and neatly, almost fashionably
j j „ fl : d o„ nl . n„ „ wppk f nr t u; r

of co-operation among themselves, lived and
dressed for another 11s. or 12s. per week. The
advantages were perfect freedom, no petty re-
straints as to visitors, or the possession of latch-
keys—there are still in Sydney many boarding-
houses where unmarried girls and “ married ladies
whose husbands are away ” are not allowed latch-
keys. In a boarding-house they have to take the
food that is set before them ; in chambers they
obtain what they fancy, if they have money
enough. They are a gentle, well-mannered, in-
offensive crowd of nice, lonely little women, and
while my studio is in St. Plug’s Buildings I
intend to be kind to as many of them as I can.
An ex-Johorer writes :
During several visits to Johore I met the old “Sultan,"
and got to know him rather well. He was a sturdy old
Malay with a hot eye. Save in so far as the Straits
Government clipped his claws, he never permitted his
principles to stand in the way of his pleasures. His prin-
ciples would not have made much of an obstacle, anyhow.
He once imported certain white girls—French and Ger-
man—for the alleviation of his leisure. He did not call
them typewriters, because 'he was not sufficiently
civilised ; but when the Straits Government booked them
back to Europe he was an aggrieved man. He had
selected them with care, and he just hated to be baulked
in his definite intentions. The oid “Sultan” was par-
tially modernised—had Scotch officials of State, swore in
English, tippled in defiance of his gods, and played bil-
liards. For any white woman life in Johore’s Istana would
be permanent burial in a place of evil odor. When I
was first in Johore, the “ Sultan ” was playing the Roman
father with some enthusiasm. The Crown Prince was in
gaol, doing six mo dhs’ hard for the abduction of a
Chinese citizen’s wife. He started too young, and the
“Sultan” wanted no trespassers on his monopoly.
Johore is a beautiful State, largely virgin and undeve-
loped—a land of wasted plenty. To pet to the capital,
Johor Bahru, you run across the island of Singapore
(12 miles in a ’rikshaw, and across the shallow strait (a
mile or less) in a sampan. (The lithe Malayan tigers
swim across this strait, and make things lively on Singa-
pore plantations. Oats sometimes take to water.) At
Johor Bahru are many modern improvements : an English
club, a post and telegraph office, the world, the flesh, and
the devil. Johore, like Tasmania, farms a gambling
monopoly, and at the big casino the fat Chinese crowd
about the tables, cheek by jowl with ladies of question-
able character, kept for convenience on the premises.
Picnicking missionaries from Singapore find Jolior
Bahru singularly picturesque. So did I.
“Polstan ” : Re risky boilers (“ Yarram”,
8.24/9/’03): lonce went with anengineerto inspect
a second-hand boiler that was under offer to a
flour-milling company. He asked permission to
try one of the plates with a small punch. The
owners wouldn’t allow it, so he passed that kettle
out. A few weeks later it was purchased for
a factory, and the first time it was put under
pressure burst and killed a man. Asked the
engineer why he had suspected the thing. “It
was freshly painted inside,” he said, “and paint
hides many flaws. Never buy a boiler that is
painted inside.”
Unconscious slab of humor from Austra-
lasian Catholic Record in an article on Thomas
More:
One blow of the headsman’s axe, as our own Cardinal
so beautifully put it, gave to the brightest genius and
noblest heart in England, the unfading crown of a
glorious martyr.

AND DON’T FORUET
THE FIERY DRaGON.
A little private Bill, struggling along with
Government help through the Westralian Parliament,
throws an interesting side-light on pearl-shelling. The
old trick of the Asiatic diver or lugger-hand was to
swallow the pearl when he found it and the boss hadn’t
his eyes that way. The boss hired more eyes to watch,
and then the mild heathen thought of something else.
He works in collusion with a “dummying” boat, and
when he strikes a patch, leaves it and leads the boss and
the boat away, but gives the dummy the signal to get
there. The Bill asks power to wipe the dummy out.
Euchre isn’t the only game that Ah Sin can play.
* » * *
The Friday lode of the North Boulder (W.A.)
is a continuation of the Oroya-Brown Hill lode. The
lode on the outcrop forms a semi-circle, and has a very
flat but irregular underlay to the west. The rich chute
outcropped in the Brown Hill, and runs diagonally
through the lode with a dip of 18deg. to the south, but
gradually getting steeper. It was expected in Oroya’s
Pomeroy shaft at 700 ft., judging from the bore, but was
found to be 100 ft. east. Pomeroy shaft itself is 250 ft.
east of the Kalgurli boundary. The westerly underlay,
as stated in Bulletin of 17th Sept., carries the rich chute
to about the middle of the Oroya’s southern boundary-
line before reaching the Associated. Unless the chute
dies out suddenly, it must be found in the Associated, as
the Oroya has followed it almost to the boundary. All
the ore in the Friday lode was worked out to the Asso-
ciated boundary four years ago, since when the North
Boulder has been a dead bird. The non-locating of the
lode by the Associated proves nothing, except that boring
on these fields is often a waste of money. On the Rand the
boring is through stratified rocks, or sandstone, and the
bores are not so liable to deflection.
* • * *
Queen Margaret, Bulong (W.A.), tributors con-
tinue to do well, and have already treated 92 tons for
6900 z. As the co. gets paid for crushing and collars a
large royalty, it comes out better than if working the
ground itself. The Vosperton property looks well, but
the reef is too small for a highly capitalised co. A signifi
cant fact is that manager Henderson, who has been con-
nected with the co. for years, has resigned, to take the
Princess Royal Central, Norseman. This latter concern
looks like a very good gamble, judging from its position,
as a deep-level proposition.
* * • *
Up to now, the Westralian State batteries and
cyanide works have treated 143,000 tons of ore for
£705,000 worth of gold, most of which would never have
been mined but for the Government battery. These bat-
teries are now practically self-supporting, except that
nothing is allowed for depreciation, and, sooner or later,
the amount expended will have to be written-off as lost.
But, on the credit side, is the vast amount of good done
to the State by opening up new goldfields.
• * * *
A new find of telluride has been made at Mul-
gabbie (W.A.), about 80 miles north-east of Kanowna.
The place has long been famous for very rich alluvial—
now worked out—and for being intersected by very rich
quartz leaders, yielding up to hundreds of ounces per ton
in patches. On one of the deserted dumps a fossicker
recently picked up some stone, or said he did, which he
thought looked like telluride, and sent it to Kalgoorlie
for analysis ; and the analyst certified that it was telluride
ore worth 4550 z. per ton. Needless to state that the
country for a mile or two round has been pegged, but
this will -lead to nothing unless capital can be wooed.
The quartz-leaders have been followed to water-level
(90ft.), the limit of a fossicker’s tether.
* * * *
Ivanhoe South Extended (W.A. ), a block of 22
acres west of the Horseshoe, has cut a lode by diamond
drilling at a depth of 1920 ft. The core is said to assay
loz. 15dwt. per ton. The lode is alleged to be 14ft. from
the boundary, but, as nearly all deep bore holes on the
fields have been deflected 300 to 400 ft., this lode may not
be in the Extended’s area at all. The directors have,
however, decided to sink the main shaft to 2000 ft. to
thoroughly test the ground. The shaft is down 821 ft.,
and has been at a standstill for over a year, while
directors have been nursing the £50,000 in hand. The
concern is managed by J. W. Sutherland, of the Horse-
shoe, who'should know the character of the ground well.
The co. is in 150,000 shares and the block is in a fairly
good position, but, until something is really found, the
scrip is a pure gamble. Of course, there is room for a
very big rise if a really payable lode is located.
*'* * *
Paddington Consols (W.A.) has, apparently,
again come to the end of its tether, and all wages hands
have been fired out. The main shaft is still being sunk
to 500 ft., that work being on contract. The mine has
proved so poor at 400 ft. that it may be taken for granted
that it has seen its best days, and it is questionable if
work will be resumed. The wild-cats of W.A. have been
numerous and lusty, but 1903 has rung the death knell of
most of the litter. Another of them, the Lady Loch, is
nearing its end.
* * * *
North Kalgurli (W.A.) has come out with its
initial crushing of 1000 tons for 7720 z., but the bullion is
worth only about 675. an oz. The slimes plant, however,
was not operating, so a better average may be looked for
in future. No costs are given, and, as the mill could easily
have done another 200 tons of the soft, oxidised ore, and
not have burst itself, sceptics have doubts about the
tonnage. A parcel of 110 tons of telluride ore was also
treated at Cockle Creek for 5420 z. of fine gold and 17040 z.
of fine silver. That is the trouble with the sulphide ore
that, though the average runs fairly high, the bulk of
the bullion is silver. Speculators who take a hand in
the deal must bear in mind that no development is
being done, and that the mine has no established per-
manency.
* * * *
Paringa, Kalgoorlie(W. A.), has had its diamond
drill bore tested by Bawden’s plumb bob, which makes
out that the bore is 360 ft. askew. As the bore is right in
the corner of the lease, it may have gone anywhere, but,
as it found nothing of value, it matters little where it
went. The co. is still boring, but what it expects to find
nobody but the board has the slightest idea.
* * * *
Donelly River, in the south-west of Westralia,
is the place where the alleged find of platinum, tin, and
gold, recently mysteriously talked of by the Minister for
Mines, was made. Some sort of a rush has taken place,
though old prospectors have sniffed and reflected that
the high assay-figures quoted represent the contents of
concentrates, and don’t give the smallest idea of the
value of the crudes. The Minister would have been wiser
had he held his tongue till more inquiries were made.
* * * *
S. Australia’s one coal-mining co.—the Leigh’s
Creek Co.—has had a life of trouble. The deposit,
which is in the Far North, was discovered 12 years ago,
and an Adelaide co. was formed to work it. The stuff,
something between lignite and ordinary bituminous coal,
burns well enough, but has not been able to Compete
with the Newcastle product in the Adelaide market, and
the railway people insist that it is out of the question for
loco, steaming. Practically no work has been done on
the mine for two or three years; the co. has spent its
time and a few calls frying to induce the Railway
Department to alter its mind. Incidentally it has tried
to sell the mine, but can’t get an offer; and as the
calling power is now about exhausted, it looks as if
S.A.’s one coal co. will soon throw up the sponge.
• * * •
Bendigo’s October output, 24,8860 z., was the
biggest month’s record for 28 years.
Madame Berry West (Creswick, Vie.) had, to
the beginning of November, won £458,052 worth of gold,
of which £17,598 was credited to the past six months.
The half-year’s profit was £4499, or ss. Bd. in the £ of the
gross receipts. On a paid-up capital of £11,700 the divi-
dends have so far amounted to £70,337 ; and the outlook
is still good. t . *
Croydon (N.Q.) has been disappointing this
vear, during the last quarter especially. For the first
nine months 41,363 tons gave 46,6180 z. ; this is 39950 z.
better than last year’s, but the average per ton is poorer.
The October yield was 62090 z. from 3625 tons stone ;
average 34dwt., but then the bullion was worth only
£2 3s. an ounce. For the last four months £7BOO has
been paid in divs. ; calls have reached £2680. Golden
Gate Consols is doing best ; Roger’s, though a fair pro-
ducer, is spending what it earns in exploration. The
outside mines are not up to expectations.

• • * •
Maoriland’s coal output is growing to something
sizeable. Last year it was 1,366,000 tons, or 126,364 tons
more than in 1901. The Westport Co. was responsible
for over 600,000 tons of this. The old Brunner mine,
also on the West Coast of the South Island, gave 116,714
tons. But the Brunner is nearing its end ;it is living
now simply by taking out its pillars. The Blackball
ranks third among West Coast mines outside the West-
port Co.’s. On the East Coast, only the Kaitangata
(118,050 tons) can compare with these. In the North
Island the Taupiri (89,263 tons) heads the list, and few
others approach it. The Westport Co.’s most productive
mine now is, and for some time past has been, the
Denniston ; there about 200,000 tons a year are hewn with
the percussive pick machine, worked by compressed air.
Only about a quarter of the output is now got out in the
old laborious way. M.L. is ahead of N.S.W. in that
matter.
* * * *
Adelaide Sewage Farm announces a profit of
£660 on its last year’s working, and that after paying to
the Government not only rent (12s. an acre), but 5 per
cent, on capital invested. Bedford Hack, the resident
manager, told an Advertiser, interviewer that he had not
had a day’s illness during his 14 years there, while his
daughter, who caught typhoid in Melbourne, had to
return home to get well 1 “ The sewage farm is a sana-
torium.”
* * * *
Queensland’s new Minister for Mines recently
directed wardens that only on exceptional occasions were
mining exemptions to be granted in future. This action
was in consequence of the large number of applications
for exemptions coming before him ; present game being,
he said, for persons to apply for land all round a pro-
mising new show', make application for exemption even
before the land had been allotted them, then sit back
and wait for cash offers.
Since the advertisement on another page
was printed, the art proofs of Phil May’s “Curio-
sity in Her Own Country” and “Majesty of the
Law ” have been exhausted. There remain only
a few copies of the other subjects, for which early
application is recommended.
Coincident with the departure of two
visitors from his abode, t’other day, a North Shore
(Sydney) resident found that a considerable
portion of his worldly wealth had departed also.
Police investigation showed that the victim had
contracted the foolish habit of putting his hoard
(400 sovereigns) in a tea-pot, which was, in turn,
placed in a kerosene tin, and the tin hidden under
some rubbish in a lumber room. The intense care
displayed in hiding a worthless kerosene tin
probably attracted attention and then trouble
followed quite naturally.
The English Cricketers seem a persevering lot,
likely to make a big bid for the Test rubber.
Whether they succeed or not in their long-winded
enterprise, the fact remains that D.C.L. Whisky
wins every match against brain fag and “that
tired feeling.” The vim of those business men
who take D.C.L. for their tonic makes them feel
that they are on a good wicket all the time.
They play the game with confidence. D.C.L.
gives that mental alertness and endurance so
necessary to one who means to keep his wicket up
and run his fortune into five figures.*
This, from Sydney Telegraph of last
Tuesday, is a pathetic instance of the yelp of
discomfiture trying to pass itself off as the shout
of triumph :
Protectionists in Australia habitually deny that a
country which has a revenue tariff has*a right to claim
that it enjoys Freetrade. But let them listen to this
“ I see that your revenue must always, probably, and
certainly for a long while to come, depend chiefly upon
indirect taxation. But in my mind, 'whenever customs
duties are balanced by excise duties, or u'henever they are
levied on articles which are not produced at home, the
enforcement of such duties is no derogation from the
principles of Freetr tde, as I understand it.”— Chamberlain
in the Imperial Conference, 1902.
Therefore, by the Telegraph’s own showing,
N.S.W. never had anything dimly resembling
Freetrade, and there isn’t one Freetrade paper
or politician in N.S.W. who seems to want
Freetrade. The N.S.W. Freetrade policy has
always been to levy large customs duties on all
manner of things that can be produced locally,
and not to balance them by excise duties, and
then to lie about the business. On the definition
of Freetrade which the Telegraph advances, the
whole Parkes-Reid-Brucepunipkin- Smith-Joe-
Cook-Pulsford crowd is about the cheekiest traitor
and backslider going.
At Poverty Point.
Miss Francis Adler—grand-daughter of Madame
Simonsen—has now won a measure of good fortune by
her grit. A chorus-girl with a promising voice in
Australia, she went to England, and, by singing at “the
halls,’’ accumulated enough cash to pay for a course
under Marchesi. Now Marchesi cables that Miss Adler
has a three years engagement at the Paris Opera
Comique—one of the four State theatres of Paris. This
puts a mint-mark of approval on the Melbourne singer
which will vouch her as good currency in almost any
part of the world.
Looks as though a certain theatrical manager and
his leading lady have arrived at a final and complete
misunderstanding this time.
Seems that John Billington was 62 when he came to
Australia as principal supporter of that aged and old-
fashioned comedian, J. L. Toole. He was 76 the other
day when he received £BOO from a benefit performance at
the Haymarket Theatre, London,
It would be interesting to hear what Madame Marzella
has to say about parrot language. The French traveller,
Du Ohaillu, states from his own experience that parrots
brought together from different parts of the world are
foreigners in language, but after six months of teaching
one another they can converse fluently.
Salvarmy “ Austral Guards ” band, at present touring
N.S. Wales, struck bad luck on its north-western trip.
On November 6th one of the baritones was drowned
while bathing in the Namoi at Gunnedah, and several
days later the wife of the major in charge suicided by
drowning at Moree.
Mel. B. Spurr, the entertainer, confesses to the front
names of Melancthon Burton, the latter presumably after
the anatomist of melancholy.
In Tree’s revival of “Richard II.,” at Her Majesty’s,
London, throughout the earlier portions of the play the
hapless king is accompanied by a greyhound, which, in
the well-known scene in Flint Oastle, deserts its master
for the usurper, Bolingbroke. This bit of doggy faith-
lessness (founded on a historical fact, vouched for by
Froissart) fetches the house.
“0.K.”: Somebody wants to know what I meant by
saying “ One gets so tired of hearing that Ada Orossley
is a good woman.” My words are not exactly quoted,
I fancy; nevertheless, I accept the quotation as correct,
and rise to explain that from my point of view the
excellence or depravity of an artist’s moral character—
the virtue or viciousness of her life—matters not a
tinker’s anathema to any intelligent person who is
considering the artist as an artist. The same inquirer
wishes to know why I dragged Madame Sterling into my
remarks. I didn’t drag her in. She got there in the
natural course of comparison. Madame Sterling, like
Ada Crossley, was praised as much for her private
virtues as for her public vocalism. And The Bulletin at
|he time professed great tiredness of Antoinette.
“Marat”: Once in the long ago I knew an artist who
hired himself for poster work to a travelling co. He was
engaged at a salary of £4 a week, and was told to pur-
chase all necessary materials out of his own pocket, and
charge to the manager. He did so, and made the hoard-
ings at Granville picturesque with flaring posters of
“ East Lynne." He got 2s. after the Granville season of
two nights, with which he bought more ink to elec-
trify Campbelltown. Here he received Is. 6d., which went
in ink for Picton. Picton brought him Is., and the
supply of posters for Moss Vale was small in consequence.
His “draw” at Moss Vale was 6d., which he spent in
beer, so Goulburn did not receive a single specimen of
his handiwork. The boss of the co. was enraged, and
said things, so the poster-artist went out, traded off a
black and white sketch for 65., and took out a summons
against the manager for £l2, wages due, and 18s. 6d.
spent for paper, inks, &c. He settled outside the court
for 7s. 6d. and his fare back to Sydney.
Ada Crossley struck an old friend in Adelaide the
other day in. the Rev. Henry Howard, who has lately
taken to Bible-thumping at Adelaide Tivoli. She con-
fessed to an interviewer that she used to play the organ
for him at his church in Gippsland—or, rather, she played
at the Anglican church in the morning, the Presby-
terian in the afternoon, and at the Methodise in the
evening. Ada was a persistent church-goer in her bush
days when her youthful hair was still hanging down her
back.
“ H.”: Maoriland has been a great show-field for the past
three or four years, and hardly a case can be quoted
where a reputable company has not done well. At
present there are evidences of a slump in this direction,
and with plenty of cause. There are only four places
that can be called cities—Auckland, Wellington, Christ-
church and Dunedin ; other towns worth visiting are
under the five-figure limit. Yet these places have been
marvellously catered for. Tiny townships have been
invaded by big J.C.W. combinations, with the result that
the standard of production is quite abnormal, and the
ordinary good show is finding itself “ up against it.”
It sounds ridiculous, but the Cuyler Hastings production
of “If I Were King” was seen at Waipawa (population
between 2000 and 3000), before it was in Melbourne ; and
Williamson’s Musical Comedy Cos., out for an airing in
Blenheim and Nelson, gave the streets an uncomfortably
crowded appearance. Then the bottom fell out, as if the
thing had been done with a can-opener. The “ Fortune-
Teller ’’ crowd has done very poor business. Frawley’s
season started at the thick end of the wedge and rapidly
got to the thin end, and stayed there—at Auckland.
Business in Wellington started thin and remained so,
though the show was one of very high merit. The
“Dream” production, at present at Auckland, doesn’t
look like paying expenses. The slump has surely come,
and managers will do well to stand off for 12 months, and
let the little Maoriland towns do with the small, wander-
ing shriekodramady company, which is really the thing
their case calls for.
Lands Minister Crick (N.S. W.) is a good
deal of a free-lance. When, the other night,
Works Minister O’Sullivan proposed that the
recommendation of the Public Works Committee
with regard to the water-supply of Broken Hill
should be carried into effect, Crick tabled an
amendment, apparently in favor of the Yanco
Glen syndicate’s scheme. Presumably what the
Owe’-Sullivan proposed is the Government policy
in the matter; but Crick went “on his own,” and
won. The whole question, consequently, goes
back to the Public Works Committee —it has been
doing that, or thereabouts, ever since there was a
Works Committee.
This is how Senator Stewart—who is going
to top the Queensland poll for the Senate, if the
electors know a straight and able man when they
see him —answers questions on the hustings :
Q. : Are you a Republican ?
A. : Yes ; I do not- believe in hereditary monarchs any
more than hereditary mayors or hereditary members of
Parliament.
Q. : Did you sit down when “ God save the Queen” was
being sung?
A. : Yes; when I was making an anti-war speech in
Rockhampton in the Jingo days. The audience broke
out with the National Anthem. I seized the opportunity
to take a rest and sat down.
Q. : Did you attack Mr. Samuel Griffith on his elevation
to the distinguished position of Chief Justice of the
Commonwealth ?
A. : Yes; Mr. Griffith, as a politician, betrayed the
trust of the people. I only hope that he will leave a
cleaner record behind, when he retires from the bench,
than he left when he retired from politics.
Q. : Are you a Protectionist ?
A. : Yes ; lam an out-and-out Protectionist. I believe
in protecting our manufactures from unfair foreign com-
petition ; our workmen from alien immigration ; our
wage slaves from the extortions of monopolists and
usurers and sweaters. I believe in protecting everything
that is in the interest of Australian manhood and an Aus-
tralian nation.
No Yes-No quibbling about these answers


The Bulletin’s Christmas number will
be issued on 10th December. The Bulletin’s

The N.S.W. Government, having had its
credit stopped, now makes a cheerful pretence
that it left off its large borrowing policy volun-
tarily, because a good season had arrived ; that it
always intended to leave off; and that it always
regarded its big-loan devices as a mere temporary
scheme to alleviate the drought trouble. ‘ ‘ When
men were shipwrecked,” said Premier See, the
other day, “ I sent out the lifeboat.” But surely
it is customary to send out our own lifeboat—not
a borrowed one. And it isn’t usual to expect the
rescued men and their descendants to pay 3| per
cent, interest on the lifeboat for ever afterwards.

Political Points for the People.
Tub George Reid tariff policy is revenue with destruction
of native industries--as much destruction as he has
courage to effect. He will smash the small, struggling
industries—which, if helped now, will one day be great
means of employment—but he will leave untouched the
big protected ones, which can “lobby" successfully—as
he left untouched the sugar industry and the daily-paper
industry in N.S.W. The Reid policy combines Gruelty
and destruction with falsehood and hypocrisy. Vote it
down.
The one foe of the White Australia ideal before the
people is George Reid. He would relax the immigration
restriction laws and spend Australian money in increasing
the dividends of shipping cos. who boycott white labor
and employ lascars. Vote him down.
The best candidate to vote for is a Labor-Protectionist;
the next best, a Protectionist who will follow Deakin.
The Deakin programme is not progressive enough, but it
is at least not reactionary. The ideal for Radicals to
aim at is a Kingston administration, with Turner as
Treasurer. Failing that, a Deakin administration with
such a strong Radical element in its following that it
will be forced to progress, and with Turner as Treasurer.
A typical lie from a Freetrade daily :
“ The Prime Minister, who assisted to fix on the people
the present iniquitous tariff just to serve the interests of
Victorian manufacturers.”
The present tariff, as a matter of fact, is a compromise
between the slightly Protectionist one of N.S.W. and the
more decidedly Protectionist one of Victoria before
Federation ; ar.d the compromise leans always to the side
of the N.S.W. ideas. It is now, in spite of its defective
character, doing far more to stimulate industries in
N.S.W. than in any of the other States. Sydney Free-
trade politician M‘Millan’s firm has established “white-
goods ” factories and is advertising cheaper goods as the
result of the tariff. Sydney Freetrade politician Storey is
also building a hat factory in Sydney. Those are typical
results of the “ iniquitous Melbourne tariff.”
The Labor party platform for the coming Australian
Parliament: Maintenance of a White Australia; restriction
of borrowing; a Federal absentee-tax ; fiscal peace ; an
adequate area for the Federal capital site ; industrial
arbitration; Federal old-age pensions; defence on effective
citizen lines, instead of Imperialistic millinery lines;
protection to Australian shipping by navigation laws ;
nationalisation of monopolies. It’s a good programme,
and only lacks “ fiscal progress,” a Federal land tax, and
a sinking fund for the repayment of the public debt to
make it completely satisfactory. The electors cannot go
wrong in voting for J. O. Watson and his followers.
. H J’ou have got Freetrade so badly that you can’t get
rid of it, vote for a Freetrader who promises to go solid
for Freetrade ; not Reid, or Braddon, or Smith (either
Bruce or Pumpkin), or Wilks, who wants to get in on the
Freetrade ticket, and promises to practise a certain
amount of Protection when he is in. Surely there must
be a Freetrade candidate somewhere who believes in his
own belief !
Vote down any candidate who comes with the endorse-
ment of a “ religious ” body—Roman Catholic or Protes-
tant. The man elected with the help of priest influence
or parson influence will be the puppet of a church.
Wherever a church has got control of politics—whether
in Anglican England, Roman Catholic Ireland, or Presby-
terian Scotland, it has piled up a record of black infamy
—of brutal cruelty, gross hypocrisy, and disgusting
tyranny. Vote down the “sectarian" candidate, what-
ever his brand.

The Monkey Council.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The monkeys sat in council,
They were mostly strong and hale,
And there wasn’t one among them
Who was puny-built or pale.
There wasn’t one among them
But with life was overjoyed,
Not a single laughing simian
Who was ever unemployed.
And the President cried “ Order ! ’*
He’d a curious tale to tell
Of the men who grubbed and grafted
Till they made their land a hell ;
How they said they came of monkeys;
But they’d left the ape behind,
And forsaken trick and gambol
To develop soul and mind;
How they proved it by erecting
Dreary house and dreary gaol,
How they bragged they’d clean abolished
Every remnant of a tail ;
How a few of them were wealthy,
And the others short of bread ;
How they lived a life unhealthy
In a land where Hope was dead ;
How they used the steam and lightning
To propel a funny car;
How they boasted they would travel
Till they ran against a star.
And the monkeys heard in silence
All the tale of pride and sin ;
But the story of man’s rising
From the monkey raised a grin—
Raised a grin that rose to laughter,
Till the trees began to sway,
Till the echoes waked the sleepers
In the forest far away.
And they passed a little motion :
“If the men who chatter thus,
While they live a life of madness,
Think they’ve risen far from us—
“ Let us tell them we are happy,
And our only gaol’s a tree,
Where the fruit is full and sappy,
Where the monkey tribe is free.
“ And if Happiness and Freedom
Are the earthly creature’s goal,
Man is wondrous far beneath us—
Though he brags about a soul 1 ”
Q.
L.
A Lesion in Imperial Federation.
Canada’s present attitude towards Britain gives
an eloquent warning as to the danger of a “push-
ing policy ’’ in regard to Imperialism. Just at
the time when various British politicians and
Australian K.C.M.G.’s are prating of the crimson
thread and of the propriety of pulling it tighter,
Canada’s views on the Alaskan question are
crossed by those of Britain, and Canada at once
begins to talk of further separation instead of
closer union. The Alaskan boundary has long
been a subject of controversy between Canada and
the United States. At one time Russia owned
Alaska, but sold it to the United States—
probably with the same object in view that
Napoleon had when he sold Louisiana—that of
embarrassing Britain. The object was achieved.
The Behring Sea seal fisheries have been long
matter for contention between the United States
and Britain.; and later the discovery of great gold
fields at Klondike gave a new value to the
Alaskan littoral and a new cause for disagree-
ment. In 1898, the United States, feeling some-
what friendly to Britain because of the indirect
help given against Spain, an Anglo-American
Commission was appointed to settle amicably all
differences. That Commission, after many delays,
has finally come to a decision on the Alaskan
question, which decision is all in favor of the
United States. Three U.S.A. delegates and a
British delegate voted for the award against two
Canadian delegates. The U.S.A. delegates, it is
said, made little effort from the first to conceal
the fact that they had already decided in their
own favor; either they would win or there would
be a deadlock. And they won, because, the
Canadians maintain, Britain cares more for keep-
ing the uncertain friendship of its greatest
commercial enemy than for securing justice for
Canada, and Britain’s Commissioner accordingly
voted against Canada. As to the merits of the
dispute—which is as to the interpretation of the
words, “a strip of territory 30 miles from the
ocean,” whether they mean 30 miles back from
the main sea line, as the Canadians say, or 30
miles back following the indentations of the
coast to the head of the furthest inlet, as the
Americans say—an honest difference of opinion is
possible ; but there is no doubt that the United
States Government is determined that no differ-
ence of opinion, honest or otherwise, will deprive
it of the claimed territory. Britain, helpless in
the vortex of foreign hostilities, in which Im-
perialism and expansion have involved it, can do
nothing by way of protest. So Canada threatens
to claim the right to settle such matters in future
by itself. According to a speech by the Premier :
So long as Canada remained a dependency of the
Crown, her present powers would not be sufficient, and
she must now ask the Motherland for an extension, in
order to enable her to deal with similar questions in the
future “in our own fashion, and according to the best
light we have.”
That, as the London Times points out, is a plea
for practical separation. W hen Canada has power
to manage its foreign affairs—as it already does its
domestic affairs—its independence will be practi-
cally complete. And, without a doubt, that is the
natural trend of development, and all the mistaken
efforts of Imperial Federationists can but assist,
not retard, it. The Anglo-Saxon in Canada and
Australia will naturally, in time, feel that he is
entitled to as great powers of self-government as
the man of the same breed in Manchester or
Glasgow. Ultimately, they will be conceded in a
friendly way, or taken in an unfriendly way.
Even Sydney Telegraph hints at a coming
change:
It is imaginable that, in the reform of the future, the
Council may vani-sh. Now that we have a national Par-
liament, it is possible that an irresistible demand fora
one-House State Parliament may be formulated.

“ Copra-Beetle ” writes re the loss of the
Ovalau :
About 70 per cent, of copra it pure oil, the balance
highly combustible fibre, &c., and about 5 per cent,
water. When the moisture runs higher than 5 per cent.,
as it often does, a great heat is the result. Speaking
from memory I can recall the following disasters to copra-
carrying vessels and stores (and the trade is quite young):
Ovalau ; Manapouri; East Lothian, put intoS. A.port with
copra on fire ; vessel from Sydney to Valparaiso with
some copra on board, never heard of again ; vessel, name
forgotten, put into Lyttelton (>I.L.) with copra heated;
copra vessel on fire in Liverpool docks some years ago ;
copra stores in Fiji and Tonga destroyed by fire with
contents; hulk burned to the water’s edge at Levukn
with copra cargo, &c., &c.
Captain Todd is reported to have said that the cause of
the fire was probably spontaneous combustion. He, no
doubt, made a shrewd guess. I have travelled much in
copra ships, but always because I had to, and X never hid
from myself the fact that such a vessel has all the mak-
ings of a first-class crematorium. Further, it is quite a
common thing in the islands for a nigger who is stowing
cargo to wear a box of wax vestas stuck through the lobe
of one ear ; sometimes his pipe is in the other ear, or his
'bacca-box, or any little thing like that. Now and then
the matchbox will drop out, and the matches be scat-
tered. Tommy Tanna just picks up all the matches he
can find easily. The rest get mixed up with the copra.
A correspondent from Bloemfontein
(S’Africa): —
This town, like all the rest of S’Africa (Jewberg
included), is in a bad state, living very dear and very
nasty—nearly all cold storage meat and next to no vege-
tables. The place is full of men out of work, a lot of
them Australians. It is no advertisement for a man to
proclaim himself Australian, as they are shunned like the
plague.
The combined wisdom of some dozens of
Victorian municipal delegates convened in Mel-
bourne, during Cup week, to discuss the “ good
roads ” question evolved two momentous deci-
sions : that (1) the Government be asked to
restore the municipal subsidy to its original
dimensions (largely at the cost of the heavily-
rated city worker), and (2) that the Railway
Commissioners be asked to carry road metal at
cut rates (largely at the expense of the heavily-
rated city worker). Nothing about taxing lands
that would be improved by the said good roads,
or making the cheeky landholders pay rent for
closed roads, or putting up country rates and
valuations to the city standard. Yet every one
of these farmer delegates calls himself a Reformer,
and babbles about the cost of Government and the
loss of revenue on the railways.

GORDON & GOTCH
The Bitting of the Sea.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I stand on the shore
With a heart grown sore
For the lovers that never may meet,
And I look to the bay
Where the white colts play
With the blue sea under their feet.
So long unreined
Have her steeds remained
With the sea fire fretting their blood,
That they strangle and drown
When the ships roll down
On the sweep of her tides full-flood.
Long, sunlit and starred,
Have her stallions barred
The road to the South for me,
Till the pride of my race
Groweth hot for the chase
Of. the white Arab steeds of the sea.
With my buckled hate
For a spur to his gait
I would saddle the dark North wind,
And far and near
They should gather in fear
When his shod hoofs clanged behind.
From the fields in-shore
Where the stallions roar
As they ramp in the rock-bound shoals,
And far and away
Where the yearlings play
And the white mares call their foals.
Then, corralled at last,
I would fetter them fast;
I would hold them bitted and bound,
With a double band
For the corpse on the sand
And their debt to the deep-sea drowned !
Over snaffle and chain
They should call to the main
Where their kingdom was free as the air,
When their torn flanks feel
There is more in our steel
1 han the rowels the rough winds wear!
And one of these steeds
To each heart that bleeds
I would give, for a passion to prove
That the bridegroom would ride
Down the world to his bride
And the lover as far to his love.
SCOTLAND.
WILL H. O[?]ILVIE.
Whelan Gets Hitched-Up.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Winter was bitter out Bulloo way, the keen
westerlies biting into the very marrow of
humanity. Old Grimes, crouching over the fire
in his slab house, cursed the cold. The canvas
ceiling, stained by the leakage from the perishing
shingled roof, rose and fell to the howling wind
with irritating monotony. The walls of the house
were lined with cheap calico, through which the
wind found easy entrance, and at intervals in his
imprecations, Grimes stood up to toast his back,
by that means obtaining some sort of equal distri-
bution of heat over his whole body from the huge
log fire. Opposite sat his elder daughter, Susy,
gazing vacantly into the flames. Mrs. Grimes
was at the table, engaged in her nightly task of
mending.
-The dogs barked and the door opened,
admitting Whelan and a wild- gust of winter
“’Elio!” said Whelan.
‘Dang it, man !” snapped Grimes, “ain’t yer
got sense ’nough t’ shut the door, lettin’ in the
dashed cold? Come in if y’r comin,’ and’ don’
stan’ there grinnin’ like a hyener.”
The “hyener” shut the door leisurely, and
took a seat by the fire without removing his hat.
‘Well, Susy, how goes it?” greeted Whelan.
“Min’ y’r bizness ! ” she flung at him without
raising her eyes.
Whelan grinned, scraped out his pipe, and
began cutting a fill of rank tobacco, local growth.
Dashed cold,” he observed generally.
There was no immediate response for quite two
minutes. Then Grimes complained.
“ The big leggoon wus fruz hard this mornin’.
Never seen the likes afore.”
Whelan crammed his pipe and said, “Me
mistache wus full o’ hicicles runnin’ after the
cows this mornin’ down the paddick.”
“Snakes !” dubiously, from Susy.
Whelan took a firestick and lighted up, jabbing
the hot end on Susy’s hand before flinging it back
on the fire.
Stop it, yo’ pig ! ” shouted the girl, giving him
a smack in the face. The parents chuckled. Susy
went to bed. J
After half-an-hour of much smoke and silence
went to his bed without ceremony.
Whelan sat nursing his leg awhile and then walked
out, throwing back over his shoulder “S’ long !”
» * « *
This was a sample of Whelan’s courtship. It
was not in Mrs. Grimes’s nature to let things
shape their own course. She told Susy one
ev ® nir |£> it was time she got “ hitched-up.”
. Y !< no w y’ aint much t’ look at, me gel, an’
taint likely, by no means, as chaps is cornin’
tumbhn over one ’nother t’ marry yo’. Whelan’d
have yo’ t’ morro’if vo’ give him ’alf a chanst,
on’y y’r that dash pertickler.”
“That cove!” exclaimed Susy, with infinite
scorn.
Grimes set her iron down with a bang
on the empty jam-tin which served as a stand,
ana, putting her arms akimbo, asked angrily,
“What’s wrong with the man, I’d like to
know?”
He’s ugly ’s a jew lizard,” answered Susy
with vigor.
Sakes alive! Ugly, is it? Well, me gel, y’r
no Vanus y’rself, an’ nayther’s Whelan, so y’r
well sootid. Like ’nough, it’s the Prince o’
W hales yr waitin’for, or a dook. Let me tell
yo , then, me fine lady, it’s Whelan y’ll marry, an’
the sooner the better. ’
Q Susy, like many a better girl, gave-in.
ohe didn t care much. Her mother’s philosophy,
applied frequently, was like hob fomentations to a
congested organ.
“ Some gets the man they wants, and then they
don’t want him when they do, an’ wishes they
hadn’t, an’ some gets them they don’t want, an’
where’s the diff’rence ? It’s all wan which way it
goes.”
The wedding was to take place in December at
the District Registrar’s office in Quart Pot. This,
on economic grounds, was Whelan’s decision.
“ I aint goin’t’ be hitched up be no pars'n—not
me. Y’ see, it’s like this ’ere. Y’ go t’ a rejister
an’ e’ hitches y’ up straight ’n end. If y’ go t’ a
pars’n ’e gives yo’ a sermon long ’s me arm an’
prays over yo’ like a bloomin’ corpse, an’ then ’e
sez ‘Five quid ! ’ The rejister’s on’y ten bob an’
no prayin. ’”
* * * *
The eventful day arrived. It was a ten-mile
drive to Quart Pot, so Grimes and Whelan got
the dray ready before the sun rose. Susy in-
spected Whelan before starting.
“Y’ aint got no collar on, as I told you,” she
said.
“Collar be blowed!” he replied. “In yo’
get ! ”
She went back to the house and returned with a
small parcel.
“I’m fetchin’ wan o’ father’s. Ye’ll put it on
when we get to Quart Pot,” she told the bride-
groom, emphatically.
Whelan and Grimes occupied the front seat of
the dray ; the women sat in the body of the
conveyance on boxes. It was a hot, tedious
drive, and spiritless. It had the melancholy air
of a mouraing party. Susy put the collar on
Whelan, but as it was over an inch too large for
his neck, she took up a pleat in it with a pin.
Whelan swarmed into the Registrar’s office with
his party.
“We want t’ be ’itched up, mister,” he
announced, with the swagger of a bandit, and
sat down.
“Take off your hat, sir,” ordered the official
sternly.
Whelan dropped his hat on the floor promptly ;
Grimes sneaked his off and hung on to it. This
kind of reception was a shock to Whelan, and he
nervously spread his hands over his knees, his
fingers wide apart. After some necessary pre-
liminary explanation on both sides, the Registrar
arranged his papers, and asked the bride if she
objected to an oath.
“Not her, mister,” Whelan chipped in, grin-
ning ; “she kin perform a bit ’erself when ’er
dander’s riz.”
The Registrar frowned Whelan down, and ex-
plained what he meant.
“I presume neither of you is a minor?” he
asked.
“No, mister,” answered Whelan; “I’ve done
a bit o’ prospectin’, but no reg’lar diggin’.”
“ Tut ! tut! —are you both above the age of 21
years ? ”
They were. Mrs. Grimes vouched for Susy.
The oath was taken and the paper signed,
Whelan laboriously painting his name in Roman
characters, Susy splotching a dog-leg as her mark.
Whelan, thinking that finished the business,
plunged his right hand into a pocket of his trou-
sers, his left hand holding up the pocket on the
outside, his right knee drawn upwards in the
operation of extracting coin.
“What’s the damage, mister?” he asked, as he
grabbed for the money.
“Oh ! that comes later,” said the Registrar;
“ we have not finished yet.”
Then he addressed Susy :
“ Your full name, please ? ”
“ Miss Grimes.”
“Ah ! quite so ; but I mean your Christian
name.”
“ Susan she is,” said Grimes.
“Susan she isn’t,” snapped the mother. “Susan
Bridget she is.” And to Grimes she said, “Shut
y’r jaw, yo’ fool.”
“ Dear me! ” pleaded the Registrar; “ which
am I to put down ?”
“Chuck in the bloomin’ lot, mister,” advised
the bridegroom.
The Registrar told Whelan, in great swelling
words, that he objected to any chuckings or
bloomings in his presence.
Miss Grimes corroborated her mother.
“Full name, Mr. Whelan?”
“ James Sydney. ”
“What is your condition ?”
“ Hard’s nails,” said Whelan.
“I’m nicely, thank you,” answered Susy, much
gratified by this kindly interest in her health.
“We’re all a ’elthy family, thanks be !” sup-
plemented Mrs. Grimes.
The Great Presence smiled, and explained what
the official mind meant by “ condition.” Whelan
owned up to “ bachelor,” but Susy was not con-
fessing to anything vague. She was asked was
she a spinster.
“A what?” she asked, suspiciously. Bush
girls are very touchy about their good names.
“ Are you a single woman ? ”
“Nobody never dar’ say no diff’rent. They
jus’ couldn’t! ” emphatically.
“Nayther they couldn’t, me gel. She’s never
done no wrong, mister, ” enlarged the mother.
The Registrar made a mollifying explanation.
“ Birthplace ? ” to Whelan.
“ Onder a dray.”
“ Where ?”
“ Can’t say. That’s all I’ve heard.”
“ Occupation?”
“ Cocky.”
“What on earth do yon mean, sir? That’s no
occupation. Answer my question ! ”
The Great Presence was red in the face, and
Whelan was none too amiable by this.
“Why, any goat knows what a cocky is.
Selector,” he said, sulkily.
‘ Father’s name?”
“ Ain’t got none. Never knowed none.”
“Mother”
“ Never ’ad wan as I knows on.”
“Dear me! What a freak of nature ! Most
unusual.”
Whelan broke loose.
“ Blowed ’f I ever ’eard sich a lot o’ tommyrot.”
The Registrar thundered : “ I beg your pardon.”
“Granted, mister,” said Whelan, extending his
hand generously. The Registrar ignored the paw.
All things come to him who waits, and finally
Susan Bridget became Whelan’s yoke-mate. The
Registrar handed Mrs. Whelan her certificate,
with a liitle advice.
“This is your Warrant of Possession. If he
runs away from his Paradise, his good angel can
bring him back on this legal authority.”
“My troubles ! ” said Susy coldly. “He kin go
soon’s ’e likes. ”
But Mrs. Grimes had no such loose views.
“Kin ’e, jest? Not while I’m alive. Jest leave
’im try them games on wan o’ mine.”
The party left in rather an electric atmosphere.
A month later an unexpected Susy darkened
her mother’s door.
“ Y’ see I’ve kim back,” she announced.
Sakes alive, gal ! yo’ don’t tell me as ’ow ’e’s
been an’ gone an’ left yo’?” questioned the
mother.
Susy nodded pleasantly.
“ ’E’s gone f’r a week fencin’.”
“Yo’ quite skeered me. Well, an’ ’ow d’yo*
like it, Susy ? ”
“’Taint too bad, on’y Jim’s got’nawful lob o’
jaw, more’n I kin stan’, times.”
* * * *
Up on Rocky Creek Jim and a mate were
fencing. Jim adzed the end of a rail and stopped
to spit on his hands. His mate was ramming a
post.
“This’ere marryin’,” said Whelan, “ain’t the
bloomin’ game it’s cracked-up. Them there
weemin’s got too much jaw.”
EXILE.


Natal Witness on the Chinese labor
question in South Africa :
The stars in their courses are fighting for the mine
magnates, and everything is going swimmingly for
Chinese labor. The public, who have been skilfully
educated up to the necessity, are daily becoming more
eager to hurry on the season of prosperity which they
are now convinced Chinese labor alone can bring. The
people of Delagoa are protesting loudly against the
Transvaal mines being supplied with East Coast natives
at the expense of that port. In Johannesburg the mer-
chants have joined hands with the mine magnates, and
sent in deputations to the Acting High Commissioner
asking him to stop all railway construction, so that
natives who want work shall have no choice but to go to
the mines. Recently (he big houses condescended to
give the share market a little fillip, just as a sort of
earnest of whit maybe looked for in ihe sweet bv-and-
bye when the pigtailed hordes shall have been dumped
here. The while, rumors are industriously passed from
moil'll to mouth of some remarkab e development that
will start the boom without delay.

Serious Chances
Ake Taken in Neglecting a Simple Case
of Piles.
Any person takes serious chances in ne-
glecting an attack of piles. _ This ailment has a
tendency to become chronic, and there is also
a further tendency to ulceration and forming
of fistula, which we know are most difficult to
cure.
The safest remedy for any form of piles,
whether itching, or protruding, is Doan’s
Ointment. In using it there is no detention
from daily occupation, and the many cures
made by it have made it famous in every
corner of the world. Any chemist will tell
you that it enjoys a greater demand and more

When Watts Went Out to
Yugilbar.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The summer days, through heat and haze,
Had browned the meadow lands,
And in its flow sang soft and low
The river on its sands —
The river wide that once in pride
Poured out its high commands.
Now Watts and I to wet our dry,
Dull clay at “Mother’s” bar,
’Neath mid-day sun betimes had gone,
In tropic noons afar,
And oft we’d met that clay to wet
Beneath the evening star.
Sad exiles both with equal loath
Of rustio “ toff ” and clown,
“ The chaps in banks,” the local cranks,
The milch cows—red and brown —
The same old trees, the same old “ teas,”
Which gossip-fed the town.
And so we went, on peace intent,
That golden Eastertide,
With ample store of providore,
The liquid stock beside;
And, trotting free, ’twas good to see
The pack-horse in his stride.
God gave us hills to cure our ills ;
And where the river rolls
His splendid length of mountain strength
Along the pebbled shoals,
Long leagues from town, we sat us down
To rest our weary souls.
The dweller in the streets, whose thin,
White ghosts of pleasures pall—
He little deems what halcyon dreams,
What visions fair and tall,
From leaf and vine with songs divine,
In Bushland shadows call.
The spoil hard won by rod and gun,
Abroad in Nature’s ways;
The kind surcease from toil, and peace
Beside the camp fire’s blaze ;
The Dawn’s young rose, the Evening’s dose,
Made perfect all our days.
Then in the late, dim dusk my mate
(As Orpheus long ago)
The strings would sweep and clear and deep,
In joy—perchance in woe—
Love, Peace and War—all things that are
Fell out beneath the bow.
I’ve heard the grand massed fun’ral band
Behind a warrior’s pall,
In weird notes roll through heart and soul
The slow “ Dead March in Saul; ”
In youth’s wild days The Marseillaise
To arms all peoples call;
But when I dream by slope and stream,
Where upland glories are ;
Or, if my feet tread alien street
In alien lands afar,
Still will I hold those hours of gold
Way out on Yugilbar.
First night went by ; but, bearded, shy,
Unskilled of tongue or pen;
Next night from camp, a starlit tramp,
They came, rough miner men.
The “fiddler” knew, and slyly drew
The music of their ken.
My Bushland fair, let fools declare
Thee barren of sweet things;
Deep in thy heart there throbs apart
A harp of golden strings;
And to its chords, in wondrous words,
A wooing siren sings.
The dark range o’er, with wondrous store
Of silver from the moon
Was slowly filled, while ached and thrilled
Those lone bush hearts in tune
To Love and War —all things that are
Within the player’s boon.
Of “Home, Sweet Home” ’neath star-spread dome,
The dark hills heard the story;
“St. Patrick’s Day” once more away
The wild Celt called to glory;
Or sweet and sad the raw Scots lad
Dreamed of his “ Annie Laurie.”
Brave, simple souls; when backward rolls
Life’s curtain, from afar
That scene I’ll dream by hill and stream
’Neath cloudless moon and star ;
In fancy hear the echoes clear—
Way out on Yugilbar.
And, Watts, when you are passing through
That Vale the preachers tell.
Just lift your bow and he will know
The song-lord Israfel.
And loud and clear, “ Musicians here,”
He’ll cry—and ’twill be well.
N.S.w.
E. J. BRADY.
Now November’s There.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Oh, to be in England
Now that April’s there,"
sang Browning.
But—
“ Oh, to be in Melbourne
Now November’s there,"
sings my heart.
Melbourne in November ! Melbourne in Cup
week 1 Stand at “Puppy-dog’s” corner and look
down Collins-street—a surging sea of panamas,
a galaxy of beauty and rustling summer girls;
somewhere kiddies, everywhere flowers. Lilies-
of-the-valley, oarnations, Sydney flannel-flowers,
roses. “Roses, roses all the way”: bronze-pink,
gold-yellow, luscious cream, dark red velvet
“ Beaucaire ” rosea ! Hawkers with carts —straw-
berries in open wooden boxes, asparagus on
beds of olive grass, cool cucumbers, flaring per-
fumed oranges, every kind and variety of
hawker; Melbourne sun, Melbourne blue, Mel-
bourne sights, Melbourne sounds, Melbourne
dust, Melbourne smells—oh, I am Melbourne-
sick ! What are England and April to Mel-
bourne and November? Who pines for the
ohaffinch while Sweet Nell sings at the Princess’s
—and the “elm-tree bole ” and “the brushwood
Bheaf ?” Come with me to the Fitzroy Gardens
and —you will see what you shall see. The
lights and shades of those Fitzroy Gardens ! The
sheeny, rippling grass, the elm trees whis-
pering overhead, the willow with its long,
green sweep, the white Greek figures
flashing against the living foliage, the curved
black tree-boles in their emerald setting. And
theminahs, and the sparrows, the speckled golden-
throated thrushes, the wood-pigeon that cooes a
soft love-note for the old and sad, as well as the
young and happy who haunt the Gardens.
Birds in the sky, birds in the nest, birds at the
water’s brink, birds on the grass. Chirrup, and
flutter, and “tweet!” A laugh, a cry, a long
low call —the blackbird whistling to its mate1 —•
chirrup, and flutter, and “tweet ! ” . . .
lam Melbourne-sick ! I long to prowl round
the echoing picture-gallery, and stare at the pic-
tures—a.nd wonder at the Chinamen ! To wander
into Mullen’s and handle the last magazine,
and latest book, to buy frills and chiffons at
Buckley’s, to oat strawberries under the wistaria
in the Winter Garden, to waste someone’s sub-
stance on crystallised fruit at Bates’, to hear the
tram bells clang as the traffic thickens, and the
hansoms dodge the water-carts. / want
I want to walk along Swanston-street side by
side with a sun-tanned jackeroo who has a cheque
for the “blue-ing,” to go to the Cup and see the
horses, and more panamas, and rustling, gaily-
colored summer-girls. To hear the bookmakers
yell, and the great crowd hum like a swarm of
hiving bees. And I want to drift home by moon-
light, to sit with that jackeroo on the river bank,
and watch the stars open shyly, one by one, like
flowers in the deep blue sky. To see those other
stars come out, the twinkling jewelled lights, the
wicked' shifting lights—the lights o’ Melbourne
town.
And I want that jackeroo to murmur softly in
my ear of the loneliness of waiting, of the dreams
of the night shifts, of the Love that passeth under-
standing. And —God pity me !
I sit up in the dark, and rub my eyes, and
stretch wide my empty arms and— listen 1
Nothing—nothing—but darkness—darkness and
silence !
Then the sleet on the window, and the low
breathing of my English husband—and its —
“ Oh, to be in Melbourne
Now Novembers there
THE YOUNG 'UN.
Tales of the Penance Track.
No. 27’s ESCAPE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
No. 27 was a “ sil vert ail.” He had been, before
his fall, a Clerk of Petty Sessions, magnate of a
country town, supping with the magistrate, bank
manager and parson. His cankering trouble was
the thought of his correspondence. The deficit in
his cash was mainly due to the spending capacities
of another woman, and his wife learned all at the
trial. No. 27 had never been able to explain,
nor to ask forgiveness. She wrote every six weeks,
no reproaches, no grumbling, but a strong, cheer-
ful, loving letter, mapping out a future bright
with happiness for both. And 27 swore long and
silently—at himself.
He had five months still to do, and it dragged;
days seemed years, and long, dreamy nights
seemed ages, with the glimpse of the good future
showing in fitful snatches of slumber.
* * * *
It was four months and seven days from the
date of his discharge, when the Deputy-Governor
called 27 into his office and told him. No. 27
came out stupid. He would never see her, never
ask her forgiveness, never have a chance to tell
her all—how that other had forced herself into his
heart, with her fierce, wild ways and caresses, and
blinded him. And now she would die without
him telling her. He was allowed to write a letter
of farewell. How could he explain that in a letter ?
* * * *
Warder Fallon, on night duty from 11 p.m.,
detected a dark form swinging from a blanket-
rope from cell 39. Warder Fallon crouched under-
neath in the shadow of the wall, and when 27
reached the ground he was promptly secured and
placed in lock-up cells till the visiting Justice
arrived.
No. 27 told him all —how she was dying ; how
he wanted one word of forgiveness from her, and
then gaol if they liked for the rest of his life. But
27 was taken back to do seven days' dark cells.
* * * *
On the fourth day the doctor and deputy-
governor went to the “ dark cell ’ to break the
news of his wife’s death to No. 27, but they found
him swinging lifeless from the window-bars. The
doctor said he had been dead three hours. The
deputy-governor looked at the telegram in his
hand. It read: “Your wife died at nine this
morning.” Then he looked at his watch—it was
just 12 o’clock.
No. 27 had scratched a message on his drinking
tin, bidding them give his watch to his mother.
“ To his mother?” said the doctor, walking out
of the cell; “ I wonder did he know.”
