“ J.A.C.” : The Faust Family, possibly the cleverest
all-round combination which has visited Australasia, is
earning unlimited kudos, under, the management of Ted
E. Faust, in the Land of 'the Burnt Nigger. Their
“ Risley ” act, so called from its originator—an acrobatic
performance in which the feet are used instead of the
hands—is regarded as ahead of anything in the line
hitherto seen. Miss Daisy Faust has been very successful
with a musical comedy eo. in “ The Show Girl.” Faust,
seftr., retired from the “ profesh,” is residing in Sydney.
I first knew him in Italy, 30 years ago, when he WM
without a rival as an acrobat.
Charles Tait writes from London that when he looked
in at the Pavilion he found Frank Lincoln giving just the
same “ turns ” that he gave in Australia more than a
dozen years ago. The average stage mummer, despite
all that can be said against him, is a hard, intellectual
grafter compared with a successful monologue showman.
An actor has to give his mind to a new character pretty
frequently, and every new character, if it’s a big one,
demands a good deal of work at first, and adds to the-,
store of words he carries in his memory. But the mon
loguer, who gets going with an entertainment when he’s
25, and keeps it going until he’s 60, without alteration—
what does he do with his brains in the meantime ? It is
as though he had painted a popular picture in his ybuth
and lived for ever afterwards on the proceeds of exhibiting
it at a shilling a head.
“ D.” : The Dickens boom will probably strike us again
shortly. In the ’60’s and ’70’s Dickens’ dramas were
popular enough. Since then, the most successful per-
formances have been Fred Marshall’s Quilp (Maggie Moore
doubled Little Nell and the Marchioness, in another ver-
sion of “The Old Curiosity Shop”), Jennie Lee’s Jo,
Toole’s Caleb Plummer and Artful Dodger, and Tyrone
Power’s Sydney Carton. Sass and Emery produced
“ Little Ejh’ly ” during their brief partnership, Sass play-
ing Peggotty, the part which fell to Cartwright in the
recent London production of another variation of “ David
Copperfield.” Dan Barry and Nance O’Neil produced
different versions .of “ Oliver Twist.’’ It is hard to say
which was the more gory.
Coquelin, possibly the finest actor on the stage at the
present day, recently completed his 63rd year. He was
born at Boulogne, France, where his father was a baker.
The paternal Coquelin wished his son to follow the busf*
ness, but the boy preferred another roll —that is r6le.
He entered the Paris Conservatoire, and subsequently
joined the famous company of the Theatre Framjais.
Since then, he has played pretty well all over the world,
and in America, in particular, has “ scooped ” untold
dollars.
“ Haresfoot ”: Thanks for the portrait of Jack Steele.
The last I heard of him he was reciting at the Melbourne
waxworks. Perhaps he was never associated with a more
curious manager than Gilbert Roberts. This extra-
ordinary little fellow was junior reporter on the Age
when he astounded the community by becoming lessee
of Coppin’s Hay market theatre, with Mr, and Mrs. J. B.
Steele leading the company. Roberts had no money,
but the friendship of the all-potent Walter Montgomery
must have found him backers. He re-christened the
theatre the Duke of Edinburgh, after another potentate
then knocking about. Montgomery appeared there.
Mr. and Mrs. Steele acted in “ Black Sheep,” “Henry
Dunbar,” and Robertson’s “Ours.” Roberts was a fore-
runner of Augustus Harris—without the luck.
The Pollards have lost still another of their “lillipu-
tians”in S’Africa; Annie Curtice, one of the original mem-
bers of the co,, was married on the last day of the
Johannesburg season to a son of Edward Richardson,
who \yas Vogel’s Minister for Public Works in M.L.’s bad
old days.
A variety artist appearing at the 'alls in England is
billed as Frank Mummery.
Of Haddon Chambers’s new play a London critic says :
“It seems really incredible that the author of ‘The
Tyranny of Tears’ should have written ‘The Golden
Silence.’” It seems not less incredible that the author
of various other plays by Haddon Chambers should have
written “The Tyranny of Tears,” which is equal to the
brightest of Pinero’s or Wilde’s work.
Of course it was Bland Holt, and not his father, who
is remembered as a first-class pantomime clown. The
evidence of Bland’s former connection with “property"
legs of mutton and strings of sandwiches can be detected
in his habit of blinking his eyes as he stands facing the
audience. This is the time-honored peculiarity of the
clown who is being charged with petty larcenj - by a pan-
tomime policeman or nurse-girl.
“Hamlet": “ It is a pity,” said Fisher to me quite seri-
ously, “ that so many of us provincial actors don’t pay our
way honestly. We go to a country town, fleece almost every-
one in a mild sort of way, and then clear out. Another
struggling crowd of mummers comes along—perhaps
they’re an honest crowd and perhaps they’re not—and
get a terrible reception, and are regarded with suspicion
by everyone from the Mayor to the town’s unregistered
mongrel. We, the Barrington Gobung Cos., struck a town
once that had, a few weeks before, been invaded by a
band of unprincipled barn-stormers, and we were refused
accommodation anywhere until we planked down our
money in advance; and even then the landlords told
Barrington that they relied upon him to see that none
of the mummers sneaked any of their silver spoons. The
local bill-sticker refused to work' until he was paid. The
season was a failure and the crownißg incident happened
as we struggled to the railway station with our props.
At the side of a shanty on the read a woman stood
washing. The clothes were hanging on the fence along-
side the road. Avery small urchin saw us coming, and
he rushed up te the woman and shouted: ‘Muvver,
muvver, take in the clothes. Here comes the actors.’ ”
“ D.”: American dramatists struggle hard to find
extraordinary titles for their plays. Some recent ones
are “A Hoosier Daisy,” “A Devil’s Lane,” “ Fogg’s
Ferry,” “ A Devil in Skirts,” and “An Eye on Hubby.”
Australia, 50 years ago, used to do something in the same
line. Among the comedies running at the old Victoria
(Sydney) during 1853 were—" The Woman I Adore,” “ Go
to Bed, Tom,” “Sink or Swim,” “A Phenomenon in a
Smock Frock,” “ The Married Bake,” and “ To Paris and
Back for Five Pounds." The company included Rogers,
Welch, and Sara Flower.
Planche related that he was behind the scenes, at the
London Lyceum, while Madame Vestris stood on a
pedestal as Apollo, waiting for the raising of the front
cloth. “Heavens!” thought Planche, “ what a piece of
impudence to put up that shivering and cowering old
woman ! ” But the stage is an elixir ! When the curtain
went up she was a radiant image of beauty. Fred Belton
used to tell, in Australia, how he managed an Edinburgh
theatre, when the principal girl for a Christmas panto-
mime was sent from London on the very day of the pro-
duction. “ Macbeth’s Third Witch ! ” was his sorrowful
exclamation as she mumbled through the only rehearsal.
At night she was an image of life, laughter and joy !
Geo. Vandenhoff relates how his heart sank when he
rehearsed the “ Hunchback ” with ugly Charlotte Cush-
man, and could not believe his eyes when she appeared, a
perfect Venus, at night.
A London critic says of veteran actor Willard (the
original “Spider” in “Silver King”) that, during his
sojourn in the States, he has “acquired the habit of
exaggeration in manner and in gesture which is the
stamp of the American actor/’ The American actor, as
Australia sees him of late years, is mostly a person who
stands stock still in the middle of an emotional scene and
aims at impressing the audience with his deadly calmness.
He doesn’t portray the natural effects of mind upon
matter, much less exaggerate them.
The French have discovered that the cake walk is of
French origin. It is.said to be a negro imitation of the
stately minuet introduced into New Orleans by refugees
from the court of Louis XVI. at the time of the first
revolution.

for several years. He is a good naan for anybody who
wants just that kind of man.”
“ Alta” : Entered the d.c. of Sydney Majesty’s Theatre,
t’other night, and seeing an immense audience, mostly
composed of white satin and bare flesh, I concluded that
“ Sassietv ” was present in overwhelming numbers. But
later on, after the hero and his country girl had decided
to “ live happy ever after,” the babble in the ladies’ cloak-
room was mostly about how these silken dames would
have to “ bust ” themselves to “ ketch ” their respective
boats and trams.
A returned actor tells that during the recent “ scrap-
ping’’season in S’Africa a second-rate company of barn-
stormers struck Bloemfontein with a one-night-only
outrage on the “ Silver King.” A mob of very tired
Tommies had just entered the town after a long and
sleepless march, and they swarmed into the hall as soon
as the doors were open. There they all immediately fell
asleep. The play began, and the entire audience still
remained in “ by-bye,” and never a soul moved an eyelid
till the crack of a firearm announced the mock murder of
Geoffrey Ware. Then the audience sprang to attention
with such violence that the company fled, and didn’t
return, the Spider remaining all night in an adjacent
brick oven, where he had taken cover. No injury was
inflicted, however, except by the players to the play—and
that was done while the audience wasn’t looking.
